Talaq — Divorce
advanced
10 min
The Bardha'iyyah Case: Three Conditional Divorces on a Non-Consummated Wife
Discusses the Bardha'iyyah case of three conditional divorces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Bardha'iyyah case, apply the Hanafi majority and Zufar's positions, and derive the number of divorces that occur.

Core Idea
In three conditional divorces on a non-consummated wife, only one divorce takes effect; the oaths dissolve without further effect.

The Bardha'iyyah case is a famous Hanafi legal problem named after the scholar Abu Sa'id al-Bardha'i, who traveled to Baghdad for seven years to find its answer. The case involves a man who says to his non-consummated wife three times: 'If I speak to you, you are divorced.' We will analyze how the Hanafi majority and the dissenting scholar Zufar resolve the oaths.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
According to the majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), the three oaths are considered one conditional unit. Upon speaking, the first oath triggers and divorces her; the second and third oaths dissolve without effect because she is no longer a wife.
The key is that the entire statement is treated as one sentence.
2
Identify Zufar's objection
Zufar holds that the second oath dissolves the first because the condition (speaking) is met, but the second oath is void because it encounters a separated woman. Only one divorce occurs, but via a different reasoning.
Zufar's view is rejected by the majority.
3
Trace the Hanafi dalil
The dalil is that the conditional sentence is one complete utterance; the result (divorce) occurs upon the condition, but since the wife is divorced after the first, subsequent oaths have no subject.
This is based on the principle of 'one subject, one result'.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Both views result in one divorce, but the reasoning differs. The practical difference appears in cases with consummated wives or other variables.
The majority view is mufta bihi.

الجملة واحدة وإلا نزل اثنان على المدخولة بتكرير كلما كلمتك فأنت طالق وانحلت بالثانية لا إلى جراء ولغت هي بعدم الملك، وفي إن حلفت بطلاقك لا تنحل اليمين الثانية إلا بتعليق طلاقها بالملك أو بعده إذ الشرط إدخالها في الجزاء. كذا في تعليق طلاقها ومدخولة بالحلف بطلاقهما إنما تنحل الثانية بتعليق طلاقها بالملك أو بعده إذ الثالثة انعقدت على المدخولة فكانت الثالثة شطر الشرط وذا في حق الثالثة شطر أيضاً فلا تنحل ما لم يحلف بطلاق المدخولة وهي البردعية ا.هـ. يعني أن هذه المسألة تلقب بالبردعية لأن أبا سعيد البردعي بعدما تفقه ودرس سئل عنها فلم يهتد إلى جوابها فارتحل إلى بغداد وتعلم سبع سنين حتى صار من كبار أصحابنا . وقيد بغير المدخولة لأن فيها يتعلق الكل بالشرط قدمه أو حسب

الكل كلاماً واحداً بخلاف ما لو لم يذكره بالفاء لكن انحلال اليمين الأولى في مسألتنا عند زفر بشرط الثانية وهو قوله إن كلمتك» لأن شرط الحنث مطلق الكلام وقد وجد فصار كما لو اقتصر عليه، ولم يتلفظ بالجزاء فيصادفها الجزاء وهي مبانة لا إلى عدة فلا تنعقد عليها اليمين الثانية وعند الثلاثة بالجزاء فانعقدت الثانية لأن الجملة الشرطية واحدة والمتعارف الكلام المفيد بخلاف ما لو اقتصر على الشرط لأن الكلام يكون تاماً وناقصاً، فإن اقتصر على الناقص علم أنه المراد، وإن جاوزه إلى التام على أنه المراد. وعلى اختيار ابن الفضل لا يحنث لو اقتصر وبه يندفع استشهاد زفر، ولأن الجملة لو لم تكن واحدة لنزل طلقتان على المدخولة بتكرير كلما طلقتك فأنت طالق لأن قوله ثانياً كلما طلقتك مخاطبة لها وكذلك فأنت طالق خطاب ثانٍ فإذا ثبت انعقاد اليمين الثانية انحلت . بوجود الثالثة لا إلى جزاء لأن الجزاء يصادفها وهي مبانة فتلغوا الثالثة لعدم الملك . وقال أبو مطيع وجماعة من مشايخ بلخ: لا ينحل منها شيء إلا بكلام مبتدأ وإليه سبق وهم أبي حنيفة حين سأله محمد في صغره عمن قال ثلاثاً والله لا أكلمك وقال يا شيخ انظر حسناً فقال : حنث مرتين فقال محمد: أحسنت. وقوله وفي إن حلفت «الخ أي وفيما لو قال ثلاث مرات لغير المدخولة إن حلفت بطلاقك فأنت طالق لا تنحل اليمين الثانية إلا بتعليق طلاقها بالملك بأن يقول إن تزوجتك فأنت طالق أو يتعلق بعد ملك النكاح بأن يتزوجها ويقول إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق لأن شرط الانحلال هنا هو الحلف بطلاقها وذلك بإدخالها في جزاء اليمين الثالثة وهو الطلاق، ولا يصح إدخالها فيه لعدم الملك عند وجودها بخلاف الأولى لأن الشرط وهو الكلام يتصور في غير الملك، وكذا الحكم في تعليق الرجل طلاق امرأتيه المدخول بها وغير المدخول بها بالحلف بطلاقيكما بأن قال لهما ثلاثاً إن حلفت بطلاقيكما فأنتما طالقان إنما تنحل الثانية في حقهما بتعليق طلاق غير المدخولة بالملك أو بعده بشرط آخر كما مر لأن اليمين الثالثة التي هي شرط انحلال الثانية إنما انعقد على المدخولة خاصة لأن الشرط في انحلال الثانية الحلف بطلاقهما وذلك بإدخالهما في جزاء الثالثة وهو الطلاق، ولم يمكن إدخال غير المدخولة فيه لعدم الملك كما فكانت الثالثة في حق انحلال الثانية شطر الشرط لا كله فلا يؤثر في انحلال شيء فإذا علق بعده طلاق غير المدخولة بالملك أو بعده بشرط آخر كمل الشرط فتطلق كل
And the author indicated that if he said to a non-consummated woman: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, and you are like my mother's back, and by Allah I will not approach you,' and she entered, she is divorced, and the zihar and ila' are dropped according to him [Abu Hanifa] because the divorce precedes, so she becomes separated and is no longer a place for what follows. According to them [the Sahibayn], he is a man who has made zihar and ila'. And that if he said to a foreign woman: 'If I marry you, you are divorced, and you are like my mother's back, and by Allah I will not approach you,' and he marries her, then it is according to the difference. Unlike if he put zihar and ila' first, then everything occurs according to everyone. As for according to them, it is apparent, and as for according to him, because ila' precedes. Then she is after that a place for zihar, then she is after both a place for divorce, so she is divorced. So in Fath al-Qadir. And that if he said to a woman 'On the day I marry you, you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced,' and he marries her, one divorce occurs and the two become void. If he said 'You are divorced, and divorced, and divorced on the day I marry you,' three divorces occur. So in al-Hawi al-Qudsi. Similarly if he said 'If I marry you' as in al-Muhit. And in Talkhis al-Jami' from the beginning of Kitab al-Ayman: if he said three times to a non-consummated woman 'If I speak to you, you are divorced,' the first is dissolved by the second because of restarting speech, unlike 'Go, O enemy of Allah.' But according to Zufar, by the condition as if he confined himself, so the second is void. According to us [Hanafis], by the result, so it is concluded, except if the ta'liq is by his saying 'You are divorced, then divorced if I marry you, then divorced,' then at that time the first becomes effective immediately, the second is suspended, and the third is void because by his saying 'You are divorced' the divorce occurred, and by his saying 'then divorced if I marry you' she divorces upon the marriage that is the condition of the divorce, and the third is void because of lack of connection to the marriage. So reflect and look at his saying 'The summary is that the letters are three...' end. This objection is based on a defective copy he had, which says: and if he connects with thumma and delays the condition, the second is suspended and the first becomes effective, so the suspended occurs at the condition after the second marriage, and the third is void. In the case of a consummated woman, the first is suspended and what follows becomes effective. According to what is in most copies, there is no objection; rather it agrees with what is in al-Fath, al-Tabyin, al-Nahr, and others. His saying: (And it is restricted to a conjunction, etc.) In Ayman al-Bazzaziyya from the third chapter on oaths of divorce: if he says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, divorced, divorced' and she is not touched, then the first is suspended by the condition, the second descends immediately, and the third is void. If he marries her and she enters the house, the suspended descends. If she enters after separation before marriage, the oath is dissolved without a result. If she is a consummated wife, the first is suspended and the second and third descend immediately. This, as you see, contradicts what he transmitted here from al-Fath, unless there is a difference between 'one, one, one' and 'divorced, divorced, divorced', which is apparent. His saying: (Unlike 'Go, O enemy of Allah' because mentioning it with the conjunction fa requires its connection to what preceded, so the sentence becomes one. Otherwise, two would descend upon a consummated woman if she repeats 'every time I speak to you, you are divorced,' and the second is dissolved without a result, and it becomes void due to lack of ownership. And in the case of 'If I swear by your divorce,' the second oath does not dissolve except by suspending her divorce upon ownership or after it, because the condition is her entry into the result. Similarly, in the suspension of her divorce, and a consummated woman by swearing by their divorce, the second dissolves only by suspending her divorce upon ownership or after it, because the third oath is concluded upon the consummated woman, so the third is a part of the condition, and this is also a part in the right of the third, so it does not dissolve until he swears by the divorce of the consummated woman. This is the Bardha'iyyah case, end. Meaning this case is called al-Bardha'iyyah because Abu Sa'id al-Bardha'i, after he became a jurist and taught, was asked about it and could not find its answer, so he traveled to Baghdad and studied for seven years until he became one of our great companions. And it is restricted to a non-consummated woman because in her case everything is suspended by the condition, whether he puts it first or last. The whole sentence is one, unlike if he did not mention it with fa. But the dissolution of the first oath in our case according to Zufar is by the condition of the second, which is his saying 'If I speak to you,' because the condition of the breach is absolute speech, and it has occurred, so it becomes as if he confined himself to it, and he did not utter the result, so the result encounters her while she is separated without a waiting period, so the second oath does not conclude upon her. According to the three [Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad], it is by the result, so the second is concluded because the conditional sentence is one, and the common usage is complete speech, unlike if he confined himself to the condition, because the speech is both complete and incomplete. If he confines himself to the incomplete, it is known that it is intended; if he goes beyond to the complete, it is intended. According to the choice of Ibn al-Fadl, he does not breach if he confines himself, and this refutes Zufar's argument. And because if the sentence were not one, then two divorces would descend upon a consummated woman by repeating 'every time I divorce you, you are divorced,' because his saying a second time 'every time I divorce you' is addressing her, and similarly 'you are divorced' is a second address. So if the conclusion of the second oath is established, it dissolves by the existence of the third without a result because the result encounters her while she is separated, so the third becomes void due to lack of ownership. Abu Muti' and a group of scholars from Balkh said: nothing dissolves from it except with a new utterance, and this was the opinion of Abu Hanifa when Muhammad asked him in his childhood about someone who said three times 'By Allah, I will not speak to you' and said 'O shaykh, look well,' so he said: he breaks twice. Muhammad said: well said. And his saying 'And in the case of 'If I swear' etc.' means: in the case of someone who said three times to a non-consummated woman 'If I swear by your divorce, you are divorced,' the second oath does not dissolve except by suspending her divorce upon ownership, such as saying 'If I marry you, you are divorced' or it is suspended after marriage by saying 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' because the condition of dissolution here is swearing by her divorce, and that is by her entry into the result of the third oath, which is divorce, and it is not valid to enter her into it due to lack of ownership at the time of its occurrence, unlike the first because the condition, which is speech, is conceivable without ownership. Similarly, the ruling in the case of a man suspending the divorce of his two wives, one consummated and one not, by swearing by their divorce, such as saying to them three times 'If I swear by your divorce, you are both divorced,' the second dissolves with respect to them only by suspending the divorce of the non-consummated woman upon ownership or after it with another condition, as passed, because the third oath, which is the condition of dissolution of the second, was concluded upon the consummated woman only, because the condition for dissolution of the second is swearing by their divorce, and that is by entering both into the result of the third, which is divorce, and it was not possible to enter the non-consummated woman into it due to lack of ownership, so the third, with respect to dissolution of the second, is part of the condition, not all of it, so it does not effect any dissolution. Then if he later suspends the divorce of the non-consummated woman upon ownership or after it with another condition, the condition is completed, so each divorces.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq covers multiple complex scenarios of conditional divorce, including the famous Bardha'iyyah case. The text begins by discussing a case where a man says to his non-consummated wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, you are like my mother's back (zihar), and by Allah I will not approach you (ila').' According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the divorce takes precedence, so the wife is divorced and becomes separated, and the zihar and ila' are nullified because she is no longer his wife. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he is considered a man who has made both zihar and ila', meaning both are still valid. This difference arises because Abu Hanifa sees that the divorce, being immediate upon fulfillment of the condition, removes her from being a wife before zihar and ila' can take effect, while the Sahibayn view the combination as simultaneous. The text then mentions that if he marries a foreign woman and says the same, the same difference applies. However, if he put zihar and ila' first, then all three occur according to all scholars. Next, the text discusses a case where a man says to a woman 'On the day I marry you, you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced' (with 'wa'). If he marries her, only one divorce occurs and the other two are void because the condition (marriage) is mentioned only with the first 'taliq', and the subsequent ones are considered repetitive without a new condition. But if he says 'You are divorced, and divorced, and divorced on the day I marry you,' then all three occur upon marriage because the condition applies to all three. The Bardha'iyyah case is then introduced: it involves a man who says to his non-consummated wife three times 'If I speak to you, you are divorced.' The question is how many divorces occur and when. The text explains that according to Zufar, the first oath is dissolved by the second because the condition (speaking) has occurred, but the second oath is void because it encounters her while already divorced. According to the majority of Hanafis (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), the second oath is concluded because the conditional sentence is considered one complete unit, and the breach occurs upon speaking, so the first divorce takes effect, and the second oath is dissolved without a result because she is already divorced. The third is similarly void. The text also mentions the opinion of Ibn al-Fadl and Abu Muti'. The story of Abu Sa'id al-Bardha'i illustrates the difficulty of this case: he was a great scholar who was stumped by this question and had to study in Baghdad for seven years to find the answer, which led to the case being named after him. The passage then discusses other scenarios involving swearing by divorce and multiple wives. Throughout, the Hanafi position is that the structure of the sentence and the presence of conjunctions determine whether multiple oaths are considered one unit or separate. The relied-upon position in the school is that of the majority (Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn) as opposed to Zufar's view.

Key Terms
البردعية (al-Bardha'iyyah): A specific case of conditional divorce named after Abu Sa'id al-Bardha'i, involving three conditional oaths on a non-consummated wife.
زفر (Zufar): Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl, a prominent Hanafi scholar who differed from the majority on some rulings of oaths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Bardha'iyyah case, what is the mufta bihi position regarding the number of divorces?
A) Three divorces.
B) Two divorces.
C) One divorce.
D) No divorce because the oaths are invalid.

Key Takeaway
In the Bardha'iyyah case, only one divorce occurs; the oaths dissolve without further effect due to the unity of the conditional sentence.

Talaq — Divorce
advanced
12 min
Complex Conditional Divorce Scenarios with Multiple Oaths and Conditions
Discusses various complex conditional divorce scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the rulings for various complex conditional divorce scenarios involving multiple oaths, conditions, and the status of the wife (consummated or non-consummated), applying the Hanafi principles of sentence unity and conjunction.

Core Idea
The structure of the sentence (conjunctions, order, and repetition) determines how many divorces occur in complex conditional scenarios.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the Bardha'iyyah case and presents additional scenarios: divorces with 'thumma' (then), oaths conditioned on marriage, and swearing by divorce involving two wives. We will apply the Hanafi framework to each scenario, focusing on how the wording affects the outcome.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the role of conjunctions
The text distinguishes between 'wa' (and) and 'thumma' (then) in conditional sentences. 'Wa' connects the oaths as one unit; 'thumma' may create separate units.
Examples: 'On the day I marry you, you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced' vs. 'You are divorced, then divorced if I marry you, then divorced'.
2
Distinguish between consummated and non-consummated wives
The rulings differ significantly. For non-consummated wives, the oaths are often treated as one unit; for consummated, multiple divorces may occur.
The Bardha'iyyah case only applies to non-consummated.
3
Apply the rule of condition and result
When a condition is attached to a result, the result occurs upon fulfillment. If multiple results are tied to the same condition, the first takes effect and subsequent ones may be void.
This is seen in the 'thumma' scenario.
4
Handle oaths conditioned on future events
Scenarios like 'If I swear by your divorce, you are divorced' require careful analysis of whether the oath can be fulfilled before marriage.
The text explains that the second oath does not dissolve except by suspending divorce upon ownership.

وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو قال لغير المدخولة إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق وأنت علي كظهر أمي والله لا أقربك فدخلت طلقت وسقط الظهار والإيلاء عنده لسبق الطلاق فتبين فلا تبقى محلاً لما بعده. وعندهما هو مطلق مظاهر مول وإلى أنه لو قال لأجنبية إن تزوجتك فأنت طالق وأنت علي كظهر أمي ووالله لا أقربك وتزوجها، فعلى الخلاف بخلاف ما لو قدم الظهار والإيلاء وقع الكل عند الكل. أما عندهما فظاهر، وأما عنده فلسبق الإيلاء. ثم هي بعده محل للظهار، ثم هي بعدهما محل للطلاق فتطلق كذا في فتح القدير. وإلى أنه لو قال لامرأة يوم أتزوجك فأنت طالق وطالق وطالق فتزوجها وقعت واحدة وبطلت الثنتان، ولو قال أنت طالق وطالق وطالق يوم أتزوجك وقعت الثلاث كذا في الحاوي القدسي. وكذا لو قال إن تزوجتك كما في المحيط وفي تلخيص الجامع من أول كتاب الأيمان : لو قال ثلاثاً لغير المدخولة إن كلمتك فأنت طالق انحلت الأولى بالثانية لاستئناف الكلام بخلاف فاذهبي يا عدوة الله، لكن عند زفر بالشرط كما لو اقتصر فلغت الثانية وعندنا بالجزاء فانعقدت إذ الكلام إلا لو كان التعليق بقوله أنت طالق ثم طالق إن تزوجتك ثم طالق فإنه حينئذ يتنجز الأول ويتعلق الثاني ويلغو الثالث لأن بقوله أنت طالق وقع الطلاق وبقوله ثم طالق إن تزوجتك تطلق بالتزوج المعلق عليه الطلاق ولغا الثالث لعدم الإضافة إلى التزوج فتأمل وانظر إلى قوله «والحاصل أن الحروف ثلاثة إلى آخره» ا هـ . وهذا الاعتراض مبني على ما وقع له من نسخة سقيمة وهي : ولو عطف بثم وأخر الشرط تعلق الثاني وتنجز الأول فيقع المعلق عند الشرط بعد التزوج الثاني ولغا الثالث، وفي المدخول بها تعلق الأول وتنجز ما عبده. وعلى ما في عامة النسخ لا اعتراض بل هو الموافق لما في الفتح والتبيين والنهر وغيرها قوله : ( وقيد بحرف العطف الخ) في إيمان البزازية من الثالث في يمين الطلاق إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق طالق طالق وهي غير ملموسة، فالأول معلق بالشرط، والثاني ينزل في الحال ويلغو الثالث، وإن تزوجها ودخل الدار نزل المعلق ولو دخل بعد البينونة قبل التزوج انحل اليمين لا إلى جزاء ولو موطوءة تعلق الأول ونزل الثاني والثالث في اهـ. وهذا كما ترى مخالف لما نقله هنا عن الفتح إلا أن يفرق بين واحدة واحدة وبين طالق طالق وهو الظاهر قوله : (بخلاف فاذهبي يا عدوة الله لأن ذكره بفاء العطف يقتضي تعلقه بما سبق فصار الحال

الجملة واحدة وإلا نزل اثنان على المدخولة بتكرير كلما كلمتك فأنت طالق وانحلت بالثانية لا إلى جراء ولغت هي بعدم الملك، وفي إن حلفت بطلاقك لا تنحل اليمين الثانية إلا بتعليق طلاقها بالملك أو بعده إذ الشرط إدخالها في الجزاء. كذا في تعليق طلاقها ومدخولة بالحلف بطلاقهما إنما تنحل الثانية بتعليق طلاقها بالملك أو بعده إذ الثالثة انعقدت على المدخولة فكانت الثالثة شطر الشرط وذا في حق الثالثة شطر أيضاً فلا تنحل ما لم يحلف بطلاق المدخولة وهي البردعية ا.هـ. يعني أن هذه المسألة تلقب بالبردعية لأن أبا سعيد البردعي بعدما تفقه ودرس سئل عنها فلم يهتد إلى جوابها فارتحل إلى بغداد وتعلم سبع سنين حتى صار من كبار أصحابنا . وقيد بغير المدخولة لأن فيها يتعلق الكل بالشرط قدمه أو حسب

الكل كلاماً واحداً بخلاف ما لو لم يذكره بالفاء لكن انحلال اليمين الأولى في مسألتنا عند زفر بشرط الثانية وهو قوله إن كلمتك» لأن شرط الحنث مطلق الكلام وقد وجد فصار كما لو اقتصر عليه، ولم يتلفظ بالجزاء فيصادفها الجزاء وهي مبانة لا إلى عدة فلا تنعقد عليها اليمين الثانية وعند الثلاثة بالجزاء فانعقدت الثانية لأن الجملة الشرطية واحدة والمتعارف الكلام المفيد بخلاف ما لو اقتصر على الشرط لأن الكلام يكون تاماً وناقصاً، فإن اقتصر على الناقص علم أنه المراد، وإن جاوزه إلى التام على أنه المراد. وعلى اختيار ابن الفضل لا يحنث لو اقتصر وبه يندفع استشهاد زفر، ولأن الجملة لو لم تكن واحدة لنزل طلقتان على المدخولة بتكرير كلما طلقتك فأنت طالق لأن قوله ثانياً كلما طلقتك مخاطبة لها وكذلك فأنت طالق خطاب ثانٍ فإذا ثبت انعقاد اليمين الثانية انحلت . بوجود الثالثة لا إلى جزاء لأن الجزاء يصادفها وهي مبانة فتلغوا الثالثة لعدم الملك . وقال أبو مطيع وجماعة من مشايخ بلخ: لا ينحل منها شيء إلا بكلام مبتدأ وإليه سبق وهم أبي حنيفة حين سأله محمد في صغره عمن قال ثلاثاً والله لا أكلمك وقال يا شيخ انظر حسناً فقال : حنث مرتين فقال محمد: أحسنت. وقوله وفي إن حلفت «الخ أي وفيما لو قال ثلاث مرات لغير المدخولة إن حلفت بطلاقك فأنت طالق لا تنحل اليمين الثانية إلا بتعليق طلاقها بالملك بأن يقول إن تزوجتك فأنت طالق أو يتعلق بعد ملك النكاح بأن يتزوجها ويقول إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق لأن شرط الانحلال هنا هو الحلف بطلاقها وذلك بإدخالها في جزاء اليمين الثالثة وهو الطلاق، ولا يصح إدخالها فيه لعدم الملك عند وجودها بخلاف الأولى لأن الشرط وهو الكلام يتصور في غير الملك، وكذا الحكم في تعليق الرجل طلاق امرأتيه المدخول بها وغير المدخول بها بالحلف بطلاقيكما بأن قال لهما ثلاثاً إن حلفت بطلاقيكما فأنتما طالقان إنما تنحل الثانية في حقهما بتعليق طلاق غير المدخولة بالملك أو بعده بشرط آخر كما مر لأن اليمين الثالثة التي هي شرط انحلال الثانية إنما انعقد على المدخولة خاصة لأن الشرط في انحلال الثانية الحلف بطلاقهما وذلك بإدخالهما في جزاء الثالثة وهو الطلاق، ولم يمكن إدخال غير المدخولة فيه لعدم الملك كما فكانت الثالثة في حق انحلال الثانية شطر الشرط لا كله فلا يؤثر في انحلال شيء فإذا علق بعده طلاق غير المدخولة بالملك أو بعده بشرط آخر كمل الشرط فتطلق كل
And the author indicated that if he said to a non-consummated woman: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, and you are like my mother's back, and by Allah I will not approach you,' and she entered, she is divorced, and the zihar and ila' are dropped according to him [Abu Hanifa] because the divorce precedes, so she becomes separated and is no longer a place for what follows. According to them [the Sahibayn], he is a man who has made zihar and ila'. And that if he said to a foreign woman: 'If I marry you, you are divorced, and you are like my mother's back, and by Allah I will not approach you,' and he marries her, then it is according to the difference. Unlike if he put zihar and ila' first, then everything occurs according to everyone. As for according to them, it is apparent, and as for according to him, because ila' precedes. Then she is after that a place for zihar, then she is after both a place for divorce, so she is divorced. So in Fath al-Qadir. And that if he said to a woman 'On the day I marry you, you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced,' and he marries her, one divorce occurs and the two become void. If he said 'You are divorced, and divorced, and divorced on the day I marry you,' three divorces occur. So in al-Hawi al-Qudsi. Similarly if he said 'If I marry you' as in al-Muhit. And in Talkhis al-Jami' from the beginning of Kitab al-Ayman: if he said three times to a non-consummated woman 'If I speak to you, you are divorced,' the first is dissolved by the second because of restarting speech, unlike 'Go, O enemy of Allah.' But according to Zufar, by the condition as if he confined himself, so the second is void. According to us [Hanafis], by the result, so it is concluded, except if the ta'liq is by his saying 'You are divorced, then divorced if I marry you, then divorced,' then at that time the first becomes effective immediately, the second is suspended, and the third is void because by his saying 'You are divorced' the divorce occurred, and by his saying 'then divorced if I marry you' she divorces upon the marriage that is the condition of the divorce, and the third is void because of lack of connection to the marriage. So reflect and look at his saying 'The summary is that the letters are three...' end. This objection is based on a defective copy he had, which says: and if he connects with thumma and delays the condition, the second is suspended and the first becomes effective, so the suspended occurs at the condition after the second marriage, and the third is void. In the case of a consummated woman, the first is suspended and what follows becomes effective. According to what is in most copies, there is no objection; rather it agrees with what is in al-Fath, al-Tabyin, al-Nahr, and others. His saying: (And it is restricted to a conjunction, etc.) In Ayman al-Bazzaziyya from the third chapter on oaths of divorce: if he says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, divorced, divorced' and she is not touched, then the first is suspended by the condition, the second descends immediately, and the third is void. If he marries her and she enters the house, the suspended descends. If she enters after separation before marriage, the oath is dissolved without a result. If she is a consummated wife, the first is suspended and the second and third descend immediately. This, as you see, contradicts what he transmitted here from al-Fath, unless there is a difference between 'one, one, one' and 'divorced, divorced, divorced', which is apparent. His saying: (Unlike 'Go, O enemy of Allah' because mentioning it with the conjunction fa requires its connection to what preceded, so the sentence becomes one. Otherwise, two would descend upon a consummated woman if she repeats 'every time I speak to you, you are divorced,' and the second is dissolved without a result, and it becomes void due to lack of ownership. And in the case of 'If I swear by your divorce,' the second oath does not dissolve except by suspending her divorce upon ownership or after it, because the condition is her entry into the result. Similarly, in the suspension of her divorce, and a consummated woman by swearing by their divorce, the second dissolves only by suspending her divorce upon ownership or after it, because the third oath is concluded upon the consummated woman, so the third is a part of the condition, and this is also a part in the right of the third, so it does not dissolve until he swears by the divorce of the consummated woman. This is the Bardha'iyyah case, end. Meaning this case is called al-Bardha'iyyah because Abu Sa'id al-Bardha'i, after he became a jurist and taught, was asked about it and could not find its answer, so he traveled to Baghdad and studied for seven years until he became one of our great companions. And it is restricted to a non-consummated woman because in her case everything is suspended by the condition, whether he puts it first or last. The whole sentence is one, unlike if he did not mention it with fa. But the dissolution of the first oath in our case according to Zufar is by the condition of the second, which is his saying 'If I speak to you,' because the condition of the breach is absolute speech, and it has occurred, so it becomes as if he confined himself to it, and he did not utter the result, so the result encounters her while she is separated without a waiting period, so the second oath does not conclude upon her. According to the three [Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad], it is by the result, so the second is concluded because the conditional sentence is one, and the common usage is complete speech, unlike if he confined himself to the condition, because the speech is both complete and incomplete. If he confines himself to the incomplete, it is known that it is intended; if he goes beyond to the complete, it is intended. According to the choice of Ibn al-Fadl, he does not breach if he confines himself, and this refutes Zufar's argument. And because if the sentence were not one, then two divorces would descend upon a consummated woman by repeating 'every time I divorce you, you are divorced,' because his saying a second time 'every time I divorce you' is addressing her, and similarly 'you are divorced' is a second address. So if the conclusion of the second oath is established, it dissolves by the existence of the third without a result because the result encounters her while she is separated, so the third becomes void due to lack of ownership. Abu Muti' and a group of scholars from Balkh said: nothing dissolves from it except with a new utterance, and this was the opinion of Abu Hanifa when Muhammad asked him in his childhood about someone who said three times 'By Allah, I will not speak to you' and said 'O shaykh, look well,' so he said: he breaks twice. Muhammad said: well said. And his saying 'And in the case of 'If I swear' etc.' means: in the case of someone who said three times to a non-consummated woman 'If I swear by your divorce, you are divorced,' the second oath does not dissolve except by suspending her divorce upon ownership, such as saying 'If I marry you, you are divorced' or it is suspended after marriage by saying 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' because the condition of dissolution here is swearing by her divorce, and that is by her entry into the result of the third oath, which is divorce, and it is not valid to enter her into it due to lack of ownership at the time of its occurrence, unlike the first because the condition, which is speech, is conceivable without ownership. Similarly, the ruling in the case of a man suspending the divorce of his two wives, one consummated and one not, by swearing by their divorce, such as saying to them three times 'If I swear by your divorce, you are both divorced,' the second dissolves with respect to them only by suspending the divorce of the non-consummated woman upon ownership or after it with another condition, as passed, because the third oath, which is the condition of dissolution of the second, was concluded upon the consummated woman only, because the condition for dissolution of the second is swearing by their divorce, and that is by entering both into the result of the third, which is divorce, and it was not possible to enter the non-consummated woman into it due to lack of ownership, so the third, with respect to dissolution of the second, is part of the condition, not all of it, so it does not effect any dissolution. Then if he later suspends the divorce of the non-consummated woman upon ownership or after it with another condition, the condition is completed, so each divorces.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq covers multiple complex scenarios of conditional divorce, including the famous Bardha'iyyah case. The text begins by discussing a case where a man says to his non-consummated wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, you are like my mother's back (zihar), and by Allah I will not approach you (ila').' According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the divorce takes precedence, so the wife is divorced and becomes separated, and the zihar and ila' are nullified because she is no longer his wife. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he is considered a man who has made both zihar and ila', meaning both are still valid. This difference arises because Abu Hanifa sees that the divorce, being immediate upon fulfillment of the condition, removes her from being a wife before zihar and ila' can take effect, while the Sahibayn view the combination as simultaneous. The text then mentions that if he marries a foreign woman and says the same, the same difference applies. However, if he put zihar and ila' first, then all three occur according to all scholars. Next, the text discusses a case where a man says to a woman 'On the day I marry you, you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced' (with 'wa'). If he marries her, only one divorce occurs and the other two are void because the condition (marriage) is mentioned only with the first 'taliq', and the subsequent ones are considered repetitive without a new condition. But if he says 'You are divorced, and divorced, and divorced on the day I marry you,' then all three occur upon marriage because the condition applies to all three. The Bardha'iyyah case is then introduced: it involves a man who says to his non-consummated wife three times 'If I speak to you, you are divorced.' The question is how many divorces occur and when. The text explains that according to Zufar, the first oath is dissolved by the second because the condition (speaking) has occurred, but the second oath is void because it encounters her while already divorced. According to the majority of Hanafis (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), the second oath is concluded because the conditional sentence is considered one complete unit, and the breach occurs upon speaking, so the first divorce takes effect, and the second oath is dissolved without a result because she is already divorced. The third is similarly void. The text also mentions the opinion of Ibn al-Fadl and Abu Muti'. The story of Abu Sa'id al-Bardha'i illustrates the difficulty of this case: he was a great scholar who was stumped by this question and had to study in Baghdad for seven years to find the answer, which led to the case being named after him. The passage then discusses other scenarios involving swearing by divorce and multiple wives. Throughout, the Hanafi position is that the structure of the sentence and the presence of conjunctions determine whether multiple oaths are considered one unit or separate. The relied-upon position in the school is that of the majority (Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn) as opposed to Zufar's view.

Key Terms
ثم (thumma): A conjunction meaning 'then', indicating sequential order.
الواو (al-waw): A conjunction meaning 'and', indicating connection without order.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a man says to a woman 'You are divorced, and divorced, and divorced on the day I marry you', how many divorces occur upon marriage?
A) One divorce.
B) Two divorces.
C) Three divorces.
D) No divorce because the condition is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The number of divorces in conditional scenarios depends on the wording, conjunctions, and wife's status; each case requires careful analysis of the sentence structure.

Talaq — Divorce
beginner
8 min
The Rule for Kinayat: Intention or Context Required
States basic rule for kinayat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that kinayat do not effect divorce without intention or contextual indication, and apply it to given expressions.

Core Idea
A kinayah does not divorce without niyyah or qarinah — the outward word alone is insufficient.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes the fundamental rule for indirect divorce expressions (kinayat). Unlike explicit words (sarih) that effect divorce immediately without need for intention, kinayat require either the husband's intention (niyyah) or a clear contextual clue (dalalat al-hal) for the divorce to be valid. The example of 'I'taddayt and Istabrayt illustrates how such phrases operate.

Guided Path
1
Identify what is a kinayah in divorce
Understand that kinayah are indirect expressions that may imply divorce but are not explicit, such as statements about waiting period or purity.
2
Understand the two conditions for kinayah to effect divorce
The passage states that divorce by kinayah only occurs with intention (niyyah) or contextual indication (dalalat al-hal). These are the only two ways a kinayah becomes operative.
3
Know the consequence when conditions are absent
If neither intention nor context is present, no divorce takes place. The woman remains married.
4
Recognise the default ruling when conditions are met
When the conditions are satisfied, the divorce is one revocable (raj‘i) divorce, as in the given example of 'I'taddayt and Istabrayt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكنايات
لا تطلق بها إلا بنية أو دلالة الحال فتطلق واحدة رجعية في اعتدى واستبرى أخره. وفي المحيط : لو قال لغير المدخول بها أنت طالق واحدة لا بل ثلاثاً إن دخلت الدار طلقت واحدة للحال وثلاثاً إن دخلت الدار ، لأن قوله أنت طالق واحدة للتنجيز وأراد بقوله لا بل ثلاثاً إن دخلت الدار تعليق الثلاث والرجوع عن إيقاع الواحدة فلا يصل الشرط المذكور آخراً بإيقاع الواحدة فصح تعليقه، ولم يصح رجوعه عن الواحدة. ولم قدم الشرط فقال إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق واحدة لا بل ثلاثاً لم تطلق حتى تدخل لأن قوله «لا بل ثلاثاً غير مستقل تام بنفسه فتعذر أن يجعل تنجيزاً فصار تعليقاً اهـ. والله أعلم بالصواب وإليه المرجع المآب.
Chapter of Kinayat: She is not divorced by them except with intention or indication of the situation, so she is divorced one revocable divorce in 'I'taddayt and Istabrayt and its end. And in al-Muhit: If he says to a non-consummated wife, 'You are divorced one, no rather three if you enter the house,' she is divorced one immediately and three if she enters the house, because his saying 'you are divorced one' is for immediate effect, and he intended by his saying 'no rather three if you enter the house' to suspend the three and to retract from effecting the one, so the condition mentioned later does not attach to the effecting of the one, so his suspension is valid, and his retraction from the one is not valid. And if he had placed the condition first and said, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced one, no rather three,' she is not divorced until she enters, because his saying 'no rather three' is not independent and complete by itself, so it cannot be made immediate, thus it becomes a suspension. End. And Allah knows best what is correct, and to Him is the return and the refuge.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq states the fundamental ruling for kinayat (indirect expressions) in divorce. The author explains that a woman is not divorced by kinayat in judicial ruling (qada) except with explicit intention (niyyah) or a clear contextual indication (dalalat al-hal). The example given is the phrase 'I'taddayt and Istabrayt' which implies divorce by referring to waiting period and seeking purity; it falls as one revocable divorce if intended. The passage then presents a detailed case from al-Muhit: for a non-consummated wife, if the husband says 'You are divorced one, no rather three if you enter the house,' the immediate one takes effect instantly, and the three becomes suspended on entry. The reasoning is that the first part is an immediate divorce, and the second part seeks to retract the first and suspend the three; the condition only attaches to the latter. If the husband reverses the order, saying 'If you enter the house, you are divorced one, no rather three,' then nothing occurs until entry because the phrase 'no rather three' cannot stand alone as an immediate statement; it becomes a suspension. The lesson shows the Hanafi fiqh principle that kinayat require additional elements to effect divorce, and the sequence of utterances affects the outcome.

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): An expression that is not explicit for divorce but may imply it, such as 'I'taddayt' (I observed the waiting period) or 'Istabrayt' (I sought purity).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is required for a kinayah to effect divorce in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Intention only
B) Context only
C) Intention or context
D) No requirement

Key Takeaway
A kinayah does not divorce without niyyah or qarinah.

Kinayah with Conditional Sequences for a Non-Consummated Wife
Expands on kinayah rule with a complex case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a complex utterance involving both immediate and conditional divorce, applying the rule of kinayah and the effect of sequence.

Core Idea
When a husband combines immediate and conditional divorce, the immediate takes effect first; reversing the order suspends both until the condition.

The passage from al-Muhit presents a detailed scenario for a non-consummated marriage (ghayr madkhul biha). The husband says a compound statement mixing an immediate one divorce with a conditional three divorces. The ruling depends on the order of the phrases. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the two possible sequences and the Hanafi juristic analysis of how each part is interpreted.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario: non-consummated wife, mixed divorce utterance.
The wife is not yet consummated, so divorce rules are slightly different (e.g., no iddah for non-consummated wife). The utterance combines an immediate number and a conditional number.
2
Analyse first order: 'You are divorced one, no rather three if you enter the house'.
The immediate one divorce takes effect instantly. The second part attempts to retract and suspend three divorces, but the retraction is invalid because the immediate divorce already occurred. The condition attaches only to the three, making it conditional.
3
Analyse second order: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced one, no rather three'.
Here the condition is placed first. The phrase 'no rather three' is not an independent complete statement; it cannot serve as an immediate divorce. Therefore, the entire utterance becomes conditional on entry. No divorce occurs until entry.
4
Grasp the linguistic principle: 'لا بل ثلاثاً' (no rather three) is not independent.
The text explains that 'لا بل ثلاثاً' is not a self-sufficient phrase (ghayr mustaqill tām). Because of this, it cannot be an immediate statement (tunjīz). It becomes part of the conditional (ta‘līq).
5
Note the practical outcome: sequence matters for when divorce takes effect.
In the first order, one divorce occurs immediately and three are conditional. In the second order, no divorce until entry. The husband’s intention to retract the one is blocked in the first order because it is already executed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكنايات
لا تطلق بها إلا بنية أو دلالة الحال فتطلق واحدة رجعية في اعتدى واستبرى أخره. وفي المحيط : لو قال لغير المدخول بها أنت طالق واحدة لا بل ثلاثاً إن دخلت الدار طلقت واحدة للحال وثلاثاً إن دخلت الدار ، لأن قوله أنت طالق واحدة للتنجيز وأراد بقوله لا بل ثلاثاً إن دخلت الدار تعليق الثلاث والرجوع عن إيقاع الواحدة فلا يصل الشرط المذكور آخراً بإيقاع الواحدة فصح تعليقه، ولم يصح رجوعه عن الواحدة. ولم قدم الشرط فقال إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق واحدة لا بل ثلاثاً لم تطلق حتى تدخل لأن قوله «لا بل ثلاثاً غير مستقل تام بنفسه فتعذر أن يجعل تنجيزاً فصار تعليقاً اهـ. والله أعلم بالصواب وإليه المرجع المآب.
Chapter of Kinayat: She is not divorced by them except with intention or indication of the situation, so she is divorced one revocable divorce in 'I'taddayt and Istabrayt and its end. And in al-Muhit: If he says to a non-consummated wife, 'You are divorced one, no rather three if you enter the house,' she is divorced one immediately and three if she enters the house, because his saying 'you are divorced one' is for immediate effect, and he intended by his saying 'no rather three if you enter the house' to suspend the three and to retract from effecting the one, so the condition mentioned later does not attach to the effecting of the one, so his suspension is valid, and his retraction from the one is not valid. And if he had placed the condition first and said, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced one, no rather three,' she is not divorced until she enters, because his saying 'no rather three' is not independent and complete by itself, so it cannot be made immediate, thus it becomes a suspension. End. And Allah knows best what is correct, and to Him is the return and the refuge.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq states the fundamental ruling for kinayat (indirect expressions) in divorce. The author explains that a woman is not divorced by kinayat in judicial ruling (qada) except with explicit intention (niyyah) or a clear contextual indication (dalalat al-hal). The example given is the phrase 'I'taddayt and Istabrayt' which implies divorce by referring to waiting period and seeking purity; it falls as one revocable divorce if intended. The passage then presents a detailed case from al-Muhit: for a non-consummated wife, if the husband says 'You are divorced one, no rather three if you enter the house,' the immediate one takes effect instantly, and the three becomes suspended on entry. The reasoning is that the first part is an immediate divorce, and the second part seeks to retract the first and suspend the three; the condition only attaches to the latter. If the husband reverses the order, saying 'If you enter the house, you are divorced one, no rather three,' then nothing occurs until entry because the phrase 'no rather three' cannot stand alone as an immediate statement; it becomes a suspension. The lesson shows the Hanafi fiqh principle that kinayat require additional elements to effect divorce, and the sequence of utterances affects the outcome.

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): An expression that is not explicit for divorce but may imply it, such as 'I'taddayt' (I observed the waiting period) or 'Istabrayt' (I sought purity).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to a non-consummated wife, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced one, no rather three,' when does divorce occur?
A) Immediately
B) Only upon entry
C) Not at all
D) Depends on his intention

Key Takeaway
The sequence of immediate and conditional divorce statements determines whether any divorce is immediate or all are suspended.

Comprehensive Definition of Kinayah in Language, Rhetoric, Usul, and Fiqh
Provides definitions of kinayah across language, rhetoric, usul, and fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the multiple definitions of kinayah across disciplines and apply the Hanafi fiqh ruling that kinayat in divorce require intention or contextual indication.

Core Idea
Kinayah in fiqh divorce is not explicit; its effect depends on intention or context, not just the words.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq offers a rare multi-disciplinary view of kinayah, tracing its meaning from language through rhetoric and usul to its critical role in Hanafi divorce law. Understanding these layers is essential to grasp why a simple phrase like 'I'taddayt' may or may not effect a divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic root and general definition of kinayah
Begin by understanding that kinayah linguistically derives from 'kana' meaning conceal. The general definition from al-Radhi covers expressing something indirectly for various purposes.
2
Understand the balaghah (rhetoric) definition
In rhetoric, kinayah is distinguished from explicit metaphor; it mentions a necessary concomitant to imply the intended meaning, e.g., 'long of the sword-belt' implies a tall man.
3
Learn the usul al-fiqh definition
In usul, kinayah is defined in contrast to sarih: if the intended meaning is hidden, it is kinayah; this applies to both literal and figurative speech.
Note the fluidity: a literal term that fell into disuse becomes kinayah, and a predominant figurative term becomes sarih.
4
Comprehend the fiqh definition for divorce
In fiqh, kinayah is any expression that could imply divorce or other meanings, such as 'I'taddayt'. The ruling is that it does not effect a divorce without intention or contextual indication.
The text restricts this to judicial ruling (qada'); in religion (diyanatan), only intention matters.
5
Apply the ruling to examples
Use the example from al-Muhit: if a husband says 'You are divorced if you wish and choose' and she responds, two divorces occur because of the preceding explicit word. Another example: the expression 'I'taddayt' requires analysis of intent and context.
Also consider the mufti's procedure for when the husband denies intention.

He placed the explicit (expressions) before them because it is the basis in speech since it is made for comprehension. And kinayah in language is derived from 'kana yaku' when he conceals. And al-Radhi mentioned that in language and terminology it is to express something, whether a word or meaning, with a word that is not explicit in indicating it, either for obscurity to some listeners like your saying 'so-and-so came to me' while you mean Zayd, and saying 'so-and-so said such-and-such' obscuring from some who hear, or for the offensiveness of what is expressed like 'hun' for the private part, or for brevity like pronouns, or for a type of eloquence like your saying 'so-and-so has much ash and much cut' or for other reasons. End. And in the science of rhetoric (bayan) according to the most correct view as in al-Mutawwal, that one does not explicitly mention the borrowed word but rather mentions its concomitant and necessary implication that indicates it. So the meaning of our saying 'claws of death' is a metaphor (isti'ara) of a predator for death, like the metaphor of lion for a brave man in our saying 'I saw a lion' but we did not explicitly mention the borrowed word, i.e., the predator, but we only mentioned its concomitant to transition from it to the intended, as is the way of kinayah. So the borrowed word is the word 'predator' which is not expressed; the borrowed-from is the predatory animal, the borrowed-for is death, etc. And in usul al-fiqh, it is said in al-Tanqih: Then each of the literal and figurative, if in itself the intended is not hidden, then it is explicit, otherwise kinayah. So the literal that has not fallen into disuse is explicit, and the one that has fallen into disuse and its figurative meaning predominates is kinayah. And the figurative that is predominantly used is explicit, and the non-predominant is kinayah. And according to scholars of rhetoric, kinayah is a word by which is intended from its meaning a second, necessary meaning, and it does not negate the intention of the original meaning, because it is used in it but its meaning intends a second meaning, as in 'long of the sword-belt,' unlike metaphor (majaz) which is used in other than what it was placed for, with the negation of intending the original meaning. End. And by his saying 'itself in' he excludes the uncovering of the intended through explanation and clarification, and the ambiguous and the total fall into it. And in fiqh here, it is what admits the possibility of divorce and other. His saying: 'She is not divorced by them except with intention or indication of the situation' means she is not divorced by kinayat in judicial ruling except with one of these two because they are not placed for divorce but placed for what is more general than it. Its ruling: as will come, that other than the three of them do not intend divorce at all, but rather the separation from marriage. And by 'indication of the situation' is meant the apparent condition that conveys his intention. Among them is the precedence of mention of divorce, as in al-Muhit: if he says to her 'You are divorced if you wish and choose,' and she says 'I wish and I choose,' two divorces occur: one by the wishing and the other by the choosing without intention because of the precedence of the explicit. And 'hal' in language is a description of a thing, masculine and feminine, it is said 'hal hasan' and 'hasana'. Thus in al-Misbah. We restricted it to judicial ruling because it does not occur religiously (diyanatan) except with intention, and no consideration is given to indication of the situation, such as if he says 'You are divorced' and intends it as 'from the shackle,' it does not occur religiously. And in al-Mujtaba from Sadr al-Qada in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: if he says 'I did not intend divorce,' he must take an oath if she claims divorce; if she does not claim, he also swears by Allah. Abu Nasr ibn Salama said: Should the judge administer the oath or should she? He said: It suffices for her to administer the oath to him in his house; if she makes him swear and he swears, she is his wife; otherwise she raises the matter to the judge. If he refuses the oath before the judge, he separates them. End. And in al-Bazzaziyya: In every situation where intention is required, the mufti looks at the questioner's question; if he says 'I said such-and-such, does it occur?' he says 'Yes, if you intended'; if he says 'How many occur?' he says 'One' and does not mention the condition of intention. And from Muhammad, I said: His statement: 'So she is divorced one revocable in I'taddayt and Istabrayt, may Allah have mercy on you, and you are one' because the first admits the possibility of waiting period from marriage and from Allah's blessings, so the first is determined by intention, and it necessitates a prior divorce which follows the return if after consummation; before it, it is a metaphor for 'I am divorced' from applying the ruling and intending the cause, and it is not made a metaphor for 'my divorce' because no divorce occurs by it, nor for 'you are divorced' or 'I divorced you' as will come in the speech of al-Tanqih. As for this, it is the metaphorical metonymy (al-isti'ara al-makniyya) corresponding to the explicit. Then I saw it criticized in al-Nahr after he mentioned the meaning of kinayah according to them similarly to what will come; he said: What al-Bahr mentioned is the metaphor by kinayah.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides a comprehensive definition of kinayah (indirect expression) across multiple disciplines: language, rhetoric (bayan), usul al-fiqh, and fiqh. The author begins by noting that explicit expressions (sarih) are prioritized in speech because they are the origin. Linguistically, kinayah derives from 'kana' meaning to conceal. Al-Radhi defines it as expressing something with a non-explicit word for various reasons: to obscure from listeners, avoid offensiveness, achieve brevity, or for eloquence. In rhetoric, the correct view distinguishes kinayah from explicit metaphor: kinayah mentions a necessary concomitant (lazim) to imply the intended meaning without stating the borrowed word itself, as in 'claws of death' where 'death' is not mentioned but its necessary attribute 'claws' (from a predator) is used. In usul al-fiqh, as per al-Tanqih, the distinction between explicit and kinayah applies to both literal and figurative speech: if the intended is not hidden, it is explicit; otherwise it is kinayah. Thus, a literal term that has fallen into disuse and is figuratively dominant becomes kinayah, and a figurative term that is predominant becomes explicit. In fiqh, kinayah in divorce is defined as any expression that could admit divorce or other meanings, such as 'I'taddayt' (I observed the waiting period) or 'Istabrayt' (I sought purity). The fiqh ruling is that such expressions do not effect divorce in judicial ruling (qada') except with intention or contextual indication, because they are not exclusively placed for divorce. Religion (diyanatan), however, only intention matters, not context. The passage gives examples: if a husband says 'You are divorced if you wish and choose' and she responds, two divorces occur due to the preceding explicit word 'divorced.' It also discusses cases where the husband denies intention, requiring an oath, and procedures for the mufti. Finally, it addresses a specific kinayah: 'You are divorced one revocable in I'taddayt and Istabrayt' and its interpretation, tying it to the concept of metaphorical metonymy (isti'ara makniyya). The commentary clarifies that the Hanafi fiqh definition is practical: any expression that is not a clear, explicit divorce term requires additional elements to be effective.

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): In fiqh: an expression that could imply divorce but also other meanings, thus not explicit; requires intention or context to effect divorce.
دلالة الحال (dalalat al-hal): A contextual indication that reveals the speaker's intention, such as the preceding mention of divorce.
صريح (sarih): An explicit, unambiguous expression for divorce, e.g., 'You are divorced.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi fiqh definition of kinayah in divorce, which of the following best describes it?
A) An expression that has only one meaning and is explicitly for divorce.
B) An expression that could mean divorce or something else, thus requiring intention or context.
C) An expression that is always figurative and never used literally.
D) An expression that is only used in rhetoric and not in legal rulings.

Key Takeaway
Kinayah in Hanafi divorce law is not self-effectuating; it requires intention or contextual indication to have legal effect.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Rulings on Specific Kinayat: I'taddi, Istabrii, and Anti Wahidah
Explains the ruling and conditions for three kinayah words resulting in one revocable divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the three kinayah words (i'taddi, istabrii rahimak, anti wahidah) and apply the istihsan-based proof for i'taddi.

Core Idea
Intention is required; the default is one revocable divorce for each of these three kinayat.

In this passage, Imam Ibn Nujaym explains the rulings for three specific kinayah words used in divorce. Each word – 'i'taddi', 'istabrii rahimak', and 'anti wahidah' – requires intention to effect a divorce, and the default outcome is a single revocable divorce. This lesson unpacks the proofs, including the hadith of Sawda that establishes istihsan for 'i'taddi', and clarifies the conditions under which these expressions are effective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three words and their common ruling
The passage states that each of the three kinayah words results in one revocable divorce when accompanied by intention. Note that these are kinayat (allusive expressions), not explicit (sarih) terms.
2
Analyze the proof for 'i'taddi'
The author discusses the istihsan basis: the Prophet (s) said 'I'taddi' to Sawda and then took her back, indicating a revocable divorce. Qiyas would suggest a ba'in divorce, but istihsan overrides it because the cause (illa) for ba'in is absent: 'i'taddi' implies a prior divorce and 'iddah, not a new irrevocable separation.
The hadith of Sawda is the primary dalil for istihsan.
3
Understand the condition of intention for each word
For 'istabrii rahimak', the phrase explicitly asks to check the womb, so it could be interpreted as a past or future divorce. Intention distinguishes the two. For 'anti wahidah', it could be a description or a number; intention specifies it as one divorce.
Intention is always required for kinayah.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
There is a difference regarding whether intending a ba'in (irrevocable) divorce with 'i'taddi' is valid. The mashayikh hold it is not valid; Fath al-Qadir suggests it could be valid on one interpretation. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) follows the mashayikh.
The text gives preference to the mashayikh.
5
Apply the ruling to scenarios
If a husband says 'I'taddi' intending a single revocable divorce, it is effective. If he intends ba'in, his intention is not valid and only a revocable divorce occurs. Similarly for 'istabrii rahimak' and 'anti wahidah'.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is whether the husband can take the wife back or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

مجاز عن كوني طالقاً من إطلاق الحكم وإرادة العلة ولا يجعل مجازاً عن طلقي لأنه لا يقع به طلاق ولا عن أنت طالق أو طلقتك ما يأتي في كلام التنقيح، أما هذه فهي الاستعارة المكنية المقابلة للمصرحة، ثم رأيته تعقبه في النهر بعد ما ذكر معنى الكناية عندهم بنحو ما يأتي قال : إن ما ذكره في البحر هو الاستعارة بالكناية لأنهم يشترطون التوافق في الصيغة كذا في التلويح وما في الشرح من أنه من إطلاق المسبب وإرادة السبب فممنوع لأنه يرد عليه أن شرطه اختصاص المسبب بالسبب والعدة لا تختص بالطلاق لثبوتها في أم الولد إذا أعتقت وما أجيب ،به من أن ثبوتها فيما ذكر لوجود سبب ثبوتها في الطلاق وهو الاستبراء إلا بالأصالة فغير دافع سؤال عدم الاختصاص. كذا في فتح القدير. وفي التلويح والاعتداد شرعاً بطريق الأصالة مختص بالطلاق لا يوجد في غيره إلا بطريق التبع والشبه كالموت وحدوث حرمة المصاهرة وارتداد الزوج وغيرها. وقد يقال إن «اعتدي من باب الإضمار أي طلقتك فاعتدي أو اعتدي لأني طلقتك، ففي المدخولة يثبت الطلاق وتجب العدة، وفي غيرها يثبت الطلاق عملاً بنيته ولا تجب العدة هـ. وهو يفيد أنه من باب الاقتضاء في غير المدخولة أيضاً وإن كان أمرها فيها بالعدة ليس شيئاً فلا حاجة إلى تكلف المجاز. والمراد بالمسبب هنا وجوب عد الأقراء المستفاد من الأمر، وما في النوادر من أن وقوع الرجعي بها استحسان الحديث سودة يعني أنه عليه السلام قال لها اعتدي ثم راجعها، والقياس أن يقع البائن كسائر الكنايات بعيد، بل ثبوت الرجعي قياس واستحسان. لأن علة البينونة في غير الثلاثة منتفية فيها فلا يتجه القياس أصلاً. كذا في فتح القدير. وقد سلك المحقق في فتح القدير طريقاً غير طريقهم في تقرير أن «اعتدي» من باب الاقتضاء فقال : إن «اعتدي» يقتضي فرقة بعد الدخول وهي أعم من رجعي وبائن ذلك تعين البائن بل تعين الأخف لعدم الدلالة على الزائد ا هـ. وهو مسلك حسن لكن يلزم عليه أنه لو نوى البائن في قوله «اعتدي صحت نيته، وعلى ما قرره المشايخ الطلاق لم تصح نيته. وأما استبرى رحمك فلأنه تصريح بما هو المقصود من العدة تعرف براءة الرحم فيحتمل استبريه لأني طلقتك أو لأطلقك إذا علمت خلوه عن الولد. وعلى الأول يقع، وعلى الثاني لا فلا بد من النية ويجب كونه مجازاً عن كوني طالقاً المدخولة إذا كانت آيسة أو صغيرة وفي غير المدخولة مطلقاً. وأما «أنت واحدة» فيحتمل أن يكون نعتاً لمصدر محذوف معناه تطليقة واحدة، فإذا نواه مع هذا الوصف فكأنه قاله والطلاق يعقبه الرجعة، ويحتمل غيره نحو أنت واحدة عندي أو في قومك مدحاً وذماً فقد ظهر أن الطلاق في هذه الألفاظ الثلاثة مقتضى ولو كان مظهراً لا يقع به إلا واحدة، فإذا كان مضمراً أو أنه أضعف منه أولى. وأشار المصنف بقوله واحدة رجعية) إلى أنه لو نوى البينونة الكبرى أو الصغرى لا تعتبر نيته وهو ظاهر في الأوليين، وأما في أنت واحدة فالمصدر وإن لكن لا يوجب من هو في التي من المجاز بعلاقة المشابهة ولا يصح إرادتها في شيء من الألفاظ الآتية بخلاف الكناية بالمعنى المذكور فإنه يصح إرادتها في نحو «اعتدي» كما سيأتي .
قوله: (وهو يفيد أن من باب الاقتضاء) قال في النهر : فيه نظر كيف وقد جعله مقابلاً له
His statement: So she is divorced one revocable divorce in 'I'taddi', 'Istabrii rahimak', and 'Anti wahidah'. Because the first [i'taddi] is probable of 'counting the waiting period from marriage' and from 'the blessings of Allah', so the first is determined by intention, and it necessitates a prior divorce which is followed by return (raj'a) if after consummation; but before consummation it is metaphorical for 'I am divorced' by releasing the ruling and intending the cause, and it is not made metaphorical for 'my divorce' because no divorce occurs by it, nor for 'you are divorced' or 'I divorced you' as will come in the speech of al-Tanqih. As for these, they are the metonymic metaphor (isti'ara makniyya) opposed to the explicit, then I saw him follow it in al-Nahr after mentioning the meaning of kinayah according to them in a similar way: he said that what he mentioned in al-Bahr is isti'ara bi al-kinayah because they require agreement in the form, as in al-Talwih. And what is in the sharh that it is releasing the cause and intending the effect is prohibited because it is objected that its condition is the specificity of the effect to the cause, and 'iddah is not specific to divorce since it is established for umm al-walad when she is freed. And what was answered by that its establishment in what was mentioned is due to the existence of its cause in divorce, which is istibra', except fundamentally, does not repel the objection of lack of specificity. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Talwih: 'iddah legally in the primary way is specific to divorce, not found in other than it except by way of subsequence and similarity, like death, occurrence of prohibition of affinity, apostasy of husband, etc. And it may be said that 'i'taddi' is from the chapter of implied [pronoun], i.e. 'I divorced you, so observe 'iddah' or 'observe 'iddah because I divorced you'. So in the consummated [wife] divorce is established and 'iddah is obligatory; in the non-consummated, divorce is established by his intention but 'iddah is not obligatory. It is understood that it is from the chapter of implication (iqtida') in the non-consummated as well, although commanding her to observe 'iddah is nothing, so there is no need for strained metaphor. And the intended effect here is the obligation of counting menstrual periods derived from the command. And what is in al-Nawadir that the occurrence of revocable divorce by it is istihsan based on the hadith of Sawda, meaning that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to her 'I'taddi' then took her back. The analogy (qiyas) would be that it occurs as a ba'in divorce like other kinayat, but it is far. Rather, the establishment of revocable divorce is both qiyas and istihsan, because the cause of ba'in divorce in other than the three [words] is negated in it, so analogy does not apply at all. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And the muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir took a path different from theirs in establishing that 'i'taddi' is from the chapter of implication: he said that 'i'taddi' implies separation after consummation, which is more general than revocable and irrevocable, so that necessitates the irrevocable? No, it necessitates the lighter [revocable] because there is no indication of the extra. It is a good approach, but it follows that if he intended ba'in in saying 'i'taddi', his intention is valid; according to what the mashayikh determined of talaq, his intention is not valid. As for 'Istabrii rahimak', because it is an explicit statement of what is intended from 'iddah, i.e. knowing the purity of the womb, so it is probable: 'check it because I divorced you' or 'I will divorce you if I know it is free from a child'. On the first, it occurs; on the second, it does not, so intention is necessary, and it must be metaphorical for 'I am divorced' in the consummated if she is menopausal or minor, and in the non-consummated absolutely. As for 'Anti wahidah', it is probable that it is an adjective for a deleted noun meaning 'one divorce', so if he intends it with this description, it is as if he said it, and divorce is followed by return. It is also probable of other meanings like 'you are one in my view' or 'among your people' in praise or blame. So it is apparent that divorce in these three words is implied (muqtada), and if it were explicit, only one would occur; so if it is implicit or weaker, it is more appropriate. And the author indicated by his statement 'one revocable' that if he intends major or minor irrevocability, his intention is not valid. This is clear in the first two; as for 'Anti wahidah', the [deleted] noun does not necessitate... but it is from metaphor with similarity and its intent is not valid in any of the following words, unlike kinayah in the mentioned meaning, for its intent is valid in 'i'taddi' as will come. His statement: 'It is understood that it is from the chapter of implication.' In al-Nahr: there is consideration; how has he made it opposite?
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe explaining the three kinayah words mentioned in the matn: 'اعتدى' (observe 'iddah), 'استبرى رحمك' (check your womb), and 'أنت واحدة' (you are one). The author clarifies that each requires intention to effect divorce, and the default divorce is one revocable (raj'i). He discusses the istihsan basis for 'اعتدى' being revocable rather than ba'in, citing the hadith of Sawda. He also addresses the possibility of implied meanings: 'اعتدى' can imply a prior divorce, so it necessitates 'iddah; if the wife is non-consummated, 'iddah is not required but divorce still occurs by intention. For 'استبرى رحمك', it directly addresses the purpose of 'iddah (checking for pregnancy), so intention distinguishes between a past or future divorce. 'أنت واحدة' is a description that could be praise or a number, so intention specifies it as a divorce count. The author notes that if one intends a ba'in divorce with these words, the intention is not valid in the first two according to the relied-upon position, because the words themselves only support revocable divorce. The passage ends with a critique from al-Nahr regarding the iqtida' analysis.

Key Terms
اعتدى (iʿtaddī): A kinayah word meaning 'observe the waiting period', used for divorce by implication.
استبرى رحمك (istabriī raḥimak): A kinayah phrase meaning 'check your womb for pregnancy', used for divorce by implication.
أنت واحدة (anti wāḥidah): A kinayah phrase meaning 'you are one', often interpreted as 'one divorce' with intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of a husband saying 'I'taddi' to his wife with the intention of a single revocable divorce?
A) No divorce occurs because 'i'taddi' is not a divorce word.
B) One revocable divorce occurs.
C) Three divorces occur.
D) One irrevocable divorce occurs.

Key Takeaway
Each of the three kinayah words—'i'taddi', 'istabrii rahimak', and 'anti wahidah'—requires intention and results in one revocable divorce in the Hanafi school.

Module 9 · Lesson 257

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Three Indirect Expressions for Divorce: I'taddi, Istabri Rahimak, and Anti Wahidah
Commentary on the ruling that three specific indirect expressions yield a single revocable divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for three specific indirect divorce expressions and explain why they yield a revocable divorce.

Core Idea
Three indirect expressions produce a single revocable divorce; intention for irrevocability is disregarded.

In this lesson, we examine three indirect expressions for divorce that have a unique ruling in Hanafi fiqh: 'i'taddi', 'istabri rahimak', and 'anti wahidah'. Unlike other indirect expressions, these yield only a single revocable divorce regardless of the husband's intention for a stronger effect. We will explore the proof from the hadith of Sawdah and understand the internal Hanafi dispute that led to this relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three expressions
List the three expressions and their literal meanings: 'i'taddi' (observe the waiting period), 'istabri rahimak' (seek purity of your womb), and 'anti wahidah' (you are one). Note that each has a meaning that could relate to divorce but not exclusively.
The key is that these expressions mention or imply divorce directly.
2
Understand the ruling
State the ruling: if the husband uses any of these expressions with the intention of divorce, a single revocable divorce occurs. Contrast with other indirect expressions that yield an irrevocable divorce unless intention for three is present.
The ruling is specific; not all indirect expressions are treated alike.
3
Examine the proof
Analyze the hadith of Sawdah bint Zam'ah, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) told her 'i'taddi' and later took her back (muraja'ah). This demonstrates that the divorce was revocable, as irrevocable divorce would require a new marriage for return.
This hadith is the primary textual evidence for the ruling.
4
Analyze the internal dispute
Review the debate among Hanafi scholars: some argued that 'i'taddi' implies separation generally and thus intention for irrevocability should be considered. However, the relied-upon position (mashayikh) is that it is from implication (iqtida') and yields only revocable divorce regardless of intention.
The dispute also includes whether the grammatical case of 'anti wahidah' matters; the correct view is that it does not.
