By operation of the book of divorce, chapter on indirect expressions in divorce. Because they stipulate compatibility in the wording. As in al-Talwih. And what is in the commentary that it is from the unrestricted use of the cause and intending the effect is prevented because it is objected that its condition is the specificity of the cause to the effect, and the waiting period is not specific to divorce because it is established for the umm walad when she is freed. And what was answered by that its establishment in what is mentioned is due to the existence of its cause in divorce, which is istibra', except by way of originality, so it does not repel the question of non-specificity. As in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Talwih: The waiting period (i'tidad) legally by way of originality is specific to divorce, not found in other than it except by way of following and similarity, such as death, occurrence of prohibition of in-lawship, apostasy of the husband, and others. And it may be said that 'i'taddi' is from the category of implicitness (idmar), i.e., 'I have divorced you, so observe the waiting period' or 'observe the waiting period because I have divorced you.' In the consummated woman, divorce is established and the waiting period is obligatory; in the unconsummated, divorce is established by operation of his intention and the waiting period is not obligatory. This indicates that it is from the category of implication (iqtida') in the unconsummated as well, although commanding her to the waiting period there is nothing, so there is no need to resort to metaphor. And what is intended by the effect here is the obligation of counting the menstrual periods derived from the command. And what is in al-Nawadir that the occurrence of revocable divorce by it is istihsan, the hadith of Sawdah, i.e., that he (peace be upon him) said to her: 'Observe the waiting period' and then took her back. And by qiyas, the irrevocable would occur as in other indirect expressions, but that is far; rather the establishment of revocable is by qiyas and istihsan because the cause of irrevocability in others is absent here, so qiyas does not apply at all. As in Fath al-Qadir. And the investigator in Fath al-Qadir took a different path from theirs in confirming that 'i'taddi' is from the category of implication, saying: ''I'taddi' necessitates separation after consummation, which is general covering both revocable and irrevocable, so the specific determination of the irrevocable is not necessary; rather the lighter is determined due to lack of indication of the extra.' (End) This is a good approach, but it requires that if he intends irrevocable by saying 'i'taddi', his intention would be valid, whereas according to what the scholars have established, the divorce is revocable, his intention is not valid. As for 'istabri rahimak' (seek purity of your womb), it is explicit of what is intended from the waiting period, i.e., knowing the freedom of the womb, so it may mean 'seek purity because I have divorced you' or 'so that I may divorce you when you know it is free of child.' In the first case, it occurs; in the second, it does not, so intention is necessary and it must be metaphorical for 'I am divorcing you' in the consummated if she is past menopause or a minor, and in the unconsummated absolutely. As for 'anti wahidah' (you are one), it may be an adjective for an omitted verbal noun meaning 'one divorce', so if he intends it with this description, it is as if he said it, and the divorce is followed by return (ruj'ah); and it may be other than that, such as 'you are unique in my estimation' or 'among your people' as praise or blame. Thus it is clear that divorce in these three expressions is implied (muqtada), and if it were explicit, only one would occur by it, so if it is implicit (mudmar) or weaker, it is more so. And the author indicated by his saying 'one revocable' that if he intends greater or lesser irrevocability, his intention is not considered. This is apparent in the first two expressions; as for 'anti wahidah', the verbal noun even if mentioned with its description, but the specification of one prevents intending three because it is the adjective of the limited verbal noun by the feminine (ha), so it does not exceed one. And he used the word 'one' in the absolute, so it indicates that its grammatical case is not considered. This is the statement of the common people and it is the correct view because the common people do not distinguish between the modes of grammatical case, and the elite do not adhere to it in their speech by custom; rather that is their craft and custom is their language. And we have mentioned in our commentary on al-Manar that they did not consider it here but considered it in an acknowledgment, as when he says 'he has a dirham except a daniq' with raf' or nasb, so a distinction is needed. Since the cause for the occurrence of revocable divorce with these three expressions is the existence of divorce as implied (muqtada) or implicit (mudmar), it is known that there is no restriction in his words; rather every indirect expression that contains mention of divorce falls under his words and revocable divorce occurs by it a fortiori, such as his saying: 'I am free of your divorce,' 'the divorce is upon you,' 'upon you is divorce,' 'divorce is due to you,' 'I gave you your divorce,' 'I bought your divorce without exchange,' 'God willed your divorce,' 'God decreed your divorce,' 'you wish your divorce,' 'you left your divorce,' 'I released your divorce,' 'you are divorced' with sukun on the ta, 'you are more divorced than so-and-so's wife,' 'she is divorced,' 'you are divorced' by dropping the final letter, 'take your divorce,' 'I loaned you your divorce,' 'I lent you your divorce.' And the matter becomes in her hand according to what is in al-Muhit: 'You are not a wife to me,' 'I am not a husband to you,' 'You are not a husband to me,' 'You are not a wife to me' unlike if he says 'I am free of your marriage,' for it does not occur. Ibn Salam said. And in al-Khulasa, there is disagreement regarding 'I am free of your divorce' if he intended, and the most correct is that it occurs, and the most apparent to me is that it occurs as irrevocable, as in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Mi'raj, the principle upon which fatwa is given in Persian divorce: if there is a word that is not used except for divorce, then that word is explicit, occurring without intention if addressed to the woman, such as 'zan ra ha kardam' in the custom of the people of Khorasan and Iraq, because the explicit does not vary by languages. And what is in Persian that is used for divorce and other things is from the indirect expressions of Persian, so its ruling is the same as the ruling of Arabic indirect expressions in all rulings. End. His statement: (And in other than them it is irrevocable, and if he intends two, and his intention of three is valid) i.e., in other than the three expressions, one irrevocable divorce occurs or three by intention, and the intention of two is not valid in the free woman because we have already stated that... and what is in its meaning. Reflect. His statement: (So it does not exceed one) i.e., the intention of greater irrevocability is not considered, and he did not explicitly state the non-consideration of lesser irrevocability although the discussion is intended to explain it also, as it is known from the fact that the occurrence is by the verbal noun which is 'one divorce.' His statement: (It is the statement of the common people and it is the correct view) is a precaution against what some said: that if 'one' is in the nominative (raf'), nothing occurs even if he intended, and if it is in the accusative (nasb), one occurs even if he did not intend, because then it is an adjective of the verbal noun, i.e., 'you are divorced with one divorce,' so he has explicitly divorced, and if it is with sukun, intention is needed. As in al-Fath. His statement: (So they need a distinction) said in al-Nahr: 'It seems that it is by way of caution in both chapters.' His statement: (Rather every indirect expression that contains mention of divorce, etc.) is deficient considering what he also mentions, such as his saying 'You are not a wife to me' which does not contain mention of divorce, so reflect.
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter of indirect expressions in divorce from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author explains the ruling regarding three specific indirect expressions: 'i'taddi' (observe the waiting period), 'istabri rahimak' (seek purity of your womb), and 'anti wahidah' (you are one). The main ruling is that if a husband uses any of these expressions with the intention of divorce, a single revocable (raj'i) divorce occurs. This is unlike other indirect expressions which produce an irrevocable (baa'in) divorce unless the intention is for three. The passage discusses the internal Hanafi dispute about the basis: whether it is by implication (iqtida') or metaphor (majaaz). The relied-upon position is that it is by implication, and that the divorce is revocable. The intention of the husband for a greater or lesser irrevocable divorce is not considered with these three expressions. The text also covers the ruling for Persian equivalents and lists many other indirect expressions. It emphasizes that the grammatical case of the word 'one' is not considered; the common people's usage is the standard. The proof is the hadith of Sawdah where the Prophet (peace be upon him) said 'i'taddi' and then took her back, indicating revocability.

Key Terms
كناية (kinaayah): An indirect expression that can be used for divorce but also has other meanings, requiring intention for divorce to take effect.
رجعي (raj'i): Revocable divorce where the husband may take the wife back during the waiting period without a new marriage.
بائن (baa'in): Irrevocable divorce where the husband cannot take the wife back without a new marriage and contract.
اقتضاء (iqtida'): Implication where the intended meaning is derived from the context and necessity, not from the literal wording.
مقتضى (muqtada): The implied meaning that is understood from the context, even if not explicitly stated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'أنت واحدة' (anti wahidah) with nasb (accusative case) intending divorce, what is the ruling according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) One revocable divorce occurs.
B) One irrevocable divorce occurs because of the accusative case.
C) Three divorces occur because the word 'one' specifies a single divorce.
D) No divorce occurs because the case is not accusative.

Key Takeaway
The three indirect expressions 'i'taddi', 'istabri rahimak', and 'anti wahidah' produce a single revocable divorce; intention for irrevocability is disregarded.

Three Specified Kinayat: Default Ruling and Intention for Multiple
Ruling statement with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default ruling (raj'i) for the three specified kinayat and explain the legal effect of intending two divorces.

Core Idea
Three kinayat yield raj'i; other kinayat yield ba'in. Intent for two becomes three.

In Hanafi divorce law, kinayat (allusive words) require intention. The matn specifies three phrases that are exceptional: they produce a revocable (raj'i) divorce, while all other kinayat give an irrevocable (ba'in) divorce. This lesson unpacks that rule and the impact of intending multiple divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three specified kinayat
The matn mentions 'رحمك الله' and 'وأنت واحدة' as two of the three. The third is typically understood from context (e.g., 'أنت علي حرام' in some texts). The phrase 'وفي غيرها بائنة' implies these three are exceptions to the general rule that kinayat give ba'in.
The exact list of the three may vary slightly by source, but these two are clear.
2
Determine the default ruling
When a husband utters one of these three kinayat without any intention of a specific number, a single raj'i (revocable) divorce occurs. This is the default ruling from Zahir al-Riwayah.
Raj'i means the husband can take his wife back during the iddah without a new marriage contract.
3
Analyze the effect of intending two divorces
If the husband intends two divorces, the law treats his intention as three, and she becomes ba'in (irrevocably separated). This is because the word 'واحدة' limits the number to one, so any intention exceeding one is seen as an attempt to circumvent the limit and is escalated to three.
The matn states: 'وإن نوى ثنتين وتصح نيته الثلاث وهي بائن بتة'.
4
Understand the linguistic reasoning
The word 'واحدة' is an adjective describing the implied noun 'تطليقة', and the feminine 'ha' restricts it to a single instance. Therefore, the expression cannot support an intention for two, but it can be overridden by an intention for three (which is permissible albeit resulting in ba'in).
This is a talil (causal reasoning) based on grammatical limitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب الكنايات في الطلاق رحمك وأنت واحدة وفي غيرها بائنة وإن نوى ثنتين وتصح نيته الثلاث وهي بائن بتة. كان مذكوراً بذكر صفته لكن التنصيص على الواحدة يمنع إرادة الثلاث لأنها صفة للمصدر المحدود بالهاء فلا يتجاوز الواحدة وأطلق في واحدة فأفاد أنه لا معتبر بإعرابها وهو قول العامة وهو الصحيح لأن العوام لا يميزون بين وجوه الإعراب والخواص لا تلتزمه في كلامهم عرفاً بل تلك صناعتهم والعرف لغتهم، وقد ذكرنا في شرحنا على المنار أنهم لم يعتبروه هنا واعتبروه في الإقرار فيما لو قال له درهم غير دانق رفعاً ونصباً فيحتاجون إلى الفرق.
Book of Divorce, Chapter on Kinayat in Divorce. 'May Allah have mercy on you' and 'you are one' and in other than these, it is ba'in, and if he intends two, his intention for three is valid, and she is ba'in battah. It was mentioned describing its attribute, but specifying one prevents intending three because it is an adjective for the source limited by the feminine 'ha', so it does not exceed one. And he was absolute in 'one', so it indicated that its i'rab is not considered; this is the statement of the common people and it is correct because the common people do not distinguish between types of i'rab, and the elite do not adhere to it in their speech customarily, rather that is their craft, and custom is their language. And we have mentioned in our commentary on al-Manar that they did not consider it here and they considered it in acknowledgment in the case where one says 'he has a dirham except a daniq' in nominative and accusative, so they need a distinction.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and commentary on the three specified kinayat for divorce from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The matn states that three phrases: 'رحمك الله' (may Allah have mercy on you), 'وأنت واحدة' (you are one), and other similar phrases (implied) result in ba'in divorce except that when the husband says these three specific phrases, the divorce is raj'i (revocable). However, if he intends two divorces, his intention for three is valid, and she becomes ba'in battah (irrevocably separated). The commentary focuses on 'وأنت واحدة': 'واحدة' is an adjective describing the implied source 'تطليقة' (one divorce), limited by the feminine 'ha', so it cannot exceed one. Thus intention for three divorces is invalid because the wording restricts to one. The commentary then discusses the i'rab (grammatical inflection) of 'واحدة': whether nominative or accusative. The correct view (al-‘ammah) is that i'rab is not considered in divorce statements because common people do not distinguish cases, and even the elite do not adhere to it customarily. This is supported by the fact that in legal affidavits (iqrar), i'rab is considered for precision, but not here. Thus the ruling stands regardless of grammatical case.

Key Terms
كناية (kināyah): An allusive word or phrase that requires intention (niyyah) for divorce to occur.
بائن (bā’in): Irrevocable divorce that severs the marital bond completely, allowing remarriage only after a new marriage.
رجعي (raj‘ī): Revocable divorce where the husband can take his wife back during the 'iddah without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A single raj'i divorce
B) A single ba'in divorce
C) No divorce occurs
D) Three divorces occur

Key Takeaway
The three specified kinayat yield a default raj'i divorce; intending two is treated as three.

The Irrelevance of I'rab in Divorce Statements
Ruling with talil based on 'urf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why i'rab (grammatical inflection) is not considered in divorce utterances according to the Hanafi school, and distinguish this from the rule in iqrar.

Core Idea
Custom overrides grammar: common people do not distinguish i'rab, so divorce rulings ignore it.

In the same matn, the commentator addresses whether the grammatical case of 'واحدة' affects the divorce ruling. The Hanafi position, based on custom ('urf), is that i'rab is irrelevant because the common people do not inflect correctly, and even the elite do not observe it in ordinary speech. This lesson examines the reasoning and its distinction from financial acknowledgments (iqrar).

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The matn allows 'واحدة' to be absolute (مطلق), meaning its i'rab (whether nominative, accusative, or genitive) does not need to be considered. The question is whether the ruling changes based on grammatical case.
2
State the Hanafi position
The correct view (قول العامة) is that i'rab is not considered in divorce statements. The ruling is based on the customary usage of common people who do not distinguish between cases.
3
Understand the reasoning
The argument uses 'urf (custom): the common people ('awamm) do not differentiate between nominative, accusative, and genitive endings. Even the elite (khawass) do not adhere to i'rab in everyday speech; it is a specialized skill (sinā‘ah). Custom is their effective language.
This is a talil (causal reasoning) grounded in social reality.
4
Distinguish from iqrar
In acknowledgment (iqrar), i'rab is considered because precision is needed. For example, saying 'له درهم غير دانق' with nominative vs accusative changes the meaning. In divorce, such precision is not required because the intention and customary meaning suffice.
The difference is due to the nature of the legal act: iqrar requires exactness, while divorce focuses on intention.

Source Text

Kinayat Containing 'Talaq' and Their Raj'i Ruling
Expansion of kinayat with 'talaq' as raj'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which kinayat result in raj'i divorce and explain the 'illa that makes them raj'i.

Core Idea
Any kinaya that explicitly mentions the word 'talaq' results in a single raj'i divorce.

In Hanafi fiqh, certain indirect expressions (kinayat) can effect divorce. The key distinction is whether the word 'talaq' appears. This lesson enumerates phrases containing 'talaq' and establishes their ruling as raj'i, while also noting a specific ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 'illa for raj'i divorce
The passage states that the cause ('illa) for the three specified phrases being raj'i is the presence of the word 'talaq' either explicitly (muqtaḍā) or implicitly (muḍmar). Recognize this 'illa.
2
Extend the 'illa to all kinayat
Since the 'illa is the presence of 'talaq', any kinaya that includes this word falls under the same ruling. The phrase 'كل كناية كان فيها ذكر الطلاق' indicates the generality.
3
Review the list of examples
Read through the enumerated phrases such as 'أنا بريء من طلاقك', 'الطلاق عليك', 'خذي طلاقك', etc. Note that each contains the word 'talaq'.
4
Note the exception
The passage distinguishes 'أنا بريء من نكاحك' (I am free from your marriage) which does NOT contain 'talaq' and therefore does not cause divorce.
5
Consider the ikhtilaf on 'برئت من طلاقك'
There is a difference of opinion on this specific phrase when intended. The passage states that the most correct view is that it occurs as ba'in, citing Fath al-Qadir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the principle that any kinaya containing the word 'talaq' results in a raj'i divorce. The cause ('illa) for the three specified phrases being raj'i is the presence of talaq, explicitly or implicitly. By extension, any kinaya that includes the word 'talaq' is also raj'i. The author then lists numerous examples: 'أنا بريء من طلاقك' (I am free from your divorce), 'الطلاق عليك' (divorce upon you), 'وهبتك طلاقك' (I gifted you your divorce), etc. He also notes that phrases like 'لست لي بامرأة' (you are not my wife) follow the same rule, except that if the husband says 'أنا بريء من نكاحك' (I am free from your marriage), no divorce occurs. There is also a discussion regarding 'برئت من طلاقك' if intended; the correct view is that it occurs as ba'in as stated in Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
علة (‘illah): Effective cause or legal reason that links a ruling to its basis.
مقتضى (muqtaḍā): Explicitly stated; the word 'talaq' is mentioned overtly.
مضمر (muḍmar): Implicit; the word 'talaq' is implied but not stated directly.
كناية (kināyah): An indirect expression that can imply divorce but requires intention.

Persian Kinayat in Divorce
Ruling on sarih vs kinaya for Persian divorce words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a Persian divorce word as sarih or kinaya based on its usage in local custom and state the resulting ruling on intention.

Core Idea
A Persian word exclusively used for divorce is sarih; otherwise it is kinaya—intention required.

Divorce pronouncements in languages other than Arabic raise questions of clarity. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq settles the matter for Persian: the key is local custom. If a word is used only for divorce, it is as explicit as 'Anti taliq'. If it has other meanings, it is a kinaya and requires the husband's intention. The same principle applies to any language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word's usage
Determine whether the Persian word is used exclusively for divorce in the local custom (e.g., Khurasan, Iraq) or also for other meanings.
Custom ('urf) is the criterion, not etymology.
2
Classify as sarih or kinaya
If exclusive to divorce, it is sarih. If used for divorce and other things, it is kinaya.
Sarih does not require intention; kinaya does.
3
Apply the ruling
For sarih: divorce occurs as soon as the word is directed at the wife (e.g., 'zan ra ha kardam'). For kinaya: divorce only occurs if the husband intended divorce.
The type of divorce (raj'i or ba'in) follows Arabic kinaya rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المعراج والأصل الذي عليه الفتوى في الطلاق بالفارسية أنه إن كان فيه لفظ لا يستعمل إلا في الطلاق فذلك اللفظ صريح يقع بلا نية إذا أضيف إلى المرأة مثل ز ن ر ها كردم في عرف أهل خراسان والعراق بهيم لأن الصريح لا يختلف باختلاف اللغات، وما كان بالفارسية يستعمل في الطلاق وغيره فهو من كنايات الفارسية فحكمه حكم كنايات العربية في جميع الأحكام ا هـ.
And in al-Mi'raj and the foundation upon which fatwa is given regarding divorce in Persian: if there is a word that is not used except for divorce, then that word is sarih (explicit) and occurs without intention when added to the woman, like 'zan ra ha kardam' in the custom of the people of Khurasan and Iraq, 'bahim', because the sarih does not differ by languages. And what is used in Persian for divorce and other things, then it is of the Persian kinayat, so its ruling is the same as the ruling of Arabic kinayat in all rulings.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage establishes the ruling for Persian divorce words. If a Persian word is used exclusively for divorce (e.g., 'zan ra ha kardam' in certain regions), it is considered sarih (explicit) and divorce occurs without intention, just as with Arabic sarih words. If the word is used for divorce and other meanings, it is a kinaya and follows the same rules as Arabic kinayat: intention is required, and the type of divorce (raj'i or ba'in) is determined accordingly. The key principle is that sarih does not differ by language; only the custom of exclusive usage matters.

Key Terms
صريح (ṣarīḥ): Explicit word for divorce that does not require intention to take effect.
كناية فارسية (kināyah fārisiyyah): Persian word that can be used for divorce and other contexts; requires intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) If it is derived from Arabic.
D) If it is accompanied by intention.

Key Takeaway
A Persian word exclusively used for divorce in local custom is sarih and takes effect without intention; otherwise it is kinaya and requires intention, following Arabic kinaya rules.

Intention for Two or Three in Non-Specified Kinayat
Explanation of matn 'وفي غيرها بائنة' and intention for two or three divorces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on intention for two and three divorces in non-specified kinayat and explain why intention for two is invalid for a free woman.

Core Idea
Two is a pure number, not a type of ba'inunah, so intention for two in kinayat is invalid for a free woman.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, we examine a key rule for kinayat: when a husband uses a non-specified kinaya and intends a number. The ruling hinges on the distinction between numbers that correspond to recognized categories of irrevocability. Understanding this distinction is vital for correctly applying divorce rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Distinguish between free woman and slave woman. For free woman, intention for two is invalid; for slave woman, it is valid.
The text gives the general rule and then the exception.
2
Understand the reason for invalidity
Intention for two is invalid for a free woman because two is a pure number (عدد محض) and not a distinct type of ba'inunah. Ba'inunah is only light (one divorce) or severe (three divorces).
This is the core legal reasoning (talil).
3
Understand why three is valid
Three is the whole genus (كل الجنس) of ba'inunah, so intending three covers the severe type. Hence intention for three is valid.
Contrast with two which is not a recognised category.
4
Apply to the example from al-Muhit
If a man says 'أنت بائن' after one prior divorce and intends two, only one ba'in divorce occurs because severe ba'inunah requires three. But if he intends three, it occurs (adding to the prior one makes three).
This illustrates the practical application.
5
Note exceptions
Intention for three is not valid in four kinayat: the three revocable ones (the specified phrases) and 'اختاري' (choose yourself).
These are excluded because they do not carry the same flexibility of intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وفي غيرها بائنة وإن نوى ثنتين وتصح نيته الثلاث أي في غير الألفاظ الثلاثة تقع واحدة بائنة أو ثلاث بالنية ولا تصح نية الثنيتين في الحرة لما قدمناه أنه عدد محض بخلاف الثلاث لأنه كل الجنس ولأن البينونة متنوعة إلى غليظة وخفيفة فأيهما نوی صحت نيته بخلاف أنت طالق لأنه موضوع شرعاً لإنشاء الواحدة الرجعية فلا يملك العبد تغييره. وفي المحيط : لو طلق منكوحته الحرة واحدة ثم قال لها أنت بائن ونوى ثنتين كانت واحدة لأن البينونة الغليظة لا تحصل بما نوى فلا تصح النية حتى لو نوى الثلاث تقع لأن البينونة في حقها تحصل بالثنتين وبالواحدة السابقة ا.هـ. والثنتان في الأمة كالثلاث في الحرة فلا ترد عليه كما لا يرد عليه اختاري وأمرك بيدك فإنه لا يقع بهما، بل إذا نوى التفويض كان لها التطليق فلا يقع إلا بقولها بعده اخترت نفسي ونحوه، وكما لا يرد عليه «اختاري» فإنه كناية ولا يصح فيه نية الثلاث لما سنذكره في باب التفويض، وبه اندفع اعتراض الشارح عليه. والحاصل أن الكنايات كلها تصح فيها نية الثلاث إلا أربعة : الثلاث الرواجع و «اختاري» كما في الخانية.
His saying: (And in other than these, it is ba'in and if he intends two, his intention for three is valid) meaning that in other than the three phrases, one ba'in divorce occurs or three by intention, and intention for two is not valid for a free woman because we have mentioned that it is a pure number, unlike three because it is the whole genus, and because ba'inunah is divided into severe and light, so whichever he intends, his intention is valid, unlike 'you are divorced' because it is legislated for initiating a single raj'i divorce, so the slave cannot change it. And in al-Muhit: If he divorced his free wife once, then said to her 'you are ba'in' and intended two, it is one because severe ba'inunah does not occur by what he intended, so the intention is not valid; but if he intended three, it occurs because ba'inunah in her case occurs by two and the previous one. And two in a slave woman is like three in a free woman, so it is not an objection, just as 'ikhtari' and 'amruk bi yadik' are not an objection; they do not occur by them. Rather, if he intended delegation, then she has the right to divorce, so it does not occur except by her saying afterward 'I chose myself' or similar. And just as 'ikhtari' is not an objection, it is a kinaya and intention for three is not valid in it as we will mention in the chapter on delegation. And thus the objection of the commentator is rebutted. The conclusion is that in all kinayat, intention for three is valid except four: the three revocable ones and 'ikhtari' as in al-Khaniyya.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage explains the matn statement that in kinayat other than the three specified phrases, the divorce is ba'in. If the husband intends two divorces, his intention for three is valid; but intention for two is invalid for a free woman because two is a pure number (not a type of ba'inunah), while three is the whole genus and thus can be intended. Ba'inunah (irrevocability) is of two types: light (with one or two) and severe (with three). Since two is not a distinct type, intention for it is not valid. The example from al-Muhit clarifies: if a man divorces his wife once and then says 'أنت بائن' intending two, it counts as only one because severe ba'inunah (which requires three) cannot be achieved by intending two. However, if he intends three, it occurs because the previous divorce plus two makes three. For a slave woman, two are equivalent to three for a free woman. The passage also excludes 'اختاري' (choose yourself) and 'أمرك بيدك' (your affair is in your hand) – these are not direct kinayat but delegation; if the husband intends delegation, the wife can divorce herself. In 'اختاري', intention for three is not valid because it only signifies purity/choice, not variety of ba'inunah. The four exceptions where intention for three is not valid are: the three revocable ones (the three specified phrases) and 'اختاري'.

Key Terms
بينونة غليظة (baynūnah ghalīẓah): Severe irrevocability that occurs with three divorces; wife becomes haram until she marries another.
عدد محض (‘adad maḥḍ): Pure number, i.e., the number 'two' is not a recognized category of ba'inunah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because two is a pure number and not a type of ba'inunah.
B) Because the kinaya cannot express multiple divorces.
C) Because the wife is free and cannot be divorced twice.
D) Because intention is not considered in kinayat.

Key Takeaway
In non-specified kinayat, intention for two is invalid for a free woman because two is a pure number, not a type of irrevocability; three is valid as the whole genus.

Lexical Meanings of Divorce Terms: Ba'in, Battah, Batlah, Haram
Lexical explanations of key divorce terms: ba'in, battah, batlah, haram.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the terms ba'in, battah, batlah, and haram as kinayat and state that they require intention to effect a divorce.

Core Idea
Ba'in, battah, batlah, and haram are kinayat—they only effect divorce with intention.

In the Hanafi school, not every word that suggests separation actually counts as an explicit divorce. Certain terms are considered kinayat (allusions) and only bring about divorce if the husband truly intended it. This lesson explores the linguistic roots of four such terms—ba'in, battah, batlah, and haram—and clarifies their legal status as kinayat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its root
For each term, note its Arabic root and basic linguistic meaning as provided by the lexicographers.
E.g., ba'in from bayn (separation), battah from batt (cutting off).
2
Understand the linguistic meaning
How does the root meaning relate to divorce? The term implies separation or cutting off.
This helps distinguish between explicit and allusive expressions.
3
Recognize the term as kinaya
The text states that 'you are ba'in' is a kinaya (allusion) whether stated conditionally or immediately.
Kinaya requires intention for legal effect.
4
Note the role of intention
Without intention, no divorce occurs. Even if the condition is met, the utterance alone does not suffice.
The source cites Ibn Wahban: if separation is conditioned without intention, no divorce upon fulfillment.
5
Distinguish from sarih
Explicit divorce terms (like 'talaq') do not need intention; these kinayat do.
This distinction is crucial in fatwa and court rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وهي بائن من باب بان الشيء إذا انفصل فهو بائن، وأبنته بالألف فصلته، وبانت المرأة بالطلاق فهي بائن بغير هاء، وأبانها زوجها بالألف فهي مبانة. قال ابن السكيت في كتاب التوسعة تطليقة بائنة والمعنى مبانة. قال الصغاني رحمه الله : فاعلة بمعنى مفعولة كذا في المصباح وفي منظومة ابن وهبان ما حاصله أنه لو علق بالشرط إبانة بلا نية طلاق لم يقع إذا وجد شرطه ا هـ . فأنت بائن كناية معلقاً كان أو منجزاً قوله : (بتة) من بته بتاً من باب ضرب وقتل قطعه وفي المطاوع فانبت كما يقال فانقطع ، وبت الرجل طلاق امرأته فهي مبتوتة والأصل مبتوت طلاقها وطلقها طلقة بتة وثلاثاً بتة إذا قطعها من الرجعة وأبت طلاقها بالألف لغة قال الأزهري : ويستعمل الثلاثي والرباعي لازمين ومتعديين فيقال بت طلاقها وأبته وطلاق بات وبت. كذا في المصباح. قوله : ( بتلة) من بتله بتلا من باب قتل قطعه وأبانه وطلقها طلقة بتة بتلة. كذا في المصباح قوله : (حرام) من حرم الشيء بالضم حرماً وحرماً وحراماً، امتنع فعله والممنوع
His saying: (And she is ba'in) from the root 'bān' meaning the thing separated, so it is ba'in; and 'abantu' with alif means I separated it. And the woman separated by divorce is ba'in without the feminine 'ha', and her husband separated her with alif so she is mubanah. Ibn as-Sikkit said in at-Tawsi'ah: a ba'in divorce, meaning mubanah. As-Saghani said: fa'ilah in the sense of maf'ulah, as in al-Misbah. And in the poem of Ibn Wahban, its summary: if he conditioned separation without intention of divorce, it does not occur when the condition is met. So 'you are ba'in' is a kinaya, whether conditional or immediate. His saying: (battah) from 'battahu battan' from the pattern of daraba and qatala meaning he cut it, and the intransitive is 'inbatta' as is said 'inqata'a'. And a man batted his wife's divorce, so she is mabtutah, and the original is mabtut talaquha. And he divorced her with a battah divorce and three battah if he cut her from return. And 'abatta' with alif is a language. Al-Azhari said: the triliteral and quadriliteral are used both intransitively and transitively. It is said: 'batta talaquha' and 'abattahu' and talaq bat and batt. As in al-Misbah. His saying: (batlah) from 'batluhu batlan' from the pattern of qatala meaning he cut it off and separated it, and he divorced her with a battah batlah divorce. As in al-Misbah. His saying: (haram) from 'harima as-shay'u' with damm meaning it became forbidden, and the prohibited is mahrum.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage is a lexical commentary explaining the meanings of the terms used in divorce: 'بائن' (ba'in), 'بتة' (battah), 'بتلة' (batlah), and 'حرام' (haram). It explores their linguistic roots in Arabic. 'بائن' comes from the root 'بين' meaning separation; it is used as a fa'ilah form meaning maf'ulah (passive). 'بتة' means cutting off, used for a divorce that severs the possibility of return. 'بتلة' similarly means cutting off and separation. 'حرام' means forbidden, indicating that intercourse becomes prohibited. The commentary also notes that 'أنت بائن' is a kinaya, whether stated conditionally or immediately, and references the view that if separation is conditioned without intention of divorce, no divorce occurs upon fulfillment of the condition.

Key Terms
بائن (bā’in): Linguistically 'separated', in legal terms an irrevocable divorce.
بتة (battah): Linguistically 'cutting off', a divorce that severs the marriage completely.
بتلة (batlah): Linguistically 'cutting off', synonymous with battah.
حرام (ḥarām): Linguistically 'forbidden', used as a kinaya indicating divorce prohibits marital relations.

Key Takeaway
Ba'in, battah, batlah, and haram are kinayat in Hanafi law; they only effect a divorce when accompanied by the husband's intention.

The Sarih vs Kinaya Dispute: Are Phrases Containing 'Talaq' Explicit or Implicit?
Response to objection about sarih/kinaya classification of divorce phrases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school classifies phrases containing the word 'talaq' as kinaya rather than sarih, and evaluate the opposing view.

Core Idea
If a phrase were sarih, it would not need intention; since the author requires intention, it is kinaya.

This passage addresses a sharp scholarly dispute: are divorce utterances like 'anti taliq' explicit (sarih) or implicit (kinaya)? The Hanafi school holds that they are kinaya, meaning divorce only occurs with intention. An opposing view, noted in Hashiyat Miskin, claims they are sarih. We will unpack the reasoning and see why the Hanafi position is relied upon.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: these phrases are kinayat, not sarih. Divorce only occurs if the husband intended it.
2
Identify the opposing view
Some scholars (e.g., in Hashiyat Miskin) argue that because the word 'talaq' itself is used, the phrase is sarih and divorce occurs without intention.
3
Follow the opposing proof
The opposing argument relies on the apparent linguistic explicitness of the term 'talaq'.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
If the phrase were sarih, it would not need intention by consensus (ijma). But the author's arrangement implies intention is required, confirming these are kinayat.
The response uses a reductio ad absurdum: if sarih, no intention needed; intention is needed, so not sarih.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: if sarih, divorce occurs upon utterance regardless of intention; if kinaya, divorce only with intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وما في معناها أي مما قريباً وهو جواب عما أورد على المصنف أن كون ما عدا الثلاث يقع به بائن ممنوع بل يقع الرجعي ببعض الكنايات سوى الثلاث، وفي حاشية مسكين: إن مبنى الإيراد على أن ما سبق من هذه الألفاظ من قسم الكناية والذي يظهر خلافه وأنها من الصريح وقد كنت توقفت في ذلك برهة حتى رأيت بخط الحموي الموافقة عليه ا هـ. وفيه نظر لأنها لو كانت من الصريح لما احتاجت إلى نية وقد تقدم في باب الصريح أنه لا يتوقف على نية بإجماع الفقهاء ومقتضى كلام المؤلف من كون ما سبق داخلاً في كلام المصنف توقفها عليها قوله: (وكما لا يرد عليه اختاري أي بدون الجمع بينه وبين الأمر باليد وقوله لما سنذكره أي عند قول المصنف «ولم تصح نيه الثلاث لأنه إنما يفيد الحلوص والصفا فهو غير متنوع والبينونة تثبت فيه مقتضى فلا تعم بخلاف أنت بائن ونحوه لتنوع البينونة إلى غليظة وخفيفة ا.هـ. وفي هذ الجواب نظر وكلام النهر يقتضي أن النسخة لما سيذكر بالياء أي المصنف والمعنى أنه أطلق هنا والمراد ما عدا اختاري اعتماداً
His saying: (and what is in its meaning) i.e., from what is close, and it is a response to what was objected to the author: that the ruling that other than the three occur as ba'in is denied; rather, raj'i occurs by some kinayat besides the three. In the hashiya of Miskin: the basis of the objection is that the aforementioned phrases are from the category of kinaya, but what appears is the opposite, that they are sarih, and I hesitated in that for a while until I saw in the handwriting of al-Hamawi agreement with it. But it is questionable because if they were sarih, they would not need intention, and it has preceded in the chapter on sarih that it does not depend on intention by consensus of the jurists, and the implication of the author's speech is that what preceded is included in the author's words and depends on it. His saying: (and just as 'ikhtari' is not an objection) i.e., without combining it with the command of the hand, and his saying: 'as we will mention' i.e., at the statement of the author: 'and intention for three is not valid because it only signifies purity and clarity, so it is not varied, and ba'inunah is established in it as a requirement, so it does not generalize', unlike 'you are ba'in' and similar because of the variety of ba'inunah into severe and light. In this answer there is consideration, and the statement of an-Nahr indicates that the version is 'as he will mention' with the ya, i.e., the author, and the meaning is that he was absolute here but the intent is other than 'ikhtari', relying on...
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection raised against the author (al-Marghinani) regarding the ruling that kinayat other than the three specified produce ba'in divorce. The objector claims that some of the listed kinayat (containing 'talaq') should produce raj'i, not ba'in, based on earlier principles. In the hashiya of Miskin, it is noted that some scholars considered these phrases to be sarih rather than kinaya, which would change the ruling. However, the commentary refutes this: if they were sarih, they would not require intention, but the author's arrangement implies they do. The passage also discusses 'اختاري' (choose yourself), stating that it is not an objection to the rule because it does not produce divorce directly; it is a delegation. The response includes the reasoning that 'اختاري' only signifies purity and clarity, not variety, so intention for three is not valid in it, unlike 'أنت بائن' where intention for three can be valid due to the variety of ba'inunah. The text ends with a note about the version of the text and a reference to an-Nahr, indicating that the intended meaning is to exclude 'اختاري' from the general rule.

Key Terms
صريح (ṣarīḥ): Explicit wording; a divorce term that does not require intention to take effect.
كناية (kināyah): Implicit or ambiguous wording; divorce occurs only if intention is present.
حاشية (ḥāshiyah): A super-commentary or marginal note, here referring to Miskin's work.
الإيراد (al-īrād): An objection or criticism raised against a legal ruling.

Key Takeaway
Phrases containing the word 'talaq' are kinayat in the Hanafi school, requiring intention for divorce to be effective.

The Hanafi Ruling on 'Haram' as a Kinayah for Divorce
Ruling on the kinyā word 'haram' and its derivatives for divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when the word 'haram' effects a ba'in divorce and identify the roles of intention and custom.

Core Idea
A husband's statement 'you are haram upon me' causes a ba'in divorce only if intention or local custom so indicate.

In the Hanafi school, the word 'haram' is classified as a kinayah (allusive expression) for divorce. Unlike explicit words like 'talaq', its effectiveness depends on the speaker's intention or the customary usage of the community. This lesson explores the ruling and its nuances from Al-Bahr al-Raiq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression as kinayah
Recognize that 'haram' is not a sarih word for divorce; it requires additional factors such as intention or custom to effect divorce.
Sarih words like 'talaq' are automatic; kinayah are conditional.
2
Determine the role of intention
If the husband intends divorce when uttering the phrase, a divorce occurs. However, intention may be presumed by local custom.
The text mentions Imam Zahir al-Din's view: we do not deny the need for intention but deem it present by custom.
3
Apply the custom test
In communities where such phrases are customarily understood as divorce, intention is deemed present even if the husband denies it in court.
The ruling changes based on 'urf (custom).
4
Classify the type of divorce
When the divorce takes effect, it is ba'in (irrevocable), not raj'i, because customary usage dictates that these phrases are used for final separation.
The hypothetical objection assumes it should be raj'i like sarih, but custom overrides.
5
Distinguish qada and diyanah
In court (qada), the ruling follows custom; in private conscience (diyanah), the husband's actual intention matters.
This distinction appears at the end of the passage from Al-Qunyah.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يسمى حر الطلاق، هي حرام كالماء حراماً تسمية بالمصدر وسيأتي في آخر باب الإيلاء عن الفتاوى أنه لو قال لها أنت علي حرام والحرام عنده طلاق وقع. وإن لم ينو وذكر الإمام ظهير الدين لا نقول لا تشترط النية ولكن نجعله ناوياً عرفاً ولا فرق بين قوله أنت علي حرام أو محرمة علي أو حرمتك علي أو لم يقل «علي» أو أنت حرام بدون «علي» أو أنا عليك حرام أو محرم أو حرمت نفسي عليك . ويشترط قوله عليك» في تحريم نفسه لا نفسها، وكذا قوله حلال المسلمين علي حرام، وكل حل علي حرام وأنت معي في الحرام، فإن قلت: إذا وقع الطلاق بلا نية ينبغي أن يكون كالصريح فيكون الواقع رجعياً قلت : المتعارف به إيقاع البائن لا الرجعي وإن قال لم أنو لم يصدق في موضع صار متعارفاً. كذا في البزازية وسيأتي تمامه في الإيلاء. وفي القنية : لو قال أنت امرأة حرام ولم يرد الطلاق يقع قضاء وديانة، ولو قال تحرم لأنه تشبيه بالسرعة
It is called the prohibition of divorce. 'She is forbidden' like 'water is forbidden' as a verbal noun. It will come in the last chapter of Ila' from al-Fatawa that if he said to her 'You are forbidden upon me' and in his view forbidden means divorce, it occurs even if he did not intend. And Imam Zahir al-Din said: We do not say that intention is not required, but we make him as intending by custom. And there is no difference between his saying 'You are forbidden upon me' or 'prohibited upon me' or 'I have made you forbidden upon me' or he did not say 'upon me', or 'you are forbidden' without 'upon me', or 'I am forbidden upon you' or 'prohibited' or 'I have made myself forbidden upon you'. And the condition of saying 'upon you' is in making himself forbidden, not her. And similarly his saying 'the halal of Muslims upon me is haram', and 'every halal upon me is haram', and 'you are with me in haram'. If you say: when divorce occurs without intention, it should be like explicit (sarih) and thus the occurring divorce would be raj'i. I say: The customary usage is to effect ba'in (irrevocable) not raj'i. And if he said 'I did not intend', he is not believed in a place where it has become customary. This is in al-Bazzaziyyah, and its completion will come in Ila'. And in al-Qunyah: If he said 'You are a forbidden woman' and did not intend divorce, it occurs both in qada and diyanah. And if he said 'you are haram' because it is like a simile for hardship.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage discusses the kinyā word 'haram' and its derivatives. The author establishes that such expressions are kinayah (allusive) for divorce. The ruling is that if the husband uses such a phrase with the intention of divorce, or in a community where it is customarily understood to mean divorce, then a ba'in (irrevocable) divorce occurs. The text clarifies that no explicit intention is required if the phrase is conventionally used for divorce. The Hanafi reasoning is based on 'urf (custom). A hypothetical objection is raised: if divorce occurs without intention, should it not be like sarih (explicit) and thus raj'i? The response is that the customary usage of such phrases is to effect a ba'in divorce, not raj'i. The passage also notes that the wife's saying something similar does not have the same effect. Key distinctions are made between qada (court ruling) and diyanah (in private conscience).

Key Terms
حرام (haram): In this context, a kinyā word for divorce meaning 'forbidden upon me', used as an allusive expression for talaq.
بائن (ba'in): An irrevocable divorce where the husband cannot take back his wife without a new marriage contract.
كناية (kinayah): An allusive or indirect expression that requires intention (niyyah) to effect divorce, unlike explicit words (sarih).

Four Kinyā Words for Divorce: Khaliyyah, Bari'ah, Habluki 'ala Gharibik, alhaqi bi-ahlik
Definitions and rulings of four specific kinyā words for divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define each of the four kinyā words for divorce and explain their legal effect under Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
These four kinyā phrases require intention or custom to effect divorce; their literal meanings metaphorically indicate separation.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the linguistic origins and legal usage of four specific kinyā words used in Hanafi divorce law. Understanding these terms helps you distinguish between explicit (sarih) and implicit (kinyā) expressions of divorce. The key takeaway is that every kinyā word requires either the husband's intention (niyyah) or customary usage ('urf) to produce a legal effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify each kinyā word
Read the passage and list the four terms: khaliyyah, bari'ah, habluki 'ala gharibik, alhaqi bi-ahlik. Note their literal meanings as described: 'empty', 'free/innocent', 'your rope on your hump', 'join your family'.
2
Understand the metaphor
For each term, see how the literal meaning is extended to imply divorce. Khaliyyah originally describes a camel freed from its hobble; bari'ah can mean free from the marriage tie; habluki 'ala gharibik is a direct metaphor for releasing a camel; alhaqi bi-ahlik suggests returning to her family, equivalent to separation.
The camel metaphor is particularly vivid in pre-Islamic Arab culture.
3
Determine the legal category
Recognize that all four words are kinyā (implicit) expressions for divorce, not sarih (explicit). In Hanafi law, sarih words like 'anti taliq' effect divorce immediately; kinyā words require additional conditions.
The passage explicitly calls them kinyā.
4
Apply the condition for validity
For a kinyā expression to effect a valid divorce, the husband must either intend divorce at the time of utterance, or the phrase must be customarily ('urf) used for divorce in that society. Without either, no divorce occurs.
The passage illustrates this with the case from al-Qunyah where the husband intended divorce and it occurred.
5
Review variations and ambiguities
Note that bari'ah has multiple possible meanings: free from evil, free from good, or free from marriage. Alhaqi bi-ahlik has a pronunciation variant (hamzat al-wasl vs. hamzat al-qat'). These nuances affect how the kinyā is interpreted but do not change the basic legal rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (خلية) من خلت المرأة من مانع النكاح خلواً فهي خلية، ونساء خليات، وناقة خلية مطلقة من عقالها فهي ترعى حيث شاءت ومنه يقال في كنايات خلية . كذا هي في المصباح قوله : ( بريئة) يحتمل النسبة إلى الشر أي بريئة من حسن الخلق وأفعال المسلمين وإلى الخير أي عن الدنيا أو عن البهتان ويحتمل أن أنت بريئة عن النكاح. وفي الكافي : بريئة من البراءة ولهذا وجب همزها قوله : (حبلك على غاربك) تمثيل ذات لأنه تشبيه بالصورة المنتزعة من أشياء وهي هيئة الناقة إذا أريد إطلاقها ترعى وهي وألقى الحبل على غاربها وهو ما بين السنام والعنق كيلا تتعقل به إذا كان مطروحاً فشبه بهذه الهيئة إلا طلاقية انطلاق المرأة من قيد النكاح أو العمل والتصرف. كذا في فتح القدير. وفي المصباح : إنه استعير للمرأة وجعل كناية عن طلاقها أي اذهبي حيث شئت كما يذهب البعير. وفي النوادر : الغارب اعلا كل شيء والجمع الغوارب قوله : (الحقي بأهلك) بهمز وصل كما في فتح القدير يعني فتكسر الهمزة وتفتح الحاء من لحقته ولحقت به من باب لحاقاً تعب بالفتح أدركته. وفي المصباح وألحقته بالألف مثله فعلى هذا لا تتعين الهمزة للوصل فيجوز أن تكون للقطع مع كسر الحاء من باب الأفعال وفي غاية البيان: والحقي من الإلحاق وانتقلي وانطلقي كالحقي. وفي القنية : قالت لزوجها تغير لوني من اللحوق لا فقال الزوج رددتك بهذا العيب ونوى الطلاق يقع
His saying: (Khaliyyah) from khala al-mar'atu (the woman became empty) of the impediment of marriage, so she is khaliyyah (empty), and women are khaliyyat (empty), and a she-camel is khaliyyah (free) from her hobble, so she grazes wherever she wishes, and from this it is said in kinyā khaliyyah. Thus it is in al-Misbah. His saying: (Bari'ah) it is possible it denotes evil, i.e., free from good character and the actions of Muslims, or good, i.e., from the world or from slander, and it is possible that you are free from the marriage tie. In al-Kafi: Bari'ah is from bara'ah (innocence), and for this reason its hamza is obligatory. His saying: (Hablu ki 'ala gharibik) is a representation of a self because it is a simile of a form extracted from things, and it is the condition of a she-camel when one wants to let her loose to graze, and he throws the rope onto her gharib (the part between the hump and the neck) so that she does not become entangled with it when it is thrown; so she is likened to this condition except for the talaqiyyah (divorce) meaning the release of a woman from the bond of marriage or from work and disposal. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Misbah: It is metaphorically used for a woman and made a kinyā for her divorce, i.e., go wherever you wish as the camel goes. In al-Nawadir: al-gharib is the top of everything, the plural is ghawarib. His saying: (alhaqi bi-ahlik) with a connecting hamzah as in Fath al-Qadir, meaning the hamzah is broken and the ha' is opened from lahiqtuhu and lahiqtu bihi from the verb form lahaqa meaning fatigue? [؟] t, I caught up. In al-Misbah: I joined him with alif similarly. Based on this, the hamzah is not necessarily for connection; it is permissible for it to be for cutting with kasr of the ha' from the form of verbs. In Ghayat al-Bayan: alhaqi is from ilhaq, meaning move and depart like alhaqi. In al-Qunyah: A woman said to her husband: My color changed because of the joining? [؟] No. The husband said: I return you due to this defect, and he intended divorce; it occurs.

Commentary
This passage provides definitions and rulings for four specific kinyā words used for divorce. The author explains each word's linguistic origin and how it becomes a kinyā for talaq. 'Khaliyyah' (empty) originally describes a woman free from marriage impediments or a she-camel freed from its hobble; used for divorce, it implies the woman is free from the marriage. 'Bari'ah' (free/innocent) can have various connotations but may also mean free from the marriage tie. 'Habluki 'ala gharibik' (your rope on your hump) is a metaphor likening the woman to a camel let loose to graze freely, symbolizing her freedom from the marriage. 'alhaqi bi-ahlik' (join your family) means to return to her family, implying separation. The discussion includes variations in pronunciation and a case from al-Qunyah where a husband says 'I return you due to this defect' with intention of divorce, which is effective. Each word is a kinyā and requires intention or customary usage to effect divorce.

Key Terms
خلية (khaliyyah): A woman who is empty of marriage impediments; used as kinyā for divorce meaning she is free from marriage.
بريئة (bari'ah): Free or innocent; used as kinyā for divorce with possible interpretations.
حبلك على غاربك (habluki 'ala gharibik): Your rope is on your hump; a metaphor for releasing a camel, used as kinyā for divorce.
الحقي بأهلك (alhaqi bi-ahlik): Join your family; used as kinyā for divorce implying return to her family.

General Rule for Kinyā Divorce Words: Intention Required in Court
General ruling that kinyā words without intention are not effective in court

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that kinyā divorce words require intention for effect in court (qada) and distinguish it from the diyanah ruling.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, kinyā divorce words are ineffective in court without intention, but valid in conscience if intended.

This passage introduces the foundational Hanafi principle that allusive expressions (kinayat) of divorce do not effect a divorce in court unless the husband had the intention (niyyah) to divorce. However, in the realm of conscience (diyanah), the ruling follows his actual intention. This lesson will guide you through the general rule and its application when the wife initiates divorce talk.

Guided Path
1
Identify the list of kinyā words
The passage lists several words: wahabt, la battala, haram, khaliyyah, bari'ah, habluki 'ala gharibik, alhaqi bi-ahlik, wahabtuki li-ahlik, sarrahtuki, faraqtuki. These are allusive expressions for divorce.
These are not exhaustive; they are examples.
2
State the general rule
If the husband has no intention of divorce when uttering these words, they do not effect a divorce in court (qada).
The rule applies only in the context of court proceedings.
3
Understand the diyanah dimension
Between the husband and Allah, the divorce is valid if he intended it, even if not proven in court. If the wife then divorced herself in the same session, it is valid; otherwise, she remains his wife.
This is a key distinction between legal and moral accountability.
4
Consider the wife-initiated scenario
If the wife asks for her divorce (e.g., 'Hib li talaqi') and the husband responds with a kinyā expression, there is a difference of opinion. Some say it occurs only with intention; others say it occurs automatically because it is a response to her request. The passage leans toward requiring intention.
This shows the complexity of intention in interactive contexts.
5
Apply the rule to the husband's claim
If the husband claims he intended the divorce to be in the wife's hand (i.e., she has the power to divorce herself), he is not believed in court, but it is considered in diyanah.
This protects the wife's rights and prevents manipulation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهبت لا بتلة حرام خلية بريئة حبلك على غاربك الحقي بأهلك وهبتك لأهلك سرحتك فارقتك إذا لم تكن له نية تطلق في القضاء ولو قال نويت أن يكون في يدها لا يصدق، وأما فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى فهو كما نوى فإن طلقت نفسها في ذلك المجلس طلقت وإلا فهي زوجته. هذا إذا ابتدأ الزوج، فلو ابتدأت فقالت هب طلاقي تريد اعرض عنه فقال يقع وإن نوى لأنه جوابها فيما طلبت كذا قيل وفيه نظر، بل يجب أن يقع إذا نوى لأنه لو ابتدأ به ونوى يقع فإذا نوى الطلاق فقد قصد عدم الجواب وأخرج الكلام ابتداء وله ذلك وهو أدرى بنفسه ونيته. وفي البزازية الحقي برفقتك يقع إذا نوى قوله : (وهبتك لأهلك) يحتمل البينونة لأن الهبة تقتضي زوال الملك . أطلقه فشمل ما إذا لم يقبلوها لأن القبول لا يحتاج إليه لإزالة الملك، كذا في المحيط والتحقيق أنه مجاز عن رددتك إليهم فتصير إلى الحالة الأولى وهي البينونة كالحقي بأهلك، ومثله وهبتك لأبيك أو لابنك أو للأزواج لأنها ترد إلى هؤلاء بالطلاق عادة، وخرج عنه ما لو قال وهبتك للأجانب فإنه ليس بكناية، والأخ والأخت والعمة والخالة من الأجانب هنا فلا يقع وإن نوى كما في المعراج لأنها لا ترد إليهم بالطلاق عادة، وخرج عنه ما لو قال وهبتك بعض طلاقك فإنه ليس بكناية، وقدمنا أنه لو قال وهبت لك طلاقك فإنه يقع في القضاء بلا نية ولا يصدق أنه أراد كونه في يدها إلا إذا وقع جواباً لقولها هب لي طلاقي فإنه لا يقع وإن نوى وفي المعراج : لو قال أبحتك طلاقك لا يقع وإن نوى. وفي الذخيرة وهبت نفسك منك يقع إذا نوى.
Wahabt (I gave), la battala (not irrevocably?), haram, khaliyyah, bari'ah, habluki 'ala gharibik, alhaqi bi-ahlik, wahabtuki li-ahlik, sarrahtuki, faraqtuki - if he has no intention, it does not cause divorce in court. And if he said 'I intended that it be in her hand', he is not believed. However, between him and Allah, it is as he intended. Then if she divorced herself in that session, she is divorced; otherwise she is his wife. This is if the husband initiated. But if the wife initiated and said 'grant me my divorce' meaning 'withdraw', and he said 'it occurs', it occurs even if he intended? [؟] Because it is a response to her request. Thus it was said, and there is consideration. Rather, it must occur if he intended, because if he had initiated it and intended, it would occur. So if he intended divorce, he intended not to respond and produced the speech as initiation, and he has that right, and he knows his own intention better. In al-Bazzaziyyah: 'alhaqi bi-raftiqik' (join your companion) occurs if he intended. His saying: (Wahabtuki li-ahlik) implies ba'in (irrevocability) because gift (hibah) entails transfer of ownership. His expression is absolute and includes the case where they do not accept it, because acceptance is not needed to transfer ownership. Thus in al-Muhit. The verification is that it is a metaphor for 'I have returned you to them', so you return to the first state, which is ba'in, like 'alhaqi bi-ahlik'. Similarly, 'I have given you to your father' or 'to your son' or 'to husbands', because she is customarily returned to these through divorce. It excludes if he said 'I have given you to strangers', which is not kinyā, and brother, sister, paternal aunt, maternal aunt are among strangers here, so it does not occur even if he intended, as in al-Mi'raj, because she is not customarily returned to them by divorce. It excludes if he said 'I have given you some of your divorce', which is not kinyā. And we have previously mentioned that if he said 'I have given you your divorce', it occurs in court without intention, and he is not believed that he intended it to be in her hand, except when it occurs as a response to her saying 'grant me my divorce', then it does not occur even if he intended. In al-Mi'raj: if he said 'I have made your divorce permissible for you', it does not occur even if he intended. In al-Dhakhirah: 'I have given yourself to you' occurs if he intended.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage first provides a general rule: for the listed kinyā words, if the husband has no intention of divorce, the divorce is not effective in court (qada). However, in private conscience (diyanah), it is as he intended. Furthermore, if the wife initiates by asking for divorce and the husband responds with a kinyā expression, the ruling may differ. The specific word 'wahabtuki li-ahlik' (I have given you to your family) is analyzed. It is kinyā for ba'in divorce because gift (hibah) implies transfer of ownership, and by analogy, the wife is returned to her family. The rule is absolute: it applies even if the family does not accept, because acceptance is not required. The kinyā applies only if she is given to family or equivalent persons (father, son, husbands) because customarily she would return to them through divorce. Giving her to strangers (like brother, sister, aunt) is not a kinyā because custom does not associate divorce with return to them. Also, saying 'I have given you some of your divorce' is not kinyā. There is a distinction with 'I have given you your divorce' which is explicit in court without intention, except when it is a direct response to her request for her divorce, in which case it is not effective. The passage concludes with related rulings from various sources.

Key Terms
هبة (hibah): Gift; the act of transferring ownership without compensation.
بينونة (baynunah): Irrevocable separation; the state of being ba'in.
كناية (kinayah): An allusive expression that requires intention to effect divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) No, because intention is required for kinyā words.
C) Yes, if the wife accepts.
D) No, because the word is not a kinyā.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, kinyā (allusive) divorce words require the husband's intention to be effective in court, but in conscience the ruling follows his actual intention.

Specific Ruling on 'Wahabtuki li-ahlik': Kinyā for Ba'in Divorce
Specific analysis of 'wahabtuki li-ahlik' as kinyā for ba'in divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that 'wahabtuki li-ahlik' is kinyā for ba'in divorce, and identify when it is not considered kinyā (e.g., when directed to strangers).

Core Idea
Saying 'I have given you to your family' is kinyā for ba'in divorce, but only if the recipient is a person to whom she is customarily returned through divorce.

The specific phrase 'wahabtuki li-ahlik' (I have given you to your family) is analyzed in detail. The Hanafi school treats it as a kinyā for irrevocable divorce (ba'in) because of the analogy to gift (hibah) which transfers ownership. However, this ruling is limited: it applies only if the phrase refers to family members or equivalents, not strangers. This lesson unpacks the conditions and exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Isolate the phrase and understand its structure
The phrase 'wahabtuki li-ahlik' uses the verb 'wahabtuki' (I gave you) and 'li-ahlik' (to your family). Recognize that this is a kinyā because it alludes to divorce through the concept of gift.
The verb 'wahabt' is also listed among the general kinyā words in the previous lesson.
2
Analyze the analogy to gift (hibah)
The passage states: 'because gift (hibah) entails transfer of ownership.' The husband metaphorically transfers the wife's ownership to her family, which is equivalent to divorce. The divorce is ba'in (irrevocable) because the transfer is absolute.
This is a distinctive Hanafi reasoning based on the nature of hibah.
3
Determine the type of divorce
The passage says it 'implies ba'in' because it returns the wife to her original state (before marriage). It is like 'alhaqi bi-ahlik' which is also ba'in.
Ba'in divorce means the husband cannot revoke the divorce without a new marriage contract.
4
Identify the scope of the kinyā
The kinyā applies when the phrase is directed to family members: father, son, husband (i.e., the wife's husband? Actually 'li-ahlik' means to her family, and similar phrases like 'to your father' or 'to your son' or 'to husbands'). It does NOT apply when directed to strangers, like brother, sister, paternal aunt, maternal aunt, because customarily she is not returned to them through divorce.
The criterion is 'customary return' ('urfi).
5
Distinguish from other phrases
Passage distinguishes 'wahabtuki li-ahlik' from 'wahabtuki ba‘da talaqik' (I have given you some of your divorce), which is not kinyā. Also 'wahabtuki talaqik' (I have given you your divorce) is sarih (explicit) and does not require intention, except in certain contexts.
This shows the precision needed in classifying divorce phrases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهبت لا بتلة حرام خلية بريئة حبلك على غاربك الحقي بأهلك وهبتك لأهلك سرحتك فارقتك إذا لم تكن له نية تطلق في القضاء ولو قال نويت أن يكون في يدها لا يصدق، وأما فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى فهو كما نوى فإن طلقت نفسها في ذلك المجلس طلقت وإلا فهي زوجته. هذا إذا ابتدأ الزوج، فلو ابتدأت فقالت هب طلاقي تريد اعرض عنه فقال يقع وإن نوى لأنه جوابها فيما طلبت كذا قيل وفيه نظر، بل يجب أن يقع إذا نوى لأنه لو ابتدأ به ونوى يقع فإذا نوى الطلاق فقد قصد عدم الجواب وأخرج الكلام ابتداء وله ذلك وهو أدرى بنفسه ونيته. وفي البزازية الحقي برفقتك يقع إذا نوى قوله : (وهبتك لأهلك) يحتمل البينونة لأن الهبة تقتضي زوال الملك . أطلقه فشمل ما إذا لم يقبلوها لأن القبول لا يحتاج إليه لإزالة الملك، كذا في المحيط والتحقيق أنه مجاز عن رددتك إليهم فتصير إلى الحالة الأولى وهي البينونة كالحقي بأهلك، ومثله وهبتك لأبيك أو لابنك أو للأزواج لأنها ترد إلى هؤلاء بالطلاق عادة، وخرج عنه ما لو قال وهبتك للأجانب فإنه ليس بكناية، والأخ والأخت والعمة والخالة من الأجانب هنا فلا يقع وإن نوى كما في المعراج لأنها لا ترد إليهم بالطلاق عادة، وخرج عنه ما لو قال وهبتك بعض طلاقك فإنه ليس بكناية، وقدمنا أنه لو قال وهبت لك طلاقك فإنه يقع في القضاء بلا نية ولا يصدق أنه أراد كونه في يدها إلا إذا وقع جواباً لقولها هب لي طلاقي فإنه لا يقع وإن نوى وفي المعراج : لو قال أبحتك طلاقك لا يقع وإن نوى. وفي الذخيرة وهبت نفسك منك يقع إذا نوى.
Wahabt (I gave), la battala (not irrevocably?), haram, khaliyyah, bari'ah, habluki 'ala gharibik, alhaqi bi-ahlik, wahabtuki li-ahlik, sarrahtuki, faraqtuki - if he has no intention, it does not cause divorce in court. And if he said 'I intended that it be in her hand', he is not believed. However, between him and Allah, it is as he intended. Then if she divorced herself in that session, she is divorced; otherwise she is his wife. This is if the husband initiated. But if the wife initiated and said 'grant me my divorce' meaning 'withdraw', and he said 'it occurs', it occurs even if he intended? [؟] Because it is a response to her request. Thus it was said, and there is consideration. Rather, it must occur if he intended, because if he had initiated it and intended, it would occur. So if he intended divorce, he intended not to respond and produced the speech as initiation, and he has that right, and he knows his own intention better. In al-Bazzaziyyah: 'alhaqi bi-raftiqik' (join your companion) occurs if he intended. His saying: (Wahabtuki li-ahlik) implies ba'in (irrevocability) because gift (hibah) entails transfer of ownership. His expression is absolute and includes the case where they do not accept it, because acceptance is not needed to transfer ownership. Thus in al-Muhit. The verification is that it is a metaphor for 'I have returned you to them', so you return to the first state, which is ba'in, like 'alhaqi bi-ahlik'. Similarly, 'I have given you to your father' or 'to your son' or 'to husbands', because she is customarily returned to these through divorce. It excludes if he said 'I have given you to strangers', which is not kinyā, and brother, sister, paternal aunt, maternal aunt are among strangers here, so it does not occur even if he intended, as in al-Mi'raj, because she is not customarily returned to them by divorce. It excludes if he said 'I have given you some of your divorce', which is not kinyā. And we have previously mentioned that if he said 'I have given you your divorce', it occurs in court without intention, and he is not believed that he intended it to be in her hand, except when it occurs as a response to her saying 'grant me my divorce', then it does not occur even if he intended. In al-Mi'raj: if he said 'I have made your divorce permissible for you', it does not occur even if he intended. In al-Dhakhirah: 'I have given yourself to you' occurs if he intended.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage first provides a general rule: for the listed kinyā words, if the husband has no intention of divorce, the divorce is not effective in court (qada). However, in private conscience (diyanah), it is as he intended. Furthermore, if the wife initiates by asking for divorce and the husband responds with a kinyā expression, the ruling may differ. The specific word 'wahabtuki li-ahlik' (I have given you to your family) is analyzed. It is kinyā for ba'in divorce because gift (hibah) implies transfer of ownership, and by analogy, the wife is returned to her family. The rule is absolute: it applies even if the family does not accept, because acceptance is not required. The kinyā applies only if she is given to family or equivalent persons (father, son, husbands) because customarily she would return to them through divorce. Giving her to strangers (like brother, sister, aunt) is not a kinyā because custom does not associate divorce with return to them. Also, saying 'I have given you some of your divorce' is not kinyā. There is a distinction with 'I have given you your divorce' which is explicit in court without intention, except when it is a direct response to her request for her divorce, in which case it is not effective. The passage concludes with related rulings from various sources.

Key Terms
هبة (hibah): Gift; the act of transferring ownership without compensation.
بينونة (baynunah): Irrevocable separation; the state of being ba'in.
كناية (kinayah): An allusive expression that requires intention to effect divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says 'Wahabtuki li-ahlik' to his wife intending divorce. The wife's family does not accept the 'gift.' According to the passage, does an irrevocable divorce occur?
A) Yes, because the phrase is kinyā regardless of acceptance.
B) No, because acceptance is required for hibah.
C) Yes, only if the family accepts.
D) No, because the wife is not returned to them.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on 'wahabtuki li-ahlik' as kinyā for ba'in divorce depends on the recipient being a person to whom a woman is customarily returned through divorce, with intention required for effect in court.

Are 'Sarah'tuki' and 'Faraqtuki' Explicit or Allusive? – Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Hanafi argument that 'sarah'tuki' and 'faraqtuki' are kinayah, contra Shafi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school classifies 'sarah'tuki' and 'faraqtuki' as kinayah and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A word is sarih only if it is never used for non-women; Quranic frequency does not override 'urf.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a fundamental dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the nature of certain divorce words. Understanding this distinction is crucial because the legal effect—whether divorce occurs without intention—hinges on the classification. The Hanafi position relies on a strict definition of sarih and the role of custom ('urf).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on sarih vs. kinayah
Identify the precise Hanafi claim: 'sarah'tuki' and 'faraqtuki' are kinayah (allusive) for divorce, not sarih (explicit).
Sarih in Hanafi means a word used exclusively for women.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i argues these words are sarih because they appear in the Quran frequently in divorce contexts (e.g., 'tasrih bi ihsan').
Shafi'i considers Quranic usage as determinative for Shariah terminology.
3
Examine the Hanafi response: definition and 'urf
The Hanafi response defines sarih as words not used for any non-women context. Since 'sarah'tu' is used for releasing camels and 'faraqtu' for parting from debtors, they are ambiguous.
General custom ('urf) governs, not isolated Quranic occurrences.
4
Note the internal Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some Khwarazm Hanafi scholars treated 'tasrih' as sarih, effecting raj'i divorce without intention. This shows internal diversity.
The relied-upon position is kinayah.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If sarih, divorce occurs without intention. If kinayah, intention or local custom is required for divorce to take effect.
This practical difference affects countless marital situations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (سرحتك فارقتك وجعلهما الشافعي من الصريح لورودهما في القرآن للطلاق كثيراً. قلنا : المعتبر تعارفهما في العرف العام في الطلاق لاستعمالها شرعاً مراداً . كذا في فتح القدير. وفي الكافي : ولنا الصريح ما لا يستعمل في غير النساء وهم يقولون سرحت إبلي وفارقت غريمي، ومشايخ خوارزم من المتقدمين ومن المتأخرين كانوا يفتون بأن لفظ التسريح بمنزلة الصريح يقع به طلاق رجعي بدون النية. كذا في المجتبى. وفي الخانية : لو قال أنت السراج فهو كقوله أنت خلية اعزبي وفي القنية والإقرار بالفرقة ليس بإقرار بالطلاق لاختلاف أسبابها
His saying: (Sarah'tuki and faraqtuki) and al-Shafi'i considered them as sarih (explicit) because they occur in the Quran for divorce frequently. We say: Consideration is given to their common usage in the general custom of divorce, because they are used in Shariah in a intended sense. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Kafi: For us, sarih is what is not used for other than women. They say: 'I released my camels' and 'I parted from my debtor'. The scholars of Khwarazm, both early and later, used to give fatwa that the word tasrih (sarah'tu) is equivalent to sarih, and with it a raj'i divorce occurs without intention. Thus in al-Mujtaba. In al-Khaniyyah: If he said 'you are the head' (al-siraj), it is like his saying 'you are khaliyyah' (empty) meaning 'go away'. In al-Qunyah: Acknowledgment of separation is not an acknowledgment of divorce, because their causes differ.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the status of the words 'sarah'tuki' (I released you) and 'faraqtuki' (I separated from you). Imam al-Shafi'i considers these words sarih (explicit) for divorce because they appear in the Quran in the context of divorce (e.g., 'tasrih bi ihsan'). The Hanafi school argues that sarih is defined as words that are only used for women and not for other contexts. Since 'sarah'tu' is also used for releasing camels and 'faraqtu' for parting from debtors, these words are ambiguous and thus kinayah, not sarih. The Hanafi position is that 'urf (customary usage) determines their meaning. However, the passage notes that some Hanafi scholars, specifically from Khwarazm, gave fatwa that 'tasrih' is equivalent to sarih and effects a raj'i divorce without intention. The passage also distinguishes between acknowledging separation and acknowledging divorce. This illustrates the internal Hanafi diversity on this point, though the relied-upon position is that both are kinayah.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): In this context, an explicit word for divorce that does not require intention; it is only used for divorce, not for other matters.
تسريح (tasrih): Verbal noun of sarahtu; releasing or setting free; in divorce context, it means divorcing with kindness.
فراق (firaq): Separation; parting; in family law, it can mean divorce or general separation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, why are 'sarah'tuki' and 'faraqtuki' considered kinayah and not sarih?
A) Because they are not mentioned in the Quran.
B) Because they are used in other contexts like releasing camels or parting from debtors.
C) Because they require the wife's consent.
D) Because the Quran uses them only for divorce, so they must be sarih.

Key Takeaway
A word is sarih only if it is used exclusively for women; mere Quranic occurrence does not make it explicit.
