Kinayah Expressions for Divorce: Tafweed, Freedom, and Covering
Definitions and rulings for kinayah divorce expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each kinayah expression for divorce (tafweed, freedom, covering) and state the ruling when the husband intends divorce.

Core Idea
Kinayah for divorce requires intention; each expression implies a different legal consequence.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the remaining kinayah words for divorce. The author categorizes them into delegation (tafweed), freedom from marriage (hurrah), and covering expressions (taqanna'i, takhammari, istatiri). Each requires the husband's intention to effect divorce. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi divorce law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the kinayah expression
Read the husband's statement and determine if it is one of the listed kinayah words or phrases.
Common expressions include 'amruki bi-yadik', 'ukhtari', 'anti hurrah', 'taqanna'i', et al.
2
Categorize the expression
Classify it as tafweed (delegation), freedom from marriage, or covering (veiling) expression.
Tafweed gives the wife the right to divorce herself; freedom implies she is no longer in the marriage tie; covering implies she has become a non-mahram stranger.
3
Assess the husband's intention
Since these are kinayah, intention is necessary. If he intended divorce, the divorce takes effect; otherwise, no divorce occurs.
Intention can be inferred from context or explicit declaration.
4
Determine the type of divorce
For tafweed, the wife may divorce herself. For freedom and covering expressions, one pronouncement of divorce (ba'in) usually occurs if intended.
The divorce is generally considered ba'in (irrevocable) unless specified otherwise in the text or by custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : أمرك بيدك اختاري كنايتان للتفويض فإذا نوى تفويض الطلاق إليها كان لها أن تطلق نفسها كما سيأتي قوله : (أنت حرة) عن حقيقة الرق أو عن رق النكاح. وفي فتح القدير واعتقتك مثل أنت حرة. وفي البدائع : كوني حرة واعتقي مثل أنت حرة ككوني طالقاً مثل أنت طالق قوله : (تقنعي تخمري استتري) لأنك بنت وحرمت علي بالطلاق أو لئلا ينظر إليك أجنبي. وفي المصباح : قناع المرأة جمعه قنع مثل كتاب وكتب، وتقنعت لبست القناع والخمار ثوب تغطي به المرأة رأسها والجمع خمر ككتاب ذكره في القولة التي بعده.
His saying: (Your affair is in your hand, choose) are two kinyahs for tafweed (delegation). So if he intends delegation of divorce to her, she may divorce herself, as will come. His saying: (You are free) from the reality of slavery or from the bond of marriage. In Fath al-Qadir: 'I have freed you' is like 'you are free'. In al-Bada'i': 'Be free' and 'be freed' are like 'you are free', just as 'be divorced' is like 'you are divorced'. His saying: (Cover yourself, veil yourself, conceal yourself) because you have become a daughter and have become forbidden to me by divorce, or so that a stranger does not look at you. In al-Misbah: A woman's qina' (veil) its plural is quna' like kitab and kutub; she placed a qina' (veiled). Al-khimar is a garment with which a woman covers her head, its plural is khumr like kitab; he mentioned it in the saying that follows.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage covers the remaining kinyā words for divorce. 'Amruki bi-yadik' (your affair is in your hand) and 'ukhtari' (choose) are both kinayah for tafweed, meaning the husband delegates the power of divorce to the wife. If he intends delegation, the wife can divorce herself. 'Anti hurrah' (you are free) can mean freedom from slavery or freedom from the bond of marriage; it is kinayah for divorce. The related forms like 'i'taqtuki' (I freed you) are the same. The words 'taqanna'i' (cover your face), 'takhammari' (cover your head), and 'istatiri' (conceal yourself) are kinayah for divorce because they imply she has become a stranger (non-mahram) due to divorce. The linguistic explanation of qina' (face veil) and khimar (head cover) is provided. Each of these requires intention to effect divorce.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafweed): Delegation of the right of divorce from the husband to the wife.
عتق (itq): Emancipation from slavery; when used towards a wife, it may be a kinayah for divorce meaning freedom from the marriage tie.
تقنع (taqanna'a): To cover one's face with a qina' (veil); used as kinayah for divorce implying she must cover because she is now a stranger.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No divorce because it is about slavery.
B) A ba'in divorce because it is kinayah for divorce.
C) A raj'i divorce because it is considered sarih.
D) The marriage continues as if nothing happened.

Key Takeaway
Kinayah divorce expressions require clear intention; mere utterance without intent does not effect divorce.

Ruling on the Kinayah 'Wa Habtuka li-Ahlika' and Similar Expressions
Explains the ruling of the kinayah 'Wa habtuka li-Ahlika' and its variations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the kinayah expression 'Wa habtuka li-ahlika' as requiring intention for divorce and apply the rule to similar expressions.

Core Idea
A kinayah expression like 'I gifted you to your family' requires intention to effect divorce; without it, no divorce occurs.

The expression 'Wa habtuka li-ahlika' (I gifted you to your family) is a common kinayah for divorce. This lesson explores why it is a kinayah, how it works, and which similar expressions are treated the same way. You will learn to distinguish between kinayah that require intention and expressions that effect divorce without intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression as kinayah
Determine that the phrase is not a clear (sarih) expression of divorce but an allusion.
Kinayah relies on customary understanding.
2
Analyze the underlying metaphor
Understand that 'gifting' implies transfer of ownership, metaphorically returning the wife to her family.
This metaphorical meaning triggers the possibility of divorce.
3
State the ruling
Divorce only occurs if the husband intended it; otherwise no effect.
Intention is the decisive factor.
4
Apply to similar expressions
Recognize that expressions like 'to your father' or 'to the husbands' are also kinayah, but not 'to strangers'.
Custom determines which relatives are considered 'family'.
5
Contrast with close expressions
Note that 'I gifted you your divorce' is treated as sarih in court, but in response to wife's request it is not.
Direct mention of divorce changes the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وهبتك لأهلك) يحتمل البينونة لأن الهبة تقتضي زوال الملك . أطلقه فشمل ما إذا لم يقبلوها لأن القبول لا يحتاج إليه لإزالة الملك، كذا في المحيط والتحقيق أنه مجاز عن رددتك إليهم فتصير إلى الحالة الأولى وهي البينونة كالحقي بأهلك، ومثله وهبتك لأبيك أو لابنك أو للأزواج لأنها ترد إلى هؤلاء بالطلاق عادة، وخرج عنه ما لو قال وهبتك للأجانب فإنه ليس بكناية، والأخ والأخت والعمة والخالة من الأجانب هنا فلا يقع وإن نوى كما في المعراج لأنها لا ترد إليهم بالطلاق عادة، وخرج عنه ما لو قال وهبتك بعض طلاقك فإنه ليس بكناية، وقدمنا أنه لو قال وهبت لك طلاقك فإنه يقع في القضاء بلا نية ولا يصدق أنه أراد كونه في يدها إلا إذا وقع جواباً لقولها هب لي طلاقي فإنه لا يقع وإن نوى وفي المعراج : لو قال أبحتك طلاقك لا يقع وإن نوى. وفي الذخيرة وهبت نفسك منك يقع إذا نوى.
His saying: ('And I gifted you to your family') implies final separation (baynunah) because gifting requires removal of ownership. He made it absolute, so it includes the case where they do not accept it, because acceptance is not needed for removal of ownership, as in al-Muhit. And the reality is that it is a metaphor for 'I returned you to them,' so you return to the first state, which is baynunah, like 'alhaqi bi-ahlik.' Similarly, 'I gifted you to your father' or 'to your son' or 'to the husbands' because by custom she is returned to them through divorce. Excluded from that is if he said 'I gifted you to strangers,' for it is not a kinayah. And the brother, sister, paternal aunt, and maternal aunt are among the strangers here, so it does not occur even if he intended, as in al-Mi'raj, because she is not returned to them by custom through divorce. Excluded from that is if he said 'I gifted you part of your divorce,' for it is not a kinayah. And we previously mentioned that if he said 'I gifted you your divorce,' then it occurs in the court without intention, and he is not believed that he intended it to be in her hand, except if it occurs as a response to her saying 'Grant me my divorce,' then it does not occur even if he intended. And in al-Mi'raj: If he said 'I made your divorce permissible for you,' it does not occur even if he intended. And in al-Dhakhirah: 'I gifted yourself to yourself' occurs if he intended.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the kinayah expression 'Wa habtuka li-ahlika' (I gifted you to your family). The author explains that this phrase implies baynunah (complete separation) because a gift (hiba) results in the transfer of ownership, so by saying this the husband metaphorically returns his wife to her family, ending the marriage. This is a kinayah (allusion), not a clear expression (sarih), so it only effects a divorce if the husband intended divorce. The ruling applies whether or not the family accepts the gift, because acceptance is not required for the removal of ownership. Similar expressions like 'I gifted you to your father,' 'to your son,' or 'to the husbands' are also kinayah, as by custom such a statement returns the wife to these individuals after divorce. However, if the husband says 'I gifted you to strangers' such as a brother, sister, aunt, or any non-mahram, it is not a kinayah because by custom a divorced woman is not returned to strangers. If the husband says 'I gifted you some of your divorce,' that is also not a kinayah. The passage contrasts this with the case where the husband says 'I gifted you your divorce' (wahabtu laki talaqiki), which in court effects a revocable divorce without intention, unless it is in response to the wife's request for her divorce. Finally, the author notes that saying 'I made your divorce permissible for you' (abahtuka talaqaki) does not effect divorce, but 'I gifted yourself to yourself' (wahabtu nafsaki minki) does if intended.

Key Terms
وهبتك لأهلك (wa habtuka li-ahlika): A kinayah expression meaning 'I have given you to your family,' which implies divorce if intended.
بينونة (baynunah): Complete separation between spouses, terminating the marriage irrevocably.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage; here the expression is a metaphor for returning the wife to her family.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'I gifted you to your father' without intending divorce, does talaq occur according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because the expression implies return to the father.
B) No, because it is a kinayah and requires intention.
C) Yes, because it is a clear expression (sarih).
D) No, because the father's acceptance is not obtained.

Key Takeaway
The expression 'Wa habtuka li-ahlika' and its variants are kinayah for divorce; they require the husband's intention to effect talaq, and neither the family's acceptance nor explicit mention of divorce is needed.

Rulings on Kinayat for Divorce: Delegation, Freedom, and Commands
This passage enumerates multiple divorce kinayat and their rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify each kinayah expression as requiring intention or not and apply the Hanafi principle of qarinah to determine whether divorce occurs.

Core Idea
Most kinayat require the husband's intention; a clear qarinah like 'tazawwaji' can eliminate that need.

This lesson examines a range of indirect divorce expressions (kinayat) discussed in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. Understanding when intention is needed and when contextual clues suffice is essential for properly applying Hanafi family law. We will analyze each expression and its ruling, including the special case of 'idhhabi fa-tazawwaji' where scholars differ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression type
Determine whether the expression is a delegation (tafwid) or a pure kinayah. Delegation gives the wife the power to divorce herself. Pure kinayat require intention (niyyah) from the husband.
Amruki bi-yadiki and ikhtari are tafwid if intended.
2
Check for a qarinah
If the expression is accompanied by a clear contextual indicator (qarinah) that points to divorce, intention may not be required. For example, 'idhhabi tazawwaji' has 'tazawwaji' as a qarinah.
A qarinah makes the kinayah act like sarih.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule
For pure kinayat without a qarinah, divorce only occurs if the husband intended divorce. If he denies intention, no divorce takes place.
This is the default Hanafi position.
4
Note intra-madhhab disagreements
Some expressions, like 'idhhabi fa-tazawwaji', have differing rulings among Hanafi scholars. Compare the views of Qadi Khan and al-Bazzaziyyah.
Al-Bazzaziyyah says one divorce occurs without intention due to the qarinah of marriage.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that intention is generally required, except when a strong qarinah is present. The author of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq seems to favor the need for intention, finding the alternative far-fetched.
Always cite the later Hanafi masters for fatwa.

قوله : أمرك بيدك اختاري كنايتان للتفويض فإذا نوى تفويض الطلاق إليها كان لها أن تطلق نفسها كما سيأتي قوله : (أنت حرة) عن حقيقة الرق أو عن رق النكاح. وفي فتح القدير واعتقتك مثل أنت حرة. وفي البدائع : كوني حرة واعتقي مثل أنت حرة ككوني طالقاً مثل أنت طالق قوله : (تقنعي تخمري استتري) لأنك بنت وحرمت علي بالطلاق أو لئلا ينظر إليك أجنبي. وفي المصباح : قناع المرأة جمعه قنع مثل كتاب وكتب، وتقنعت لبست القناع والخمار ثوب تغطي به المرأة رأسها والجمع خمر ككتاب ذكره في القولة التي بعده. قوله : (اعزبي) من العزبة بالعين المهملة أو من الغروب بالمعجمة وهو البعد أي ابعدي لأني طلقتك أو لزيارة أهلك قوله : (اخرجي اذهبي قومي) لحاجة أو لأني طلقتك. قيد باقتصاره على «الذهبي» لأنه لو قال اذهبي فبيعي ثوبك لا يقع وإن نوى، ولو قال اذهبي إلى جهنم يقع إن نوى كذا في الخلاصة. ولو قال اذهبي فتزوجي وقال لم أنو الطلاق لم يقع شيء لأن معناه تزوجي إن أمكنك وحل لك. كذا في شرح الجامع الصغير لقاضيخان وفي القنية : اذهبي وتحللي إقرار بالثلاث. وفي المعراج : تنحي عني يقع إذا نوى. وفي البزازية : اذهبي وتزوجي تقع واحدة ولا حاجة إلى النية لأن تزوجي قرينة فإن نوى الثلاث فثلاث ا.هـ. وهو مخالف لما في شرح الجامع إلا أن يفرق بين الواو والفاء وهو بعيد هنا. وفي المنتقى عن محمد : اذهبي ألف مرة ينوي به وفي البدائع عن محمد : قال لها افلحي يريد الطلاق يقع لأنه بمعنى اذهبي. تقول العرب أفلح بخير أي ذهب بخير، ويحتمل اظفري بمرادك يقال أفلح الرجل إذا ظفر بمراده. قوله : (ابتغي الأزواج إن أمكنك وحل لك أو اطلبي النساء إذ الزوج مشترك بين الرجل والمرأة، أو ابتغي الأزواج لأني طلقتك وتزوجي مثلي. وفي القنية، زوج امرأته من غيره لا يكون طلاقاً.
His saying: ('Your affair is in your hand', 'Choose') are two kinayat for delegation (tafwid). So if he intended delegation of divorce to her, then she can divorce herself, as will come. His saying: ('You are free') regarding the reality of slavery or regarding the bond of marriage. And in Fath al-Qadir: 'I have freed you' is like 'You are free.' And in al-Bada'i: 'Be free' and 'You are free' are like 'You are free,' just as 'Be divorced' is like 'You are divorced.' His saying: ('Cover your face', 'Put on a khimar', 'Cover yourself') because you are a woman and have become forbidden to me by divorce, or so that a stranger does not look at you. In al-Misbah: The woman's veil (qina') plural like kitab and kutub, and 'taqanna't' means I wore the veil; and the khimar is a garment with which a woman covers her head, plural khumur like kitab. His saying: ('Depart' (i'zabi)) from 'azbah (with the unimportant 'ayn) or from ghurub (with the dotted letter) meaning distance, i.e., 'distance yourself because I have divorced you' or to visit your family. His saying: ('Go out', 'Go', 'Stand up') for a need or because I have divorced you. It is restricted to his limiting to 'go' (idhhabi), because if he said 'Go and sell your garment,' it does not occur even if he intended; and if he said 'Go to Hell,' it occurs if he intended, as in al-Khulasa. And if he said 'Go and get married' and said 'I did not intend divorce,' nothing occurs because its meaning is 'Get married if you can and it is permissible for you.' As in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir of Qadi Khan. And in al-Qunyah: 'Go and seek dissolution' is an acknowledgment of three. And in al-Mi'raj: 'Move away from me' occurs if he intended. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: 'Go and get married' occurs as one (divorce) and there is no need for intention because 'get married' is a qarinah; if he intended three, then three. This contradicts what is in Sharh al-Jami' unless one differentiates between waw and fa', which is far-fetched here. And in al-Muntaqa from Muhammad: 'Go a thousand times' he intends with it. And in al-Bada'i' from Muhammad: He said to her 'Be successful (iflahi)' intending divorce, it occurs because it means 'go.' The Arabs say 'aflaha bi-khayr' meaning 'went with good,' and it could mean 'achieve your desire'; it is said a man is 'mufleh' when he achieves his desire. His saying: ('Seek husbands' if you can and it is permissible for you, or seek women, since 'zawj' is common for man and woman; or 'Seek husbands' because I have divorced you and marry someone like me. And in al-Qunyah: He marries his wife to someone else, this is not divorce.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage provides rulings on a list of kinayat expressions for divorce. 'Amruki bi-yadiki' (your affair is in your hand) and 'ikhtari' (choose) are delegation (tafwid) - if the husband intends to delegate the power of divorce, the wife can divorce herself. 'Anti hurra' (you are free) and 'a'taqtuki' (I freed you) are kinayah for divorce from the bond of marriage, not from slavery. 'Taqanna'i' (cover your face), 'takhammari' (put on a khimar), 'istitiri' (cover yourself) - these are commands for covering, implying she is a woman and thus forbidden to the husband after divorce, or to prevent a stranger from looking. 'I'zabi' (depart) means distance yourself, either due to divorce or for visiting family. 'Ukhruji' (go out), 'idhhabi' (go), 'qumi' (stand up) - these are commands that can be for a need or for divorce. However, if the husband says 'idhhabi fa-bi'i thawbaki' (go and sell your garment), it is not divorce because the additional phrase shows it is for a purpose. 'Idhhabi ila jahannam' (go to Hell) is divorce if intended. 'Idhhabi fa-tazawwaji' (go and marry) - according to Qadi Khan, it is not divorce unless intended; but according to al-Bazzaziyyah, due to the qarinah of marriage, one divorce occurs without intention. The passage notes a contradiction but suggests a distinction between the conjunction waw and fa'. 'Iflahi' (be successful) is a kinayah meaning 'go,' so divorce occurs if intended. 'Ibtaghi azwajan' (seek husbands) - this can be interpreted as permission to remarry after divorce, so it is a kinayah. The passage ends by noting that marrying one's wife to someone else is not divorce.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation of the power of divorce to the wife; the husband gives her authority to divorce herself once.
قرينة (qarinah): Contextual evidence or clue that clarifies the intended meaning of an ambiguous expression like a kinayah.
كناية (kinayah): An indirect expression that can refer to divorce but does not explicitly state it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following expressions does the Hanafi school consider as requiring the husband's intention for divorce?
A) 'amruki bi-yadiki' (Your affair is in your hand)
B) 'idhhabi fa-tazawwaji' (Go and get married) according to al-Bazzaziyyah
C) 'anti hurra' (You are free)
D) 'idhhabi ila jahannam' (Go to Hell)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats most divorce kinayat as requiring the husband's intention, except when a strong contextual indicator (qarinah) makes the meaning unmistakable.

The Comprehensive Rule for Kinayah Divorce
Comprehensive principle for kinayah divorce effect and credibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for kinayah divorce by distinguishing between states of rida and ghadab with or without a divorce request to determine when the husband's claim of no intent is accepted.

Core Idea
Kinayah divorce depends on context: husband's claim accepted when expression can be rejection or insult, not when it is a direct response.

This passage presents the comprehensive rule (dabit) for kinayat in divorce, resolving a dispute between early Hanafi authorities. It outlines three scenarios based on the husband's emotional state and the wife's request, explaining when his claim of not intending divorce is believed in court. Understanding this rule is essential for correctly applying kinayah divorce in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scholarly positions on kinayah
Note the disagreement: al-Quduri and al-Sarakhsi hold all kinayah effect divorce by dalalat al-hal; Fakhr al-Islam requires niyyah for some.
2
Understand the three scenarios
The rule distinguishes: (1) rida without request, (2) rida with request, (3) ghadab without request. In each, the husband's credibility differs.
3
Classify expressions as salih li al-radd, salih li al-sabb, or salih li al-jawab
Based on the scenario, determine which category the kinayah falls into. Only expressions that can be rejection or insult allow the husband's claim.
In ghadab without request, expressions like 'haram' are salih li al-sabb.
4
Apply the rule to new kinayah examples
Given a new expression and context, state whether divorce occurs based on the rule and whether the husband's claim is accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف بإطلاقه إلى أن الكنايات كلها يقع بها الطلاق بدلالة الحال وقد تبع في ذلك القدوري والسرخسي في المبسوط وخالفهما فخر الإسلام وغيره من المشايخ فقالوا بعضها لا يقع بها إلا بالنية . والضابط على وجه التحرير أن في حالة الرضا المجرد عن سؤال الطلاق يصدق في الكل أنه لم يرد الطلاق، وفي حالة الرضا المسؤول فيها الطلاق يصدق فيما يصلح رداً أنه لم يرده مثل : اخرجي، اذهبي، اعزبي قومي تقنعي استتري تخمري وفي حالة الغضب المجرد عن سؤال الطلاق يصدق فيما يصلح سباً أو رداً أنه لم يرد به إلا السب أو الرد كخلية بريئة بتة بتلة بائن حرام وما يجري مجراه ولا يصدق فيما يصلح جواباً فقط كاعتدي واستبري رحمك وأنت واحدة واختاري وأمرك بيدك فما يصلح للجواب فقط خمسة كما في غاية البيان .
And the author (al-Musannif) indicated by his absolute statement that all kinayat effect divorce by contextual indication (dalalat al-hal). He followed in that al-Quduri and al-Sarakhsi in al-Mabsut. And Fakhr al-Islam and other mashayikh differed with them, saying some of them do not effect (divorce) except with intention. The precise rule (al-dabit) in detail is that: in a state of satisfaction (rida) free from a request for divorce, he is believed in all of them that he did not intend divorce. And in a state of satisfaction where divorce is requested, he is believed regarding what can be a rejection (radd) that he did not intend it, such as: 'ukhruji, idhhabi, i'zabi, qumi, taqanna'i, istitiri, takhammari.' And in a state of anger (ghadab) free from a request for divorce, he is believed regarding what can be an insult (sabb) or rejection, that he did not intend by it except the insult or rejection, like: 'khaliyah, bari'ah, battah, battalah, ba'in, haram, and what runs along those lines. And he is not believed regarding what can only be a response (jawab), such as: 'i'taddi, istibri, rahimaki, anti wahidah, ikhtari, amruki bi-yadiki.' For what can only be a response are five, as in Ghayat al-Bayan.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive rule (dabit) for when kinayat effect divorce and when the husband's claim of not intending divorce is accepted in court. The author notes two positions: One (al-Quduri and al-Sarakhsi) holds that all kinayat effect divorce based on contextual indication (dalalat al-hal). Another (Fakhr al-Islam) holds that some require explicit intention. The author then gives the detailed rule, distinguishing three cases: (1) In a state of contentment (rida) without any request for divorce from the wife, the husband is believed in all kinayat that he did not intend divorce. (2) In a state of contentment but when the wife has requested divorce, the husband is believed regarding kinayat that can serve as a rejection (radd) of her request, e.g., commands like 'go,' 'depart,' 'cover yourself.' (3) In a state of anger (ghadab) without a request for divorce, the husband is believed regarding kinayat that can serve as insults or rejection, e.g., 'empty vessel,' 'innocent,' 'forbidden.' However, he is not believed regarding kinayat that can only be a response (jawab) to a request, such as 'observe your iddah,' 'seek purification,' 'you are one.' These five are considered as direct responses and thus effect divorce in court even if the husband claims he did not intend divorce.

Key Terms
دلالة الحال (dalalat al-hal): Contextual evidence from the circumstances that indicates the intended meaning.
صالح للرد (salih li al-radd): An expression that can reasonably be interpreted as a rejection of a request, not necessarily as divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Rida without request
B) Rida with request
C) Ghadab without request
D) Ghadab with request

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi dabit for kinayah divorce balances context and intent, protecting husbands from unintended consequences while respecting the wife's rights.

General Rule and Dispute on Kinayah in Divorce
Dispute on the effect of kinayah: context vs intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether all kinayah words effect divorce by contextual indication and explain the opposing view of Fakhr al-Islam.

Core Idea
All kinayah may effect divorce by context, but some Hanafi scholars require explicit intention — a key intra-madhhab dispute.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq introduces the Hanafi rules on kinayah (allusive expressions) in divorce. It lists common kinayah terms and discusses whether they require intention (niyyah) or whether the situational context (dalalat al-hal) alone suffices. The author notes a significant intra-Hanafi disagreement between the majority view (Quduri, Sarakhsi) and Fakhr al-Islam and others who restrict the effect of certain words.

Guided Path
1
Identify the kinayah terms listed
Note each expression mentioned in the passage and the specific rulings given for them, such as idhhabi, tazawwaji, etc.
These terms are allusive; they do not explicitly say 'divorce'.
2
Understand the role of intention
For each term, determine whether the passage says divorce occurs with or without niyyah, and note the conditions.
Some terms require context; others require explicit intent.
3
Recognize the general rule of Kanz
The author of Kanz states that all kinayah effect divorce by dalalat al-hal. Identify this rule and its proponents (Quduri, Sarakhsi).
This is the broader view.
4
Identify the opposing position
Fakhr al-Islam and other mashayikh hold that some kinayah never effect divorce without intention. Locate this objection.
They argue certain words are too ambiguous.
5
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider a concrete example: if a husband says 'Go and marry' during a calm moment, does divorce occur under each view?
The practical difference is whether the husband's denial is accepted.

كتاب الطلاق باب الكنايات في الطلاق
أمرك بيدك اختاري أنت حرة تقنعي تخمري استتري اعزبي اخرجي اذهبي قومي ابتغي طلاقاً فثلاث .

قوله: (وهو بعيد (هنا أقول : يؤيده تصريح الذخيرة بخلافه حيث قال : ولو قال لها اذهبي وتزوجي لا يقع الطلاق إلا بالنية وإن نوى فهي واحدة بائنة وإن نوى الثلاث فهي ثلاث ا هـ قوله: (وفي المنتقى الخ) يخالفه ما في شرح قوله أنت طالق بائن أو البتة أو أفحش الطلاق الخ» إنه لو قال أنت علي حرام ألف مرة تقع واحدة ونبهنا عليه هناك قوله : (كخلية برية الخ) تمثيل لقوله

كتاب الطلاق باب الكنايات في الطلاق
حالة
وفي حالة الغضب المسؤول فيها الطلاق يجتمع في عدم تصديقه في المتمحض جواباً سببان : المذاكرة والغضب. وكذا في قبول قوله فيما يصلح رداً لأن كلاً . من المذاكرة والغضب يستقل بإثبات قبول قوله في دعوى عدم إرادة الطلاق، وفيما يصلح للسبب ينفرد الغضب بإثباته فلا تتغير الأحكام. وبهذا علم أن الأحوال ثلاثة : حالة مطلقة وحالة مذاكرة الطلاق وحالة الغضب، وأن المراد بالمطلقة المطلقة عن قيدي الغضب والمذاكرة، فقول الشارح وهي الرضا مما لا ينبغي. وأن الكنايات ثلاثة أقسام : قسم يصلح جواباً ولا يصلح رداً ولا شتماً، وقسم يصلح جواباً ورداً ولا يصلح شتماً، وقسم يصلح جواباً وشتماً ولا يصلح رداً. وعن أبي يوسف في قوله لا ملك لي عليك ولا سبيل لي عليك وخليت سبيلك، وفارقتك، أنه يصدق في حالة الغضب لما فيها من احتمال معنى السبب كذا في الهداية. وجعل فخر الإسلام وصاحب الفوائد الظهيرية هذه الألفاظ ملحقة عند أبي يوسف بما يصلح للجواب وهي اعتدي واختاري وأمرك بيدك. وإنما لم يذكر المصنف هذه التفاصيل لأن الحاكم الشهيد في الكافي الذي هو جمع كلام محمد في كتبه لم يذكره ولم يتعرض له شارحه الإمام السرخسي. وحاصل ما في الخانية أن من الكنايات ثلاث عشرة لا يعتبر فيها دلالة الحال ولا تقع إلا بالنية: حبلك على غاربك تقنعي ،تخمري ،استتري قومي، اخرجي، اذهبي، انتقلي، انطلقي تزوجي اعزبي لا نكاح لي عليك وهبتك لأهلك. وفيما عداها تعتبر الدلالة لكن ثمانية تقع بها حال المذاكرة : أنت خلية، برية بتة بائن، حرام، اعتدي، أمرك بيدك، اختاري وثلاثة من هذه الثمانية يقع بها حال الغضب: اعتدي، أمرك بيدك، اختاري. ثم قال بعد هذه لو قال في مذاكرة الطلاق فارقتك وباينتك أو بنت منك أو لا سلطان لي عليك أو سر حتك أو وهبتك لنفسك أو تركت طلاقك أو خليت سبيل طلاقك أو سبيلك أو أنت بائنة أو أنت حرة أو أنت أعلم بشأنك فقالت اخترت نفسي يقع الطلاق، وإن قال لم أنو الطلاق لا يصدق ا هـ . فصارت الألفاظ الواقع بها حال المذاكرة عشرين لفظاً. وإنما وقع البائن بما عدا الثلاث وما كان بمعناها مع أن المكنى عنه الطلاق وهو يعقب الرجعة لأنا نمنع أن المكنى عنه الطلاق بل إنما هو البينونة لأنها . اللفظ الدائر في سباً لا له ولقوله أوردا لأنها لا تصلح له وارجع إلى النهر تزدد بصيرة قوله: (وبهذا علم أن الأحوال ثلاثة) قال في النهر : وعندي أن الأولى هو الاقتصار على حالة الغضب والمذاكرة إذ الكلام في الأحوال التي تؤثر فيها الدلالة لا مطلقاً. ثم رأيته في البدائع بعد أن قسم الأحوال ثلاثة كالشارح قال : ففي حالة الرضا يدين في القضاء وإن كان في حالة مذاكرة الطلاق أو الغضب فقد قالوا: إن الكنايات أقسام ثلاثة وذكر ما مر وهذا هو التحقيق قوله : (قسم يصلح جواباً) أي جواباً لطلبها
الطلاق أي التطليق .
هي
معنی
Book of Divorce, Chapter on Kinayah (allusions) in Divorce. 'Your affair is in your hand', 'Choose yourself', 'You are free', 'Cover your face', 'Veil yourself', 'Conceal yourself', 'Leave', 'Go out', 'Go', 'Stand up', 'Seek a divorce' — so three. And he wrote, and the woman veiled herself and covered herself: she wore the khimar (headcover). End. In al-Mi'raj: 'Taqanna'i' is from qina'ah (covering), or from qina' (veil). He confined to his saying 'Istatiri', so it indicates that if he said 'Conceal yourself from me', it exits being a kinayah, as Qadikhan mentioned in his commentary. His saying: (I'zabi) from 'uzba (spinsterhood) or from ghurub (setting), meaning distance: go away because I divorced you or to visit your family. His saying: (Ukhruji, Idhhabi, Qumi) for a need or because I divorced you. He restricted to 'al-Dhahabi' because if he said 'Go and sell your garment', it does not occur even if he intended; and if he said 'Go to Hell', it occurs if he intended, as in al-Khulasa. If he said 'Go and marry' and said he did not intend divorce, nothing occurs because its meaning is 'marry if you can and it is permissible for you', as in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir by Qadikhan. In al-Qunyah: 'Go and declare yourself free' is an acknowledgment of three. In al-Mi'raj: 'Remove yourself from me' occurs if he intends. In al-Bazzaziyyah: 'Go and marry' occurs as one divorce without need for intention because 'marry' is a contextual indication; if he intends three, then three. End. This contradicts what is in Sharh al-Jami' unless one differentiates between 'wa' and 'fa', which is far-fetched here. In al-Muntaqa from Muhammad: 'Go a thousand times' with intention; in al-Bada'i' from Muhammad: he said to her 'Aflihi' intending divorce, it occurs because it means 'go'. The Arabs say 'Aflaha bi khayr' meaning 'he went with good', and it is possible it means 'achieve your desire', as it is said 'Aflaha al-rajul' when he achieves his desire. His saying: (Ibtaghi al-azwaj) if possible and permissible for you, or seek women since 'zawj' is common between man and woman, or seek husbands because I divorced you and marry the likes of me. In al-Qunyah: he marries his wife to another, it is not divorce. Then he numbered for another: if he intends divorce, she is divorced. In it before: 'You are a foreign woman' and intends divorce, it does not occur because it is a rejection, and in the context of discussing divorce it is an acknowledgment. The author (of Kanz) indicated by his absolute statement that all kinayah effect divorce by indication of the situation. He followed in that al-Quduri and al-Sarakhsi in al-Mabsut. But Fakhr al-Islam and others among the mashayikh disagreed, saying some do not effect without intention. The precise rule: In the state of mere contentment without request for divorce, he is believed in all that he did not intend divorce. In the state of contentment with request for divorce, he is believed in what can serve as a rejection that he did not intend it, such as: 'Go out', 'Go', 'Leave', 'Stand up', 'Cover your face', 'Veil yourself', 'Conceal yourself'. In the state of mere anger without request for divorce, he is believed in what can serve as an insult or rejection that he intended only insult or rejection, such as: 'khaliyyah', 'bari'ah', 'battah', 'batlah', 'ba'in', 'haram' and the like; and he is not believed in what can serve only as a response, such as: 'I'taddi', 'Istabiri rahimaki', 'Anti wahidah', 'Ikhtari', 'Amruki bi yadik'. So what can serve only as a response are five, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. His saying: (and it is far-fetched) I say: it is supported by the explicit statement in al-Dhakhirah contrary to it: 'if he said to her Go and marry, divorce does not occur except with intention, and if he intends, she is one ba'in, and if he intends three, then three.' End. His saying: (In al-Muntaqa etc.) contradicts what is in Sharh on his saying 'Anti taliq ba'in or al-battah or afhash al-talaq' that if he said 'Anti 'alayya haram' a thousand times, one occurs, and we pointed out there. His saying: (Khalilah bari'ah etc.) is an example of his saying. Only. In the state of anger with request for divorce, two reasons combine for not believing him in a word that is purely a response: the discussion and anger. Similarly, in accepting his statement in what can serve as a rejection, because each of discussion and anger independently establishes acceptance of his statement in the claim of not intending divorce. In what can serve as a cause, anger alone establishes it, so the rulings do not change. By this it is known that the states are three: absolute state, state of discussing divorce, and state of anger. And that by 'absolute' is meant free from the two constraints of anger and discussion, so the commentator's saying that it is contentment is not appropriate. And that the kinayah are three categories: a category that serves as a response and does not serve as a rejection or insult; a category that serves as a response and rejection but does not serve as an insult; and a category that serves as a response and insult but does not serve as a rejection. From Abu Yusuf, regarding his saying 'No ownership for me over you', 'No way for me over you', 'I released your way', 'I separated from you', that he is believed in the state of anger because of the possibility of the meaning of a cause, as in al-Hidayah. Fakhr al-Islam and the author of al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyyah made these expressions, according to Abu Yusuf, attached to what serves as a response: namely I'taddi, Ikhtari, Amruki bi yadik. The author did not mention these details because al-Hakim al-Shahid in al-Kafi, which is a collection of Muhammad's statements in his books, did not mention it, and his commentator Imam al-Sarakhsi did not address it. The summary of what is in al-Khaniyyah is that among the kinayah, thirteen are not considered with indication of the situation and only occur with intention: 'Hablu 'ala gharibik', 'Taqanna'i', 'Takhammari', 'Istatiri', 'Qumi', 'Ukhruji', 'Idhhabi', 'Intaqili', 'Intaliqi', 'Tazawwaji', 'I'zabi', 'La nikaha li 'alayki', 'Wahabtuki li-ahlik'. Other than these, indication is considered. However, eight occur in the state of discussing divorce: 'Anti khaliyyah', 'bari'ah', 'battah', 'ba'in', 'haram', 'I'taddi', 'Amruki bi yadik', 'Ikhtari'. Three of these eight occur in the state of anger: 'I'taddi', 'Amruki bi yadik', 'Ikhtari'. Then he said after that: if he said in a discussion of divorce 'Faraqtuki', 'Bayantuki', or 'Bintu minki', 'La sultana li 'alayki', 'Sarrahtuki', 'Wahabtuki li-nafsiki', 'Taraktu talaqaki', 'Khallaytu sabili talaqaki', 'Sabili' or 'Anti ba'inah' or 'Anti hurrah' or 'Anti a'lamu bi-sha'niki' and she said 'I choose myself', divorce occurs, and if he said 'I did not intend divorce', he is not believed. End. Thus the words that occur in the state of discussing divorce amount to twenty words. And indeed ba'in occurs with other than the three and what is in their meaning, even though what is intended is divorce which is revocable? We deny that what is intended is divorce; rather it is binunah (irrevocability) because that word is used in insults, not for it, and because it does not serve for it. Refer to al-Nahr for more insight. His saying: (By this it is known that the states are three) He said in al-Nahr: In my view, the best is to restrict to the state of anger and discussion, since the discussion is about states that affect the indication, not absolutely. Then I saw in al-Bada'i' after he divided the states into three like the commentator, he said: In the state of contentment, he is believed judicially, but if he is in a state of discussing divorce or anger, they said: the kinayah are three categories, and he mentioned what preceded, and this is the verification. His saying: (A category that serves as a response) meaning a response to her request for divorce, i.e., divorcing.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage is a detailed expansion from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq on the chapter of kinayah (allusive words) in divorce. The author opens by listing a series of kinayah expressions that, if uttered in a context that indicates divorce (such as a discussion of divorce or anger), can effect divorce depending on intention. He then explains each term individually, citing various Hanafi authorities like Qadikhan, al-Mi'raj, al-Khulasa, and others. For each expression, he specifies whether divorce occurs with or without explicit intention, and under what conditions. For example, if a husband says 'Go and marry', the word 'marry' is a contextual indicator (qarina) that he intends divorce, so one divorce occurs without need for further intention. However, if he denies intent, he is not believed in certain states. The passage then shifts to a broader legal principle: the author of Kanz (al-Nasafi) held that all kinayah effect divorce by indication of the situation (dalalat al-hal), following al-Quduri and al-Sarakhsi. But Fakhr al-Islam and others disagreed, holding that some kinayah require explicit intention. The text then provides a refined rule dividing states into three: (1) state of mere contentment (rida), (2) state of discussing divorce (mudhakarat al-talaq), and (3) state of anger (ghadab). It explains how each state affects the acceptance of the husband's claim that he did not intend divorce. Kinayah are further classified into three categories based on whether they can serve as a response (jawab), a rejection (radd), or an insult (sabb). The passage also cites Abu Yusuf's view on certain expressions and concludes with a comprehensive list from al-Khaniyyah: thirteen kinayah that never effect divorce without intention, and eight that do in the context of discussing divorce, with three of those eight also operating in anger. The overall ruling (mufta bihi) appears to be the detailed position that distinguishes between words and states, as this is the most practical and widely accepted view among later Hanafi scholars.

Key Terms
كناية (kināyah): An allusive expression that does not explicitly mention divorce but implies it, such as 'Go to your family' or 'You are free'.
دلالة الحال (dalālat al-ḥāl): The indication derived from the circumstantial context, such as the presence of anger or discussion of divorce, which influences whether the husband's words are interpreted as divorce.
مذاكرة الطلاق (mudhākarat al-ṭalāq): A state where the spouses are discussing divorce, which provides a strong contextual indication that the husband's allusive words are meant as divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which two Hanafi authorities held that all kinayah effect divorce by contextual indication?
A) al-Quduri and al-Sarakhsi
B) Fakhr al-Islam and Qadikhan
C) Muhammad al-Shaybani and Abu Yusuf
D) al-Nasafi and al-Haskafi

Key Takeaway
The effect of kinayah in divorce depends on both the type of word and the situational context, with a significant intra-Hanafi dispute on whether intention is always required.

Talaq — Divorce
advanced
10 min
Classification of Kinayah by States and Word Types
Detailed classification: states and word types for kinayah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify kinayah words according to the three states (rida, mudhakara, ghadab) and three categories (jawab-only, jawab+radd, jawab+sabb) and apply the list from al-Khaniyyah.

Core Idea
Kinayah are divided into three categories based on semantic function; their effect depends on three states of the husband.

This passage continues the discussion of kinayah by providing a precise rule (dabit) for determining when a husband's denial of intention is accepted. It introduces the three states—contentment, discussion of divorce, and anger—and the three categories of kinayah words based on whether they can serve as a response, rejection, or insult. It culminates with the comprehensive list from al-Khaniyyah, which distinguishes thirteen words that always require intention from eight that operate by context, with three of those also working in anger.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three states
Identify the states: absolute contentment (rida), discussion of divorce (mudhakara), and anger (ghadab). Note how each state affects the acceptance of the husband's claim of no intent.
The state of anger with request for divorce combines two factors.
2
Learn the three categories of kinayah
Recognize that kinayah are divided into: (1) those that can only serve as a response to a request for divorce, (2) those that can serve as a response and rejection, and (3) those that can serve as a response and insult.
Each category has different rules for when the husband's denial is accepted.
3
Memorize the list from al-Khaniyyah
Thirteen words never work without intention; eight work in the state of discussing divorce; three of those eight also work in anger.
This list is the operative rule in later fatwa.
4
Apply the rule to a given scenario
Given a kinayah word and a state (e.g., 'I'taddi' during anger), determine whether divorce occurs without intention.
Use the list and the category of the word.

كتاب الطلاق باب الكنايات في الطلاق
أمرك بيدك اختاري أنت حرة تقنعي تخمري استتري اعزبي اخرجي اذهبي قومي ابتغي طلاقاً فثلاث .
وكتب، واختمرت المرأة وتخمرت لبست الخمار ا هــ وفي المعراج : تقنعي من القناعة، وقيل من القناع وهو الخمار، واقتصر على قوله «استتري) فأفاد أنه لو قال استتري مني خرج عن كونه كناية كما ذكره قاضيخان في شرحه قوله : (اعزبي) من العزبة بالعين المهملة أو من الغروب بالمعجمة وهو البعد أي ابعدي لأني طلقتك أو لزيارة أهلك قوله : (اخرجي اذهبي قومي) لحاجة أو لأني طلقتك. قيد باقتصاره على «الذهبي» لأنه لو قال اذهبي فبيعي ثوبك لا يقع وإن نوى، ولو قال اذهبي إلى جهنم يقع إن نوى كذا في الخلاصة. ولو قال اذهبي فتزوجي وقال لم أنو الطلاق لم يقع شيء لأن معناه تزوجي إن أمكنك وحل لك. كذا في شرح الجامع الصغير لقاضيخان وفي القنية : اذهبي وتحللي إقرار بالثلاث. وفي المعراج : تنحي عني يقع إذا نوى. وفي البزازية : اذهبي وتزوجي تقع واحدة ولا حاجة إلى النية لأن تزوجي قرينة فإن نوى الثلاث فثلاث ا.هـ. وهو مخالف لما في شرح الجامع إلا أن يفرق بين الواو والفاء وهو بعيد هنا. وفي المنتقى عن محمد : اذهبي ألف مرة ينوي به وفي البدائع عن محمد : قال لها افلحي يريد الطلاق يقع لأنه بمعنى اذهبي. تقول العرب أفلح بخير أي ذهب بخير، ويحتمل اظفري بمرادك يقال أفلح الرجل إذا ظفر بمراده. قوله : (ابتغي الأزواج إن أمكنك وحل لك أو اطلبي النساء إذ الزوج مشترك بين الرجل والمرأة، أو ابتغي الأزواج لأني طلقتك وتزوجي مثلي. وفي القنية، زوج امرأته من غيره لا يكون طلاقاً. ثم رقم لآخر : إذا نوى الطلاق طلقت. وفيها قبله : أنت أجنبية ونوى الطلاق لا يقع لأنه رد وفي حال مذاكرة الطلاق إقرار وأشار المصنف بإطلاقه إلى أن الكنايات كلها يقع بها الطلاق بدلالة الحال وقد تبع في ذلك القدوري والسرخسي في المبسوط وخالفهما فخر الإسلام وغيره من المشايخ فقالوا بعضها لا يقع بها إلا بالنية . والضابط على وجه التحرير أن في حالة الرضا المجرد عن سؤال الطلاق يصدق في الكل أنه لم يرد الطلاق، وفي حالة الرضا المسؤول فيها الطلاق يصدق فيما يصلح رداً أنه لم يرده مثل : اخرجي، اذهبي، اعزبي قومي تقنعي استتري تخمري وفي حالة الغضب المجرد عن سؤال الطلاق يصدق فيما يصلح سباً أو رداً أنه لم يرد به إلا السب أو الرد كخلية بريئة بتة بتلة بائن حرام وما يجري مجراه ولا يصدق فيما يصلح جواباً فقط كاعتدي واستبري رحمك وأنت واحدة واختاري وأمرك بيدك فما يصلح للجواب فقط خمسة كما في غاية البيان .
قوله: (وهو بعيد (هنا أقول : يؤيده تصريح الذخيرة بخلافه حيث قال : ولو قال لها اذهبي وتزوجي لا يقع الطلاق إلا بالنية وإن نوى فهي واحدة بائنة وإن نوى الثلاث فهي ثلاث ا هـ قوله: (وفي المنتقى الخ) يخالفه ما في شرح قوله أنت طالق بائن أو البتة أو أفحش الطلاق الخ» إنه لو قال أنت علي حرام ألف مرة تقع واحدة ونبهنا عليه هناك قوله : (كخلية برية الخ) تمثيل لقوله

كتاب الطلاق باب الكنايات في الطلاق
حالة
وفي حالة الغضب المسؤول فيها الطلاق يجتمع في عدم تصديقه في المتمحض جواباً سببان : المذاكرة والغضب. وكذا في قبول قوله فيما يصلح رداً لأن كلاً . من المذاكرة والغضب يستقل بإثبات قبول قوله في دعوى عدم إرادة الطلاق، وفيما يصلح للسبب ينفرد الغضب بإثباته فلا تتغير الأحكام. وبهذا علم أن الأحوال ثلاثة : حالة مطلقة وحالة مذاكرة الطلاق وحالة الغضب، وأن المراد بالمطلقة المطلقة عن قيدي الغضب والمذاكرة، فقول الشارح وهي الرضا مما لا ينبغي. وأن الكنايات ثلاثة أقسام : قسم يصلح جواباً ولا يصلح رداً ولا شتماً، وقسم يصلح جواباً ورداً ولا يصلح شتماً، وقسم يصلح جواباً وشتماً ولا يصلح رداً. وعن أبي يوسف في قوله لا ملك لي عليك ولا سبيل لي عليك وخليت سبيلك، وفارقتك، أنه يصدق في حالة الغضب لما فيها من احتمال معنى السبب كذا في الهداية. وجعل فخر الإسلام وصاحب الفوائد الظهيرية هذه الألفاظ ملحقة عند أبي يوسف بما يصلح للجواب وهي اعتدي واختاري وأمرك بيدك. وإنما لم يذكر المصنف هذه التفاصيل لأن الحاكم الشهيد في الكافي الذي هو جمع كلام محمد في كتبه لم يذكره ولم يتعرض له شارحه الإمام السرخسي. وحاصل ما في الخانية أن من الكنايات ثلاث عشرة لا يعتبر فيها دلالة الحال ولا تقع إلا بالنية: حبلك على غاربك تقنعي ،تخمري ،استتري قومي، اخرجي، اذهبي، انتقلي، انطلقي تزوجي اعزبي لا نكاح لي عليك وهبتك لأهلك. وفيما عداها تعتبر الدلالة لكن ثمانية تقع بها حال المذاكرة : أنت خلية، برية بتة بائن، حرام، اعتدي، أمرك بيدك، اختاري وثلاثة من هذه الثمانية يقع بها حال الغضب: اعتدي، أمرك بيدك، اختاري. ثم قال بعد هذه لو قال في مذاكرة الطلاق فارقتك وباينتك أو بنت منك أو لا سلطان لي عليك أو سر حتك أو وهبتك لنفسك أو تركت طلاقك أو خليت سبيل طلاقك أو سبيلك أو أنت بائنة أو أنت حرة أو أنت أعلم بشأنك فقالت اخترت نفسي يقع الطلاق، وإن قال لم أنو الطلاق لا يصدق ا هـ . فصارت الألفاظ الواقع بها حال المذاكرة عشرين لفظاً. وإنما وقع البائن بما عدا الثلاث وما كان بمعناها مع أن المكنى عنه الطلاق وهو يعقب الرجعة لأنا نمنع أن المكنى عنه الطلاق بل إنما هو البينونة لأنها . اللفظ الدائر في سباً لا له ولقوله أوردا لأنها لا تصلح له وارجع إلى النهر تزدد بصيرة قوله: (وبهذا علم أن الأحوال ثلاثة) قال في النهر : وعندي أن الأولى هو الاقتصار على حالة الغضب والمذاكرة إذ الكلام في الأحوال التي تؤثر فيها الدلالة لا مطلقاً. ثم رأيته في البدائع بعد أن قسم الأحوال ثلاثة كالشارح قال : ففي حالة الرضا يدين في القضاء وإن كان في حالة مذاكرة الطلاق أو الغضب فقد قالوا: إن الكنايات أقسام ثلاثة وذكر ما مر وهذا هو التحقيق قوله : (قسم يصلح جواباً) أي جواباً لطلبها

'Your affair is in your hand', 'Choose yourself', 'You are free', 'Cover your face', 'Veil yourself', 'Conceal yourself', 'Leave', 'Go out', 'Go', 'Stand up', 'Seek a divorce' — so three. And he wrote, and the woman veiled herself and covered herself: she wore the khimar (headcover). End. In al-Mi'raj: 'Taqanna'i' is from qina'ah (covering), or from qina' (veil). He confined to his saying 'Istatiri', so it indicates that if he said 'Conceal yourself from me', it exits being a kinayah, as Qadikhan mentioned in his commentary. His saying: (I'zabi) from 'uzba (spinsterhood) or from ghurub (setting), meaning distance: go away because I divorced you or to visit your family. His saying: (Ukhruji, Idhhabi, Qumi) for a need or because I divorced you. He restricted to 'al-Dhahabi' because if he said 'Go and sell your garment', it does not occur even if he intended; and if he said 'Go to Hell', it occurs if he intended, as in al-Khulasa. If he said 'Go and marry' and said he did not intend divorce, nothing occurs because its meaning is 'marry if you can and it is permissible for you', as in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir by Qadikhan. In al-Qunyah: 'Go and declare yourself free' is an acknowledgment of three. In al-Mi'raj: 'Remove yourself from me' occurs if he intends. In al-Bazzaziyyah: 'Go and marry' occurs as one divorce without need for intention because 'marry' is a contextual indication; if he intends three, then three. End. This contradicts what is in Sharh al-Jami' unless one differentiates between 'wa' and 'fa', which is far-fetched here. In al-Muntaqa from Muhammad: 'Go a thousand times' with intention; in al-Bada'i' from Muhammad: he said to her 'Aflihi' intending divorce, it occurs because it means 'go'. The Arabs say 'Aflaha bi khayr' meaning 'he went with good', and it is possible it means 'achieve your desire', as it is said 'Aflaha al-rajul' when he achieves his desire. His saying: (Ibtaghi al-azwaj) if possible and permissible for you, or seek women since 'zawj' is common between man and woman, or seek husbands because I divorced you and marry the likes of me. In al-Qunyah: he marries his wife to another, it is not divorce. Then he numbered for another: if he intends divorce, she is divorced. In it before: 'You are a foreign woman' and intends divorce, it does not occur because it is a rejection, and in the context of discussing divorce it is an acknowledgment. The author (of Kanz) indicated by his absolute statement that all kinayah effect divorce by indication of the situation. He followed in that al-Quduri and al-Sarakhsi in al-Mabsut. But Fakhr al-Islam and others among the mashayikh disagreed, saying some do not effect without intention. The precise rule: In the state of mere contentment without request for divorce, he is believed in all that he did not intend divorce. In the state of contentment with request for divorce, he is believed in what can serve as a rejection that he did not intend it, such as: 'Go out', 'Go', 'Leave', 'Stand up', 'Cover your face', 'Veil yourself', 'Conceal yourself'. In the state of mere anger without request for divorce, he is believed in what can serve as an insult or rejection that he intended only insult or rejection, such as: 'khaliyyah', 'bari'ah', 'battah', 'batlah', 'ba'in', 'haram' and the like; and he is not believed in what can serve only as a response, such as: 'I'taddi', 'Istabiri rahimaki', 'Anti wahidah', 'Ikhtari', 'Amruki bi yadik'. So what can serve only as a response are five, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. His saying: (and it is far-fetched) I say: it is supported by the explicit statement in al-Dhakhirah contrary to it: 'if he said to her Go and marry, divorce does not occur except with intention, and if he intends, she is one ba'in, and if he intends three, then three.' End. His saying: (Khalilah bari'ah etc.) is an example of his saying. Only. In the state of anger with request for divorce, two reasons combine for not believing him in a word that is purely a response: the discussion and anger. Similarly, in accepting his statement in what can serve as a rejection, because each of discussion and anger independently establishes acceptance of his statement in the claim of not intending divorce. In what can serve as a cause, anger alone establishes it, so the rulings do not change. By this it is known that the states are three: absolute state, state of discussing divorce, and state of anger. And that by 'absolute' is meant free from the two constraints of anger and discussion, so the commentator's saying that it is contentment is not appropriate. And that the kinayah are three categories: a category that serves as a response and does not serve as a rejection or insult; a category that serves as a response and rejection but does not serve as an insult; and a category that serves as a response and insult but does not serve as a rejection. From Abu Yusuf, regarding his saying 'No ownership for me over you', 'No way for me over you', 'I released your way', 'I separated from you', that he is believed in the state of anger because of the possibility of the meaning of a cause, as in al-Hidayah. Fakhr al-Islam and the author of al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyyah made these expressions, according to Abu Yusuf, attached to what serves as a response: namely I'taddi, Ikhtari, Amruki bi yadik. The author did not mention these details because al-Hakim al-Shahid in al-Kafi, which is a collection of Muhammad's statements in his books, did not mention it, and his commentator Imam al-Sarakhsi did not address it. The summary of what is in al-Khaniyyah is that among the kinayah, thirteen are not considered with indication of the situation and only occur with intention: 'Hablu 'ala gharibik', 'Taqanna'i', 'Takhammari', 'Istatiri', 'Qumi', 'Ukhruji', 'Idhhabi', 'Intaqili', 'Intaliqi', 'Tazawwaji', 'I'zabi', 'La nikaha li 'alayki', 'Wahabtuki li-ahlik'. Other than these, indication is considered. However, eight occur in the state of discussing divorce: 'Anti khaliyyah', 'bari'ah', 'battah', 'ba'in', 'haram', 'I'taddi', 'Amruki bi yadik', 'Ikhtari'. Three of these eight occur in the state of anger: 'I'taddi', 'Amruki bi yadik', 'Ikhtari'. Then he said after that: if he said in a discussion of divorce 'Faraqtuki', 'Bayantuki', or 'Bintu minki', 'La sultana li 'alayki', 'Sarrahtuki', 'Wahabtuki li-nafsiki', 'Taraktu talaqaki', 'Khallaytu sabili talaqaki', 'Sabili' or 'Anti ba'inah' or 'Anti hurrah' or 'Anti a'lamu bi-sha'niki' and she said 'I choose myself', divorce occurs, and if he said 'I did not intend divorce', he is not believed. End. Thus the words that occur in the state of discussing divorce amount to twenty words. And indeed ba'in occurs with other than the three and what is in their meaning, even though what is intended is divorce which is revocable? We deny that what is intended is divorce; rather it is binunah (irrevocability) because that word is used in insults, not for it, and because it does not serve for it. Refer to al-Nahr for more insight. His saying: (By this it is known that the states are three) He said in al-Nahr: In my view, the best is to restrict to the state of anger and discussion, since the discussion is about states that affect the indication, not absolutely. Then I saw in al-Bada'i' after he divided the states into three like the commentator, he said: In the state of contentment, he is believed judicially, but if he is in a state of discussing divorce or anger, they said: the kinayah are three categories, and he mentioned what preceded, and this is the verification. His saying: (A category that serves as a response) meaning a response to her request for divorce, i.e., divorcing.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage is a detailed expansion from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq on the chapter of kinayah (allusive words) in divorce. The author opens by listing a series of kinayah expressions that, if uttered in a context that indicates divorce (such as a discussion of divorce or anger), can effect divorce depending on intention. He then explains each term individually, citing various Hanafi authorities like Qadikhan, al-Mi'raj, al-Khulasa, and others. For each expression, he specifies whether divorce occurs with or without explicit intention, and under what conditions. For example, if a husband says 'Go and marry', the word 'marry' is a contextual indicator (qarina) that he intends divorce, so one divorce occurs without need for further intention. However, if he denies intent, he is not believed in certain states. The passage then shifts to a broader legal principle: the author of Kanz (al-Nasafi) held that all kinayah effect divorce by indication of the situation (dalalat al-hal), following al-Quduri and al-Sarakhsi. But Fakhr al-Islam and others disagreed, holding that some kinayah require explicit intention. The text then provides a refined rule dividing states into three: (1) state of mere contentment (rida), (2) state of discussing divorce (mudhakarat al-talaq), and (3) state of anger (ghadab). It explains how each state affects the acceptance of the husband's claim that he did not intend divorce. Kinayah are further classified into three categories based on whether they can serve as a response (jawab), a rejection (radd), or an insult (sabb). The passage also cites Abu Yusuf's view on certain expressions and concludes with a comprehensive list from al-Khaniyyah: thirteen kinayah that never effect divorce without intention, and eight that do in the context of discussing divorce, with three of those eight also operating in anger. The overall ruling (mufta bihi) appears to be the detailed position that distinguishes between words and states, as this is the most practical and widely accepted view among later Hanafi scholars.

Key Terms
كناية (kināyah): An allusive expression that does not explicitly mention divorce but implies it.
دلالة الحال (dalālat al-ḥāl): Contextual indication from the situation (anger, discussion of divorce).
مذاكرة الطلاق (mudhākarat al-ṭalāq): State where spouses discuss divorce, providing strong contextual indication.
الألفاظ التي لا تعتبر فيها دلالة الحال (al-alfāẓ allatī lā tuʿtabaru fīhā dalālat al-ḥāl): The thirteen words that never effect divorce by contextual indication; they always require intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The three-state classification and word-type categories provide a precise framework for determining the effect of kinayah in Hanafi law.

Talaq — Divorce

Classification of Kinayah in Divorce: Three States and Three Categories
Classification of kinayah into states and categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three states of the husband in kinayah divorce, classify any given kinayah phrase into one of three categories (response, rejection, insult), and explain how context (discussion/anger) affects the husband's claim of intent.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, the legal effect of a kinayah depends on the husband’s state (calm, discussing, angry) and the phrase’s semantic suitability.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between explicit divorce (sarih) and metaphorical expressions (kinayah). For kinayah, the husband's state of mind and the contextual suitability of the phrase determine whether his denial of intent is accepted. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq classifies the states into three (absolute, discussing, angry) and the expressions into three categories (suitable as response, rejection, or insult). Understanding this framework is essential for determining if a divorce actually occurred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three states of the husband
The husband may be in an absolute state (free from anger and discussion), a state of discussing divorce (mudhakarah), or a state of anger (ghadab). The 'absolute' means not constrained by the other two, not necessarily contentment.
The commentator in al-Nahr suggests confining to discussion and anger only, but al-Bada'i' confirms three.
2
Understand the two contextual factors that remove credibility
When the husband uses a kinayah that is purely a response to the wife's demand, both mudhakarah and ghadab combine to reject his claim of no intent. Each factor independently also removes credibility in certain categories.
For rejection categories, either factor alone suffices.
3
Classify kinayah into three categories based on suitability
Some kinayah are only suitable as a response to a demand (e.g., 'I take you back'?). Others can also be used as a rejection (e.g., 'You are free'). Still others can be an insult (e.g., 'Go to your family'). The category determines whether the husband's denial of intent is believed.
The text gives specific lists from al-Khaniyah for each category.
4
Map the interaction between state and category
In the absolute state, the husband's claim of no intent is believed in court. In discussion or anger, the category decides: for response-only kinayah, he is not believed; for rejection-suitable, he may be believed; for insult-suitable, his claim is rejected only in anger.
This is the thamarah (practical consequence) of the classification.
5
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf (Abu Yusuf's view)
Abu Yusuf holds that phrases like 'la malka li 'alayki' are kinayah in anger and the husband is believed due to probability of meaning. Other Hanafis consider them part of the response category, where he is not believed.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the three-state division from the majority.

Book of Divorce, Chapter on Kinayah in Divorce. In a state of anger, the one asked (about) divorce combines two reasons for not believing him when it is purely a response: discussing divorce and anger. Likewise in accepting his statement regarding what is suitable as a rejection, because each of discussing and anger independently establishes acceptance of his statement in claiming not intending divorce. And in what is suitable for the cause, anger alone establishes it, so the rulings do not change. His statement: (A category suitable as a response) meaning response to her request for divorce, i.e., the divorcing.
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a detailed commentary on the classification of kinayah (metaphorical expressions) in the chapter of divorce. It begins by establishing that in a state of anger, when the husband speaks a kinayah that is purely a response to the wife's demand, his claim of not intending divorce is rejected due to two combined factors: the context of discussing divorce (mudhakarah) and anger (ghadab). Each factor alone would suffice to reject his claim in some cases, but when both are present, the ruling is strengthened. The passage then identifies three states of the husband: (1) absolute state (hala mutlaqah) where he is calm, (2) state of discussing divorce, and (3) state of anger. The term 'absolute' here means free from anger and discussion, not necessarily contentment. The kinayah themselves fall into three categories based on their semantic suitability: those that can be used only as a response to a demand, those that can also be a rejection, and those that can also be an insult. This classification determines whether the husband's claim of no intent is believed. The text then reports a view of Abu Yusuf regarding specific phrases like 'there is no ownership for me over you,' etc., where he holds the husband is believed in anger due to the possibility of a different intended meaning. It also notes that later scholars like Fakhr al-Islam treat these phrases as analogous to the three that are always considered kinayah in anger: 'i'taddi', 'ikhtari', and 'amruki biyadik'. The author of al-Kafi omitted these details, so the commentator fills the gap. A list of thirteen kinayah that never consider context and require explicit intention is given from al-Khaniyah. Then eight kinayah that become effective during discussion are listed, of which three also work during anger. The total list for discussion state reaches twenty phrases. The passage concludes with a theoretical point: these kinayah effect a ba'in divorce, not a raj'i divorce, because the metonymized meaning is separation (baynunah) not simply divorce. The commentary in al-Nahr suggests limiting the classification to anger and discussion only, but al-Bada'i' confirms the three-state division.

Key Terms
كناية (kināyah): An expression that is not explicit for divorce but implies it indirectly, requiring intention to effect divorce.
المذاكرة (al-mudhākarah): The context of discussing divorce between spouses, which affects the interpretation of kinayah.
الغضب (al-ghaḍab): The state of anger, which influences the legal effect of kinayah, making intention irrelevant in some cases.
بائن (bā’in): A divorce that is irrevocable, where the husband cannot take back the wife without a new marriage.
جواب (jawāb): In this context, a phrase that is suitable as a response to the wife's demand for divorce.
رد (radd): A phrase suitable as a rejection of the wife's demand (e.g., 'I won't divorce you').
شتم (shatm): A phrase suitable as an insult (e.g., 'Go away').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which state does the husband's claim of no intent in a kinayah that is purely a response become rejected due to two combined reasons?
A) State of contentment
B) State of discussing divorce
C) State of anger
D) State of absolute (hala mutlaqah)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of kinayah into three states and three categories provides a nuanced framework for when a metaphorical divorce expression is effective, balancing linguistic suitability and contextual factors.

Linguistic Nature of Kinayah: Haqiqah or Majaz
Debate on whether divorce kinayah are literal or figurative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on whether divorce kinayah are used in their real or metaphorical sense, and distinguish between the views of al-Hidayah, al-Tahrir, and al-Tanqih.

Core Idea
Kinayah are real for separation (baynunah), not metaphor for divorce — divorce is their necessary consequence.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a fundamental linguistic-legal question: are terms like 'ba'in' used in divorce contexts literal or metaphorical? The answer determines how intention operates. We'll trace the debate among Hanafi authorities and understand why the preferred view holds that kinayah are real in their meaning of separation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Determine whether divorce kinayah are used as haqiqah (literal) or majaz (metaphorical).
This debate affects the role of intention and the type of divorce effected.
2
Understand key terms
Define haqiqah, majaz, kinayah, baynunah, and lazim in the Hanafi legal context.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Follow the argument
Trace the passage's reasoning: kinayah are real for baynunah; divorce is a necessary consequence (lazim).
The author refutes the notion that kinayah must be metaphorical.
4
Trace the dispute
Compare views of al-Hidayah (they are not real kinayah), al-Tahrir (they are real), and al-Tanqih (they are majaz).
The commentator prefers the view of al-Tahrir and al-Talwih.
5
Determine mufta bihi
Identify the relied-upon position and its practical consequence.
The mufta bihi: kinayah effect a ba'in divorce with intention of separation.

Husbands—and if he said to her 'i'taddi (observe waiting period) thrice' and intended divorce with the first and menstruation with the rest, he is believed, even if not (specifying) individuals. So its being a kinayah does not necessitate its being a metaphor (majaz) for divorce, because it is a semantic homonym of the graded type, so severance related to marriage is a type of what it relates to, and what relates to good and evil similarly, if its specification is not mentioned, just as 'man' can mean both Zayd and 'Amr and others. And baynunah (separation) is divided into severe (heavy), which results from three divorces, and light, as results from khul'. Whichever he intended is valid and what is established by the word 'taliq' (divorced) in exchange for property and 'taliq thrice' is established. Because the conclusion is that what is established legally by 'taliq' is a broader necessary consequence that is established by it and by these words and khul'. So our saying 'divorce occurs by it' means the legal necessary consequence of the word 'taliq' occurs, and the deficiency in its number is by the multiplicity of occurrence of that consequence and its completion thereby and by uttering the word 'three'. Rather, the meaning of occurrence of divorce is the occurrence of the consequence of the word 'divorce'. So what occurs by kinayah is divorce without interpretation (ta'wil). By this it is evident that the meaning of applying the name 'kinayah' (to these) is real (haqiqah). So the statement of the author of al-Hidayah, 'they are not real kinayah because they are real in their literal meanings,' was said in al-Tahrir to be a mistake, because it implies that reality contradicts kinayah, but it is not so, for kinayah can be real because of multiplicity of meaning, and can be not real in them. And their statement that the kinayah in which the intended meaning is hidden—these are known and the hesitation is regarding what is intended by them—are (phrases like) 'ba'in' for goodness or marriage. It was said in al-Tahrir that this is negated because the kinayah is due to hesitation about the intended referent, not due to hesitation about the assigned meaning, like a homonym or a specific in a particular individual. If it were a real kinayah, then the metaphor (majaz) would be in its attribution to divorce, for what is understood from attribution is that it is a kinayah for it, but it is not so, otherwise it would be... (text mutilated). In al-Hidayah, the condition is specifying one of the two types of baynunah, not divorce. Its apparent meaning is that intention of divorce is not considered in ba'in kinayah, and that intention of separation (baynunah) of marriage is necessary. In al-Tanqih, they said: 'And kinayah of divorce are used metaphorically because their meanings are not hidden, but the ambiguity is in what is connected to them, like 'ba'in' for example, which is ambiguous whether it means separated from marriage or something else. When he intends one type, it becomes specified and clarified by the requirements of the speech. If it were made a real kinayah, it would effect a raj'i divorce, because they interpreted it as that whose intended meaning is hidden, and the hidden intended here is divorce, so it becomes like his saying 'anti taliq'.' And according to the interpretation of the scholars of rhetoric (bayan), they do not need this trouble, because in their view a kinayah is that you mention a word and intend by its meaning a second meaning that is a necessary consequence of it, so by 'ba'in' its meaning is intended, then by the intention of divorce one moves from it, so she is divorced with the quality of baynunah, not that divorce was intended from it. The completion is in al-Talwih. And it is not hidden from you that his saying 'anti wahidah' (you are one) is not from the category of kinayah according to the scholars of rhetoric, but it is of the deleted type, yet it is a kinayah by virtue of the hidden intention. That is in al-Talwih. And the author restricted it with these words. His statement: (In al-Tanqih they said... its summary is that applying the term 'kinayah' to divorce kinayah is metaphorical based on the usuliyyun's interpretation that the intended meaning is hidden, and this is based on the assumption that the hidden is divorce. This is opposite to what was stated earlier that they are real kinayah based on denying that the metonymized is divorce and that it is baynunah.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq delves into the linguistic and legal nature of kinayah in divorce, specifically whether the terms used (like 'ba'in') are real (haqiqah) or metaphorical (majaz) when referring to divorce. The commentator presents a nuanced discussion involving multiple Hanafi authorities. He begins with an example: if a husband says 'i'taddi thalathan' (observe waiting period thrice) intending divorce with the first and menstruation with the rest, he is believed, showing that kinayah does not automatically imply metaphor. The key argument is that terms like 'ba'in' are not metaphors for divorce because divorce is a necessary consequence of separation; they are real terms for the type of separation (baynunah). The commentator refutes the view from al-Hidayah that such kinayah are not real kinayah because they have real literal meanings. He references al-Tahrir, which calls this a mistake, as kinayah can be real (e.g., homonyms with multiple meanings). The text then distinguishes between hesitation about the intended referent (which makes a kinayah) versus hesitation about the assigned meaning (which makes a homonym). The relied-upon view from al-Tanqih is that kinayah of divorce are used metaphorically because their literal meanings are clear but the ambiguity is about what they are connected to (e.g., ba'in from marriage or from something else). If they were real kinayah, they would effect a raj'i divorce, but the correct view is that they effect ba'in divorce. The scholars of bayan (rhetoric) have a different definition: a kinayah is a word whose literal meaning is intended but from which a secondary necessary meaning is transferred by intention. Under this definition, 'ba'in' is a real kinayah for separation, and the divorce is achieved through intention. The passage concludes by noting that 'anti wahidah' (you are one) is not a kinayah in the rhetorical sense but is treated as such due to the hidden intention. This complex discussion shows the Hanafi school's careful analysis of language and intent in divorce law.

Key Terms
حقيقة (ḥaqīqah): The real or literal meaning of a word; in this context, whether kinayah are used in their literal sense for separation.
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical usage; here, some argue kinayah are figurative for divorce.
لازم (lāzim): Necessary consequence; divorce is the legal necessary consequence of separation (baynunah).
بينونة (baynūnah): Separation, the state of being separated from marriage; the metonymized meaning in ba'in kinayah.
كناية (kināyah): An indirect expression where the intended meaning is hidden; in divorce context, words that imply separation but require intention for legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in the passage, what is the metonymized meaning (makni 'anhu) in a kinayah like 'ba'in'?
A) Divorce (talaq)
B) Separation (baynunah)
C) Anger (ghadab)
D) The waiting period ('iddah)

Key Takeaway
Kinayah in divorce are real in their literal meaning of separation; divorce is their legal consequence, not their direct metaphorical meaning.

Talaq — Divorce

Nature of Kinayat in Talaq: Haqiqah or Majaz?
Dispute over classification of divorce allusions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on whether divorce kinayat are haqiqah or majaz and state the relied-upon view regarding intention.

Core Idea
Divorce kinayat are haqiqah in bainunah; intention must target separation from marriage, not merely the word 'talaq'.

This passage explores a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school: are kinayat (allusive words) for divorce considered literal (haqiqah) or figurative (majaz)? The answer determines what a husband must intend when using such words. The passage analyzes definitions from al-Hidayah, al-Tahrir, al-Tanqih, and scholars of bayan to resolve the issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central question
Are kinayat like 'ba'in' literal or figurative in the context of divorce? The word 'ba'in' means separation, but the legal question is whether it directly signifies divorce or merely alludes to it.
2
Distinguish lexical meaning from intended referent
The word 'ba'in' has a clear meaning of severance, but the hesitation is about what it severs from (marriage, good, etc.). This hesitation is about the referent, not the lexical meaning.
This distinction is key to the Hanafi analysis.
3
Analyze al-Hidayah's stance
Al-Hidayah claims these words are not kinayat because they act upon their real meanings. This view implies that they are haqiqah and therefore not allusions.
4
Understand al-Tahrir's critique
Al-Tahrir refutes al-Hidayah, arguing that haqiqah and kinayah are not contradictory; a word can be both if it has multiple meanings. The hesitation here is about the intended particular of a general meaning.
This is the more accepted view.
5
Synthesize the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position is that kinayat are haqiqah for bainunah (separation) and majaz only in their attribution to talaq. Therefore, the husband must intend bainunah from marriage, not just the word 'talaq'.
Intention of bainunah is necessary; intention of talaq alone is insufficient.

Source Text
