الأفراد، فكونها كناية لا تستلزم كونها مجازاً عن الطلاق لأنه مشترك معنوي من قبيل المشكك، فالقطع المتعلق بالنكاح فرد من نوع ما يتعلق به والمتعلق بالخير والشر كذلك إذا لم يذكر متعقله كما يحتمل رجل كلاً من زيد وعمرو وغيرهما والبينونة متنوعة إلى غليطة وهي المترتبة على الثلاث وخفيفة كالمترتبة على الخلع، وأيهما أراد صح وثبت ما يثبت بلفظ طالق على مال وطالق ثلاثاً . وحاصله أن ما يثبت عند طالق شرعاً لازم أعم يثبت عنده وعند هذه الألفاظ والخلع. فقولنا يقع بها الطلاق معناه يقع لازم لفظ الطلاق شرعاً، وانتقاص عدده هو بتعدد وقوع ذلك اللازم واستكماله بذلك وبإرسال لفظ الثلاث بل معنى وقوع الطلاق وقوع اللازم لفظ الطلاق، فالواقع بالكناية هو الطلاق بلا تأويل. وبهذا ظهر أن هو معنى إطلاق اسم الكناية حقيقة فقول صاحب الهداية ليست كنايات على التحقيق لأنها عوامل في حقائقها قال في التحرير: إنه غلط لأنه يدل على أنه الحقيقة تنافي الكناية وليس كذلك لأن الكناية قد تكون حقيقة لأنها بتعدد المعنى، وقد لا تكون حقيقة فيها. وقولهم إن الكناية التي تكون مستتر المراد وهذه معلومة والتردد فيما يراد بها هي أبائن من الخير أو النكاح. قال في التحرير: إنه منتف بأن الكناية بسبب التردد في المراد لا بسبب التردد في المعنى الموضوع كالمشترك والخاص في فرد معين فإذا كانت كناية على الحقيقة تعين أن يكون المجاز في إضافتها إلى الطلاق فإن المفهوم من الإضافة أنها كناية عنه وليس كذلك وإلا وفع رجيعاً. وفي الهداية والشرط تعيين أحد نوعي البينونة دون الطلاق ا هـ . وظاهره أنه لا اعتبار بنية الطلاق في الكنايات البوائن، وأنه لا بد من نية بينونة النكاح وفي التنقيح قالوا : وكنايات الطلاق تطلق مجازاً لأن معانيها غير مستترة لكن الإبهام فيما يتصل بها كالبائن مثلاً فإنه مبهم في أنها بائنة عن أي شيء عن النكاح أو غيره، فإذا نوى نوعاً منها تعين وتبين بموجب الكلام، ولو جعلت كناية حقيقة تطلق رجعية لأنهم فسروها بما يستتر المراد منه والمراد المستتر هنا الطلاق فيصير كقوله أنت طالق». وبتفسير علماء البيان لا يحتاجون إلى هذا التكلف لأنها عندهم أن يذكر لفظ ويقصد بمعناه معنى ثانٍ ملزوم له، فيراد بالبائن معناه ثم ينتقل منه بنية إلى الطلاق فتطلق على صفة البينونة لا أنه أريد به الطلاق، وتمامه في التلويح ولا يخفى عليك أن قوله أنت واحدة ليس من باب الكناية بتفسير علماء البيان ولكنه من قبيل المحذوف لكنه كناية باعتبار استتار المراد. كذا في التلويح . وقيد المصنف بهذه
[The fact that they are] individuals, so being a kinayah does not necessitate being a metaphor for divorce because it is a common term of the type of mushakkik. The severance related to marriage is an individual of the type of what is related to it, and the severance related to good and evil is likewise when its accompanying [context] is not mentioned, just as 'man' can apply to each of Zayd and Amr and others. And bainunah is divided into severe, which results from three [divorces], and light, like that resulting from khul'. Whichever one he intended is valid, and what is established by the word 'taliq' with wealth and 'taliq' three times is established. The gist is that what is established by 'taliq' legally is a necessary general [consequence] that is established by it and by these words and khul'. So our saying 'divorce occurs by it' means that the necessary consequence of the word 'taliq' legally occurs, and the reduction of its number is by the multiplicity of the occurrence of that necessary consequence, and its completion is by that and by sending the word 'three'. Rather, the meaning of the occurrence of divorce is the occurrence of the necessary consequence of the word 'taliq'. So what occurs by kinayah is divorce without interpretation. And by this it becomes clear that this is the meaning of applying the name kinayah as a reality. So the statement of the author of al-Hidayah: 'They are not kinayat according to verification because they act upon their realities.' [Al-Tahrir] said: 'It is a mistake because it indicates that reality contradicts kinayah, and it is not so because kinayah can be real (haqiqah) because it [involves] multiplicity of meaning, and it can be not real in it. Their saying that kinayah is that whose intended meaning is hidden, and these are known, and the hesitation is about what is intended by them: they are 'ba'in from good or marriage.' [Al-Tahrir] said: 'It is refuted because kinayah is due to hesitation about the intended meaning, not due to hesitation about the lexical meaning, like the common term (mushtarak) and the specific for a particular individual. If it is a kinayah in reality, then necessarily the metaphor is in its attribution to divorce, because the understanding from the attribution is that it is a kinayah for it, and it is not so, otherwise it would occur as raj'i.' And in al-Hidayah: 'The condition is the specification of one of the two types of bainunah, not divorce.' End. Apparently, it gives no weight to the intention of divorce in the bainunah kinayat, and that intention of bainunah of the marriage is necessary. And in al-Tanqih they said: 'Kinayat of divorce are used metaphorically because their meanings are not hidden, but the ambiguity is in what is attached to them, like ba'in for example, for it is ambiguous whether it is ba'in from something, from marriage or other. So if he intends one of its types, it is specified and becomes clear according to the import of the speech. If made a real kinayah, it would occur as raj'i because they interpreted it as that whose intended meaning is hidden, and the hidden intended here is divorce, so it becomes like his saying 'You are divorced'. And by the interpretation of the scholars of bayan, they do not need this difficulty because according to them, it is that a word is mentioned and by its meaning a second meaning that is necessary for it is intended. So by 'ba'in' its meaning is intended, then from it by intention one moves to divorce, so it occurs with the description of bainunah, not that divorce is intended. Its completion is in al-Talwih. And it is not hidden from you that his saying 'You are one' is not from the kinayah according to the interpretation of the scholars of bayan, but it is of the type of omission, but it is kinayah in consideration of the hidden intended. Thus in al-Talwih. And the author restricted by this.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage is a definition and dispute analysis. It opens by stating that kinayat being individuals does not mean they are metaphorical for divorce. The key point: the word 'bainunah' (separation) has both severe (from triple divorce) and light (from khul') types. When a husband uses a kinayah word like 'ba'in', the divorce takes effect without interpretation because the intended meaning is the necessary legal consequence of divorce, not the literal divorce itself. The text then presents a disagreement: al-Hidayah says these are not true kinayat because they operate on their real meanings, but al-Tahrir argues this is wrong because kinayah can be haqiqah (real) when it has multiple meanings. The dispute hinges on whether the hesitation is in the lexical meaning or the intended referent. The author favors the view that these are haqiqah in the sense of bainunah, and the metaphor is in attributing them to divorce. He then quotes al-Tanqih saying kinayat are metaphorical because their meanings are clear, but the ambiguity is about what they are bain from. Finally, he mentions the interpretation of scholars of bayan (rhetoric), who consider kinayah as a word used with its meaning then intended for a necessary second meaning, so 'ba'in' is used for its literal meaning then transferred to divorce by intention. The passage ends with a note that 'You are one' is not a kinayah by this definition but by omission. The author's restriction refers to the specific words discussed earlier.

Key Terms
كناية (kinaayah): A word or phrase that alludes to something else indirectly; in divorce, words that do not explicitly say 'talaq' but imply it.
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal meaning; a word used in its original sense.
بينونة (bainunah): Separation or severance from marriage; the consequence of divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must intend the word 'talaq' itself.
B) He must intend bainunah (separation) from marriage.
C) He must intend a specific number of divorces.
D) He does not need any intention; the word itself is clear.

Key Takeaway
Kinayat for divorce are haqiqah for bainunah; the husband must intend separation from marriage, not simply the word 'talaq'.

Rulings on Specific Kinayah Phrases in Divorce
Enumeration and parsing of specific allusive divorce phrases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi rulings to distinguish which allusive phrases effect divorce (with or without intention) and which do not, citing the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Not all allusive statements effect divorce; each phrase has its own rule, and intention alone is not always decisive.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq enumerates numerous phrases that husbands (or wives) might use in the context of divorce. Some phrases are so explicit that they effect divorce without any intention; others require clear intent; and still others never effect divorce regardless of the speaker's intent. We will parse each phrase to understand its precise ruling according to the Hanafi school, noting any ikhtilaf and identifying the operative (mufta bihi) position.

Guided Path
1
Classify the phrase
Determine from the text whether the phrase falls into one of three categories: (1) never effects divorce, (2) effects divorce only with intention, (3) effects divorce without intention.
The passage often uses 'لا يقع' or 'يقع' with conditions.
2
Identify the basic ruling and any ikhtilaf
For each phrase, identify the ruling according to Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, or others cited (Ibn Abi Layla, Ibn Salam). Note where there is disagreement within the madhhab.
The text often mentions multiple views; look for 'وعن أبي حنيفة' or 'وعن محمد'.
3
Determine the mufta bihi
From the conflicting statements, identify which position is relied upon for fatwa, as indicated by expressions like 'هو الظاهر' or the default position of the text.
The author often ends with 'وهو الظاهر' or gives the view of later compilations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الألفاظ للاحتراز عما إذا قال لا حاجة لي فيك أو لا أريدك أو لا أحبك أو لا أشتهيك أو لا رغبة لي فيك فإنه لا يقع وإن نوى في قول أبي حنيفة. وقال ابن أبي ليلى: يقع في قوله لا حاجة لي فيك إذا نوى. وفي التفاريق عن ابن سلام يكون ثلاثاً إذا نوى، ولو قال فسخت النكاح ونوى الطلاق يقع، وعن أبي حنيفة إن نوى ثلاثاً فثلاث، والرواية هكذا محمد أنه عن بائن إن نوى الطلاق . وفي جمع برهان قال لم يبق بيني وبينك عمل ونوى الطلاق لا يقع . وفي فتاوى الفضلى خلافه وفي التفاريق قيل في قوله لم يبق بيني وبينك شيء أنه لا ولو قال أربعة طرق عليك مفتوحة لا يقع وإن نوى ما لم يقل خذي إلى أي طريق شئت. وفي اللآلي وهكذا عن محمد وفي النظم قال أسد قال محمد يقع ثلاثاً. وقال ابن سلام: أخاف أن يقع ثلاثاً لمعاني كلام الناس . وفي المبسوط : قال لها أنت علي كالميتة أو كلحم الخنزير أو الخمر ونوى الطلاق يقع كذا في المعراج وفي البزازية : طلبت منه الطلاق فقال لم يبق بيني وبينك عمل لم تطلق إلا أن ينوي به النكاح وينوي به إيقاع الطلاق فحينئذ يقع . وذكر في البدائع من النكايات خالعتك لا على سبيل العوض وسيأتي. وفي البزازية : أنا بريء منك لا يقع وإن نوى، ولو قال أبرأتك عن الزوجية يقع بلا نية ا هـ. وفي تلخيص الجامع وشرحه : لو قالت أبنت نفسي أو حرمت نفسي عليك فقال أجزت وقع بائناً بشرط أن ينوي كل منهما الطلاق وتصح نية الثلاث، ولو قالت اخترت نفسي فقال أجزت ناوياً الطلاق لا يقع وسنذكره بتمامه في فصل الاختيار. وفي الخانية : أنا بريء من طلاقك لا يكون طلاقاً، ولو قال برئت إليك من طلاقك يقع، نوى أو لم ينو. ولو قال أنا بريء من ثلاث تطليقات قال بعضهم يقع الطلاق، وقال بعضهم لا يقع وإن نوى وهو الظاهر ا هـ .
The words [mentioned] are to exclude when he says: 'I have no need of you', or 'I do not want you', or 'I do not love you', or 'I do not desire you', or 'I have no inclination for you' – then it does not occur [as divorce] even if he intends, according to Abu Hanifa. Ibn Abi Layla said: it occurs in his statement 'I have no need of you' if he intends. And in al-Tafariq from Ibn Salam: it would be three if he intends. And if he said 'I dissolved the marriage' and intended divorce, it occurs; and from Abu Hanifa: if he intended three then three, and the narration from Muhammad is that it is a bain [divorce] if he intended divorce. And in Jami' Burhan: he said 'there is no work between me and you' and intended divorce, it does not occur. And in Fatawa al-Fudla, the opposite. And in al-Tafariq, it was said in his statement 'nothing remains between me and you' that it does not [occur]. And if he said 'four ways are open to you' it does not occur even if he intends, unless he says 'take whichever way you wish'. And in al-La'ali: and thus from Muhammad; and in al-Nadhm, Asad said: Muhammad said it occurs as three. And Ibn Salam said: I fear that it occurs as three due to the meanings of people's speech. And in al-Mabsut: if he said to her 'you are like carrion, or like pork, or wine' and intended divorce, it occurs. Thus in al-Mi'raj. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: she solicited divorce from him, and he said 'there is no work between me and you', she is not divorced unless he intends by it [dissolution of] the marriage and intends to effect divorce, then it occurs. And it is mentioned in al-Bada'i' from the kinayat: 'I divorced you without consideration' and it will come. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: 'I am free from you' does not occur even if he intends, and if he said 'I made you free from marriage' it occurs without intention. End. And in Talkhis al-Jami' and its commentary: if she said 'I have separated myself' or 'I have made myself forbidden to you' and he said 'I agree', a bain divorce occurs on condition that each of them intends divorce, and intention of three is valid. And if she said 'I choose myself' and he said 'I agree' intending divorce, it does not occur, and we will mention it in full in the chapter of choice. And in al-Khaniyyah: 'I am free from your divorce' is not divorce, and if he said 'I am quit to you from your divorce' it occurs, whether he intended or not. And if he said 'I am free from three divorces', some said divorce occurs, and some said it does not occur even if he intends, and that is the apparent. End.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of specific rulings on various allusive phrases used by husbands or wives regarding divorce. It lists many phrases and gives the Hanafi ruling, often with differences among the imams. The teacher should note: The general rule according to Abu Hanifa is that phrases like 'I have no need of you' do not effect divorce even with intention, because they are not direct allusions. However, others like Ibn Abi Layla allow it for 'I have no need of you' with intention. The passage also covers phrases like 'I dissolved the marriage' – which does effect divorce with intention, and if he intends three, it counts as three. There are reports from Muhammad that it is a bain divorce. Some phrases like 'there is no work between me and you' have a difference: some say it does not occur, others say it does if he intends divorce. The phrase 'four ways are open to you' only effects divorce if he says 'take whichever way you wish' (which is an explicit command). The phrase 'you are like carrion, pork, wine' effects divorce if he intends. The passage also covers situations where the wife initiates and the husband agrees, like 'I have separated myself' requiring mutual intention. Overall, clarity: each phrase has its own ruling; not all kinayah words are treated equally. The relied-upon positions are given in the last part: 'I am free from you' does not effect divorce even with intention, but 'I made you free from marriage' does without intention.

Key Terms
لا حاجة لي فيك (la hajata li fiki): Phrase meaning 'I have no need of you'; considered by Abu Hanifa as not a kinayah for divorce.
فسخت النكاح (fasakhtu al-nikah): Phrase 'I dissolved the marriage'; a kinayah that effects a bain divorce if intended.
أبرأتك عن الزوجية (abratuki 'an al-zawjiyyah): Phrase 'I made you free from marriage'; effects divorce without intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a man says 'I am free from you' (أنا بريء منك) with the intention of divorce?
A) It effects a single raj'i divorce.
B) It effects a bain divorce.
C) It does not effect divorce.
D) It effects three divorces.

Key Takeaway
Each kinayah phrase in talaq has its own specific ruling; intention alone does not make an ordinary expression a valid divorce.

Intention and Context in the Phrase 'I'taddi Thalathan'
Detailed analysis of the phrase 'observe three iddahs' and the effect of different intentions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the phrase 'i'taddi thalathan' and explain how intention and context affect the number of divorces.

Core Idea
The ambiguous 'three iddahs' becomes three divorces when the context of divorce is established, else one if menses intended.

When a husband uses ambiguous words, his intention can determine the outcome—but only if consistent with the context. This lesson examines the phrase 'i'taddi thalathan' (observe three iddahs), which could refer to three menstrual periods or three divorces. The Hanafi school has detailed rules about when the word 'three' counts as one divorce or three, based on the husband's intention and the presence of a divorce context (mudhakarat al-talaq). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correct fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous phrase
Recognise that 'i'taddi thalathan' is ambiguous: it could mean observe iddah of three menstrual periods or observe iddah of three divorces.
The word 'thalathan' (three) is the key ambiguous term.
2
Distinguish the two main scenarios
If the husband intends the remaining words as menses, he is believed and only one divorce occurs. If he intends nothing, the context of divorce (mudhakarat al-talaq) turns the rest into divorces, resulting in three.
The intention for the first word as divorce establishes the context.
3
Understand the role of qarina (indication)
Once the context of divorce is established, it acts as a qarina specifying the ambiguous words. This is based on qiyas to explicit divorce statements.
The qiyas panel explains the analogy.
4
Apply to subcases
The passage lists six subcases under the first scenario (only one divorce) and several under the second (three divorces). Work through each to see the pattern.
Memorising the subcases is less important than grasping the principle.
5
Analyse the Fath al-Qadir quote
The final part discusses the phrase 'follow the four ways'. People intend all four ways, but the wording commands only one, so the likely ruling is one ba'in divorce.
This shows how the same principle applies to other ambiguous phrases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قال لها اعتدي ثلاثاً ونوى بالأولى طلاقاً وبما بقي حيضاً صدق وإن لم ينو بما بقي شيئاً فهي ثلاث لأنه بنية الحيض بالباقي نوى حقيقة كلامه، وبنية الأولى طلاقاً صار الحال حال مذاكرة الطلاق فتعين الباقيتان للطلاق بهذه الدلالة فلا يصدق في نفي النية قضاء، وبهذا علم أن مذاكرة الطلاق لا تنحصر في سؤال الطلاق بل أعم منه ومن تقدم الإيقاع. ودخل تحت المسألة الأولى ما إذا نوى بكل منهما حيضاً فتطلق واحدة وهي الأولى، وما إذا نوى بالثالثة طلاقاً لا غير، وما إذا نوى بالثالثة حيضاً لا غير وما إذا نوى بالثانية طلاقاً وبالثالثة حيضاً لا غير، وما إذا نوى بالثانية والثالثة حيضاً، ففي هذه الست لا تقع إلا واحدة. ودخل تحت المسألة الثانية ما إذا نوى بالأولى حيضاً لا غير، أو الأوليين طلاقاً لا غير، أو الأولى والثالثة طلاقاً لا غير، أو الثانية والثالثة طلاقاً وبالأولى حيضاً، أو كل من قوله: المعاني كلام الناس قال في فتح القدير : كأنه يريد أن مراد الناس بمثله اسلكي الطرق الأربعة وإلا فاللفظ إنما يعطي الأمر بسلوك أحدهما والأوجه أن تقع واحدة بائنة ا هـ .
And if he said to her 'Observe three iddahs' and he intended by the first [word?] divorce and by the rest menses, he is believed [in his intention] . And if he did not intend anything by the rest, then it is three [divorces], because by his intention of menses for the remaining [words] he intended the reality of his speech, and by his intention of divorce for the first, the situation becomes a situation of discussing divorce, so the remaining two become specified for divorce by this indication. So he is not believed in denying the intention judicially. And by this it is known that discussing divorce is not limited to asking for divorce, but is more general than that and than the prior occurrence [of divorce]. And under the first issue falls: when he intends menses by each of them, then one divorce occurs, which is the first; and when he intends divorce only by the third; and when he intends menses only by the third; and when he intends divorce by the second and menses only by the third; and when he intends menses by the second and third. In these six [cases], only one occurs. And under the second issue falls: when he intends menses only by the first, or divorce only by the first two, or divorce only by the first and third, or divorce by the second and third and menses by the first, or all of his statement: 'the meanings of people's speech.' [Fath al-Qadir] said: 'It is as if he intended that people's intended meaning by such [phrases] is take the four ways, otherwise the wording only gives the command to take one of them, and the likely view is that one bain divorce occurs.' End.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. The scenario: a husband says to his wife 'I'taddi thalathan' (Observe three iddahs). The word 'thalathan' (three) could refer to three menstrual periods or three divorces. The ruling has two main branches: (1) If he intends the first (of the three) as divorce and the remaining two as menses, he is believed (his intention is accepted) because he intended the reality of the word for the menses and the context of divorce for the first. (2) If he intends nothing by the remaining two, then it counts as three divorces because once he intends divorce by the first, the context becomes one of discussing divorce, so the remaining two automatically become divorces. The passage then lists six subcases under the first branch where only one divorce occurs, and then lists subcases under the second branch where more than one occurs. The final part discusses the phrase 'arba'u turuq alayki maftuhah' noting that people's intended meaning is to take the four ways, but the words only command taking one, so the likely ruling is one bain divorce. The teacher should emphasize the distinction between the two main cases and the detailed subcases, and the principle that once the context of divorce is established, other words become specified as divorce.

Key Terms
مذاكرة الطلاق (mudhakarat al-talaq): Discussing or bringing up the topic of divorce; a context that indicates divorce is being considered.
اعتد ثلاثاً (i'taddi thalathan): A command to observe three iddahs; ambiguous between three menstrual periods or three divorces.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'I'taddi thalathan' intending the first as divorce and the remaining two as menses, what is the result?
A) Three divorces occur.
B) One divorce occurs and he is believed.
C) No divorce occurs.
D) One bain divorce occurs.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'i'taddi thalathan' yields one or three divorces based on the husband's intention and the presence of a divorce context.

Legal Effect of 'I'taddi Thalathan' – 24 Scenarios and Muthakarah al-Talaq
Expands the ruling on 'I'taddi thalathan' with 24 scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the 24 scenarios of saying 'I'taddi thalathan' based on the husband's intention and apply the Hanafi principle of muthakarah al-talaq to determine the number of divorces that occur.

Core Idea
One divorce intention creates a 'discussion of divorce' context, limiting the husband's judicial credibility about later words.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq systematically enumerates every possible combination of intentions when a husband commands his wife to observe three waiting periods (i'taddi thalathan). Understanding these scenarios reveals how Hanafi fiqh balances the husband's inner intention with contextual legal presumptions, leading to a precise classification of whether one, two, three, or no divorces occur.

Guided Path
1
Understand the base ruling
Identify that the husband says 'I'taddi thalathan' (three words). The ruling hinges on his intention for each word: divorce, menstrual waiting (hayd), or nothing.
The phrase is a kinayah; divorce occurs only with intention.
2
Define muthakarah al-talaq
Recognize that if the husband intends divorce with any one word, the entire context becomes a 'state of discussing divorce' (muthakarah al-talaq). This creates a legal presumption that subsequent words also relate to divorce, unless a valid alternative intention (like hayd) is proven.
This presumption is rebuttable but affects judicial (qada') credibility, not religious (diyanah).
3
Categorize the three main groups of outcomes
Group the scenarios: (1) Only one divorce occurs (e.g., when hayd is intended for all three, or specific combinations with only one divorce intention). (2) Three divorces occur (when divorce is intended for three words). (3) Two divorces occur (various mixed intentions). The text also covers scenarios where nothing occurs (no intention for any word).
The text explicitly calculates 24 scenarios through combinatorial logic.
4
Enumerate the 24 scenarios using the text's systematic breakdown
Follow the method: start with the base cases (all hayd, all divorce, no intention, first hayd then two divorce, etc.). Then consider permutations where one word is hayd only, or mixed intentions, leading to 24 unique cases.
The text uses a method of counting forms (suwar) to ensure completeness.
5
Apply the key rule: effect of muthakarah on judicial belief
Once muthakarah is established by a divorce intention, the husband is not believed in court if he claims no intention for later words. However, he is believed if he claims a plausible intention (like hayd) because it is a natural expectation after divorce. Also, the husband is believed in religious (diyanah) matters with his true intention.
The text clarifies that judicial belief requires an oath; religious truth is known to Allah.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الباقيتان للطلاق بهذه الدلالة فلا يصدق في نفي النية قضاء، وبهذا علم أن مذاكرة الطلاق لا تنحصر في سؤال الطلاق بل أعم منه ومن تقدم الإيقاع. ودخل تحت المسألة الأولى ما إذا نوى بكل منهما حيضاً فتطلق واحدة وهي الأولى، وما إذا نوى بالثالثة طلاقاً لا غير، وما إذا نوى بالثالثة حيضاً لا غير وما إذا نوى بالثانية طلاقاً وبالثالثة حيضاً لا غير، وما إذا نوى بالثانية والثالثة حيضاً، ففي هذه الست لا تقع إلا واحدة. ودخل تحت المسألة الثانية ما إذا نوى بالأولى حيضاً لا غير، أو الأوليين طلاقاً لا غير، أو الأولى والثالثة طلاقاً لا غير، أو الثانية والثالثة طلاقاً وبالأولى حيضاً، أو كل من قوله: المعاني كلام الناس قال في فتح القدير : كأنه يريد أن مراد الناس بمثله اسلكي الطرق الأربعة وإلا فاللفظ إنما يعطي الأمر بسلوك أحدهما والأوجه أن تقع واحدة بائنة ا هـ. الألفاظ طلاقاً فهذه ست تقع بها الثلاث. وخرج عن ضبطها أنه لا يخلو إما أن ينوي بالكل حيضاً، أو بالكل طلاقاً. أو لم ينو بالكل شيئاً، أو بالأولى حيضاً وبالباقيتين طلاقاً، أو بالأولى حيضاً لا غير، أو بالأولى حيضاً وبالثاني طلاقاً لا غير، أو بالأولى حيضاً وبالثالث طلاقاً لا غير. فإذا نوى الحيض بالأولى فقط فله أربع صور . وإذا نوى بالثاني الحيض فقط فله أربع أخرى، وإذا نوى بالثالث فقط فله أربع أخرى فصارت اثنتى عشرة، أو ينوي بالأول والثاني حيضاً وبالثالث طلاقاً، أو لم ينو بالثالث شيئاً، أو ينوي بالثاني والثالث حيضاً وبالأول طلاقاً، أو لم ينو بالأول شيئاً صارت ست عشرة، أو ينوي بالأول والثالث حيضا وبالثاني طلاقاً، أو لم ينو بالثاني شيئاً صارت شيء بما بعدها، ويصدق في نية الحيض لظهور الأم باعتداد الحيض عقب الطلاق . وإذا لم ينو الطلاق بشيء صح، وكذا كل ما قبل المنوي بها ونية الحيض بواحدة غير مسبوقة بواحدة منوي بها الطلاق يقع بها الطلاق ويثبت بها حال المذاكرة فيجري فيها الحكم المذكور بخلاف ما إذا كانت مسبوقة بواحدة أريد بها الطلاق حيث لا يقع بها الثانية لصحة الاعتداد بعد الطلاق، ولا يخفى تخريج المسائل بعد هذا. وأشار بقوله «بما بقي حيضاً إلى أن الخطاب مع من هي من ذوات الحيض، فلو كانت آيسة أو صغيرة فقال أردت بالأولى طلاقاً وبالباقي تربصاً بالأشهر كان الحكم كذلك. وأطلق في كونه يصدق فأفاد أنه يصدق قضاء وديانة وفيما لا يصدق فيه إنما لا يصدق قضاء، وأما ديانة فلا يقع إلا بالنية وقدمنا أن المرأة كالقاضي. وفي الهداية: وفي كل موضع يصدق الزوج على نفي النية إنما يصدق مع اليمين لأنه أمين في الإخبار عما في ضميره والقول قول الأمين مع اليمين ا هـ. وسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى في الاستحلاف أن القول له مع اليمين إلا في عشر مسائل لا يمين على الأمين وهي في القنية . وأشار إلى أنه لو قال نويت بالكل واحدة كان ناوياً بكل لفظ ثلث تطليقة وهو مما لا يتجزى فيتكامل فتقع الثلاث كما في المحيط.
And if he said to her 'Observe three [waiting periods]' and he intended with the first [word] divorce and with the remaining menstrual periods, he is believed; and if he did not intend with the remaining anything, then it is three [divorces]. Because with his intention of menstrual periods for the rest he intended the reality of his speech, and with his intention of divorce for the first the situation became a state of discussing divorce, so the remaining two are determined for divorce by this indication, thus he is not believed in denying intention judicially. And by this it is known that discussing divorce is not limited to asking for divorce, but is more general, including preceding initiation. And included under the first issue is when he intended with each of them menstrual periods, so one divorce occurs, which is the first; and when he intended with the third divorce only; and when he intended with the third menstrual periods only; and when he intended with the second divorce and with the third menstrual periods only; and when he intended with the second and third menstrual periods; in these six only one occurs. And included under the second issue is when he intended with the first menstrual periods only; or with the first two divorce only; or with the first and third divorce only; or with the second and third divorce and with the first menstrual periods; or each of his statement: 'the meanings of people's speech'—he said in Fath al-Qadir: it is as if he intends that people's purpose with the like is 'take the four paths,' otherwise the wording only gives the command to take one of them, and the most likely is that one ba'in divorce occurs. The words 'divorce'—these six result in three. And from these two issues together with what is attached to them come twelve issues: first: that he does not intend with each anything, so nothing occurs; and the remaining, which is eleven, result in two divorces: and that is when he intends with the second divorce only; or with the first divorce and with the second menstrual periods only; or with the first divorce and with the third menstrual periods only; or with the last two divorce only; or with the first two menstrual periods only; or with the first and third menstrual periods only; or with the first and second divorce and with the third menstrual periods; or with the first and third divorce and with the second menstrual periods; or with the first and second menstrual periods and with the third divorce; or with the first and third menstrual periods and the second divorce; or with the third menstrual periods only; so this issue becomes subject to twenty-four possibilities. And the method of its regulation is that it is either that he intends with all menstrual periods, or with all divorce, or does not intend with all anything, or with the first menstrual periods and with the remaining two divorce, or with the first menstrual periods only, or with the first menstrual periods and with the second divorce only, or with the first menstrual periods and with the third divorce only. So if he intends menstrual periods with the first only, there are four forms; if he intends with the second menstrual periods only, there are another four; if he intends with the third only, there are another four, making twelve. Or he intends with the first and second menstrual periods and with the third divorce, or does not intend with the third anything; or he intends with the second and third menstrual periods and with the first divorce, or does not intend with the first anything—making sixteen; or he intends with the first and third menstrual periods and with the second divorce, or does not intend with the second anything—making eighteen; or he intends with the first divorce only, or with the second divorce only, or with the third divorce only—making twenty-one with the first three. The principle is that if he intended divorce with one [word], the state of discussing divorce is established, so he is not believed concerning nothing with what comes after it, and he is believed in the intention of menstrual periods because of the apparent matter of counting menstrual periods after divorce. And if he did not intend divorce with anything, it is valid; and likewise everything before the intended one; and the intention of menstrual periods with one [word] not preceded by a word intended for divorce results in divorce with it and establishes the state of discussing, so the mentioned ruling applies to it, unlike when it is preceded by a word intended for divorce, where the second does not occur because of the validity of waiting after divorce. And the derivation of the issues after this is not hidden. And he indicated by his statement 'with the remaining menstrual periods' that the address is to one who has menstrual periods; so if she was menopausal or a minor and he said 'I intended with the first divorce and with the rest waiting by months,' the ruling is similar. And he made it absolute that he is believed, indicating that he is believed judicially and religiously; and where he is not believed, it is only judicially; as for religiously, it only occurs with intention; and we have stated that the woman is like the judge. And in al-Hidayah: 'And in every place where the husband is believed in denying intention, he is only believed with an oath, because he is trustworthy in informing about what is in his conscience, and the statement of the trustworthy person is accepted with an oath.' And it will come, Allah willing, in the chapter on oath-taking, that his statement is acceptable with an oath except in ten issues where there is no oath on the trustworthy; they are in al-Qunyah. And he indicated that if he said 'I intended with all one [divorce],' he intends with each word a third of a divorce, and it is indivisible, so it is completed and three occur, as in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the ruling of a husband saying to his wife 'I'taddi thalathan' (observe three waiting periods). The core ruling is that if the husband intends divorce with the first occurrence of the word and menstrual periods with the remaining two, he is believed in his intention. If he does not intend anything with the remaining two, then all three words are considered divorces, resulting in three divorces. The reasoning is that his intention of menstrual periods for the rest aligns with the literal meaning of the words (i'tidad is waiting), but once he intends divorce with the first, the entire context becomes a 'discussion of divorce' (muthakarah al-talaq). This presumption means that the remaining two words are also presumed to be intended for divorce, unless he can prove a different intention (which is accepted only as a monthly period intention because that is a natural consequence after divorce). The passage then methodically enumerates all possible combinations of intention for the three words, arriving at 24 scenarios. The key principle is that if the husband intends divorce with any one word, the state of discussing divorce is established, and he cannot deny intent for the following words judicially. However, if he intends menstrual periods for a word not preceded by an intended divorce, that word also effects a divorce. The text also addresses cases where the wife is menopausal or a minor (where waiting is by months) and confirms the same ruling. It further clarifies that the husband is believed both judicially (in court) and religiously (before Allah) regarding his inner intention, but judicial belief requires an oath. Finally, if he says he intended all as one divorce, that is invalid because each word is a separate utterance and indivisible, so three divorces occur.

Key Terms
اعتدى ثلاثاً (i'taddi thalathan): A command to observe three menstrual waiting periods; used as a kinayah for divorce.
مذاكرة الطلاق (muthakarat al-talaq): A state where the discussion of divorce is established, creating a presumption that subsequent words relate to divorce.
قضاء وديانة (qada'an wa diyanatan): Refers to the distinction between how a matter is ruled in court (qada) and what is true before Allah (diyanah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One divorce
B) Two divorces
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'I'taddi thalathan' can yield 1, 2, 3, or 0 divorces depending on the husband's intention and the legal presumption of muthakarah al-talaq, which restricts his judicial credibility after a divorce intention.

Repeating the Kinayah 'I'taddi' Without 'Talaq'
Explains the ruling and proof for kinayah 'i'taddi' with varying conjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the number of divorces triggered by the kinayah 'i'taddi' based on its conjunction in the husband's speech and state the Hanafi position with its proof.

Core Idea
The conjunction of 'i'taddi' determines one continuous command or independent ones, affecting the number of divorces.

The word 'i'taddi' (observe the waiting period) is a kinayah for divorce in Hanafi law. This passage explores what happens when a husband repeats this word without explicitly saying 'talaq'. The presence or absence of a conjunction like 'fa' changes the ruling. Understanding this nuance is essential for correctly applying divorce law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrasings
Note the three variations: 'ant tāliq fa i'taddi', 'ant tāliq wa i'taddi', and standalone 'i'taddi' without prior talaq.
The presence of 'fa' versus 'wa' or no conjunction changes the ruling.
2
State the ruling for each
With 'fa' (so): one divorce. With 'wa' or alone: two divorces if no intention. If intention, follow it.
The Hanafi school relies on the literal meaning of conjunction.
3
Locate the proof
The proof is linguistic: 'fa' indicates connection, making the command continuous; its absence makes it independent.
The context of divorce discussion gives the kinayah its legal effect.
4
Note Zufar's opposition
Zufar argues that only one divorce occurs regardless because the kinayah is not explicit enough for multiples.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is preferred in fatwa, supported by contextual indication and literal interpretation.
Al-Marghinani and later scholars adopt this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تطليقة تعتد بها ثلاث حيض يصدق لأنه محتمل والظاهر لا يكذبه، وقد منع المحقق في فتح القدير كون ابتداء الإيقاع يثبت دلالة الحال بأن الإيقاع مرة لا يوجب ظهور الإيقاع مرة ثانية وثالثة فلا يكون اللفظ الصالح له ظاهراً في الإيقاع بخلاف سؤال الطلاق لأن ذكر الكناية الصالحة للإيقاع دو الرد عقب سؤال الطلاق ظاهر في قصد الإيقاع به وهو ترجيح لقول زفر المنقول في المحيط. وقيد بكونه كرر اعتدي من غير لفظ طلاق معه لأنه لو قال أنت طالق واعتدي أو أنت طالق اعتدي أو أنت طالق فاعتدي، فإن نوى واحدة فواحدة لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه، وإن نوى ثنتين فثنتان لأنه يحتمله، وإن لم يكن له نية إن قال أنت طالق فاعتدي تقع واحدة لأن الفاء للوصل وإن قال اعتدي أو واعتدي تقع ثنتان لأنه لم يذكره موصولاً بالأول فيكون أمراً مستأنفاً وكلاماً مبتدأ وهو في حال مذاكرة الطلاق فيحمل على الطلاق، وعند زفر تقع واحدة لما عرف ا كذا في المحيط.
One divorce with which she observes three menstrual periods, he is believed because it is possible and the apparent does not belie him. And the muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir prevented that the beginning of the occurrence is established by the indication of the situation because occurrence once does not necessitate the appearance of occurrence a second and third time, so the word suitable for occurrence is not apparent in occurrence, unlike the request for divorce because mentioning the kinayah suitable for occurrence without refusal after the request for divorce is apparent in intending occurrence with it, and it is a preponderance of Zufar's statement transmitted in al-Muhit. And it is restricted by his repeating 'i'taddi' without the word 'talaq' with it, because if he said 'you are divorced and observe the waiting period' or 'you are divorced observe the waiting period' or 'you are divorced so observe the waiting period', then if he intended one, it is one because he intended the reality of his speech, and if he intended two, it is two because it is possible, and if he had no intention: if he said 'you are divorced so observe the waiting period' one occurs because the fa' is for conjunction, and if he said 'observe the waiting period' or 'and observe the waiting period' two occur because he did not mention it connected to the first, so it is an independent command and a new speech, and he is in a context of discussing divorce, so it is interpreted as divorce. According to Zufar, one occurs because of what is known. End. Like this in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the kinayah 'i'taddi' in divorce. The author first addresses a scenario where a woman says 'one divorce with which she observes three menstrual periods' and the husband affirms; he is believed because his statement is plausible. The main issue is when a husband repeats the word 'i'taddi' without explicitly saying 'talaq'. The text distinguishes between different phrasings: if he says 'you are divorced so observe the waiting period' (with fa'), only one divorce falls because the fa' connects the two statements. If he says simply 'observe the waiting period' or 'and observe the waiting period' without connection, two divorces occur because it is an independent command made in a context discussing divorce. This is the Hanafi position. Zufar holds that only one divorce occurs regardless. The reasoning is that the kinayah is not explicit enough for multiple occurrences, but the Hanafi view relies on the context and the lack of conjunction to indicate a separate intent. The text also notes that if the husband intends a specific number, that intention is honored.

Key Terms
اعتدي (i'taddi): Imperative of 'iddah, meaning 'observe the waiting period'. Used as a kinayah for divorce.
كناية (kinayah): An allusive word that can imply divorce but requires intention or context to be effective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'ant tāliq fa i'taddi' without any intention, how many divorces occur according to the Hanafi school?
A) One divorce
B) Two divorces
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
The conjunction 'fa' makes the command continuous, resulting in one divorce; without it, each 'i'taddi' is an independent command triggering a separate divorce in a divorce context.

Saying 'Anti 'Alayya Haram' to Multiple Wives: Intention vs. Strictest Interpretation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on interpreting kinayah 'haram' for multiple wives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding one kinayah utterance aimed at multiple wives with different intentions, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
One word cannot carry two meanings; the strictest interpretation applies to all.

This lesson examines a nuanced issue within Hanafi fiqh: when a husband uses the kinayah 'anti 'alayya haram' for multiple wives but intends different legal effects (e.g., divorce for one, ila' for another). Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn diverge on whether specific intentions are respected or the strictest meaning prevails.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario and intentions
Consider a husband who says 'antuma 'alayya haram' (you two are forbidden to me) to two wives, intending divorce for one and ila' (oath of abstinence) for the other. What legal effect occurs?
The key is that a single utterance is directed at multiple wives.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's principle
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that one word cannot simultaneously signify two different legal meanings (e.g., divorce and ila'). Therefore, it must be interpreted according to the strictest possible meaning, which is divorce.
This applies even if the intentions are of different severity (triple vs. single divorce).
3
Contrast with Abu Hanifa's view
Abu Hanifa holds that each wife is divorced according to the specific intention he had for her. He argues that the word 'haram' is real (haqiqah) for triple divorce and metaphorical for single, allowing different intentions to be recognized.
Muhammad's view aligns with Abu Hanifa on this point due to the word's dual nature.
4
Analyze the underlying rationale
The Sahibayn's reasoning is based on the impossibility of a single utterance encompassing two distinct legal categories. Abu Hanifa's reasoning focuses on the linguistic ambiguity of 'haram' and the weight of intention.
This is a talil (reasoning) based dispute.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The text states 'wal fatwa 'ala qawlihima' (the fatwa is on their view), meaning the operative ruling follows Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: the strictest interpretation applies to all wives.
This is the position relied upon for issuing fatwas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية : جعل هذا التفصيل رواية عن أبي يوسف وذكر قبله أنه إذا لم ينو شيئاً وقعت ثنتان في الوجوه الثلاثة . وفيه من باب ما يحرم امرأته على نفسه وعن أبي يوسف ومحمد فيمن قال لامرأتين أنتما علي حرام ينوي الطلاق في إحداهما والإيلاء في الأخرى فهما طالقان لأن اللفظ الواحد لا ينتظم المعنيين المختلفين فيحمل على الأغلظ منهما وهو الطلاق وعن أبي يوسف أنه إذا نوى في إحداهما ثلاثاً وفي الأخرى واحدة فهما طالقان ثلاثاً لأن الحرمة نوعان : غليظة وخفيفة . واللفظ الواحد لا ينتظم النوعين فحمل على الأغلظ . وفي قول أبي حنيفة . هو كما نوى . ويجب أن يكون هذا قول محمد أيضاً بناء على أن هذا اللفظ للثلاث حقيقة وللواحدة كالمجاز لأن الثلاث يثبت الحرمة مطلقاً فصار مثل لفظة النذر إذا نوى النذر. واليمين خلافاً لأبي يوسف كذا هذا والفتوى على قولهما ولو قال نويت الطلاق لإحداهما واليمين للأخرى عند أبي يوسف يقع عليهما الطلاق وعلى قياس قولهما هو كما نوى. ولو قال لثلاث نسوة أنتن علي حرام ونوى لإحداهن طلاقاً وللأخرى يميناً وللثالثة الكذب طلقن جميعاً عند أبي يوسف، وعندهما هو كما نوى. ولو قال لامرأته أنت علي حرام قاله ونوى بالأولى الطلاق وبالثانية اليمين فهو كما نوى في قولهم جميعاً لأن اللفظ متعدد ا هـ .
And in al-Khaniyyah: He made this detail a narration from Abu Yusuf and mentioned before it that if he did not intend anything, two divorces occur in all three ways. And in it, from the chapter of what makes his wife forbidden to himself: from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, regarding someone who said to two wives 'the two of you are forbidden to me' intending divorce for one and ila' for the other, they are both divorced because one word cannot encompass two different meanings, so it is interpreted as the stricter of the two, which is divorce. And from Abu Yusuf: if he intended three for one and one for the other, they are both divorced by three because prohibition is of two types: severe and light, and one word cannot encompass both types, so it is interpreted as the stricter. In the view of Abu Hanifa: it is as he intended. And this must also be the view of Muhammad based on the fact that this word is real for three and metaphorical for one, because three establishes prohibition absolutely, so it becomes like the word 'nadhr' if he intended nadhr and yamin, contrary to Abu Yusuf. Thus this, and the fatwa is on their view (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And if he said 'I intended divorce for one and yamin for the other', according to Abu Yusuf, divorce falls on both, and by analogy of their view, it is as he intended. And if he said to three women 'you are forbidden to me' and intended divorce for one, yamin for another, and lying for the third, all are divorced according to Abu Yusuf, while according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is as he intended. And if he said to his wife 'you are forbidden to me' and intended divorce the first time and yamin the second time, it is as he intended in all their views because the word is multiple. End.
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage discusses a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the kinayah 'anti 'alayya haram' when used for multiple wives. The issue is whether one utterance can carry different intentions for different women. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that if a single phrase is directed at multiple wives, the strictest interpretation (divorce) applies to all, because one word cannot simultaneously signify two different legal effects. For example, if the husband intends divorce for one and ila' (an oath of abstinence) for another, both are divorced. Similarly, if he intends triple divorce for one and single for another, all are triple. Abu Hanifa disagrees, holding that each is as he intended, because the word 'haram' can be ambiguous and the intention should be followed. The text also notes that Muhammad's view aligns with Abu Hanifa based on the word being real for triple divorce and metaphorical for single. However, the fatwa (operative ruling) is according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the stricter view). The passage also covers scenarios with more than two women and cases where the husband explicitly states his intention after the utterance.

Key Terms
حرام (haram): In this context, a kinayah for divorce or an oath (yamin).
إيلاء (ila'): An oath to abstain from marital relations, a form of divorce-related oath.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath, in this context referring to an oath of abstinence or other oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Both are divorced
B) Only the one intended for divorce is divorced
C) Both receive ila'
D) Neither is affected

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fatwa, one kinayah word 'haram' directed at multiple wives results in the strictest intended divorce for all, regardless of individual intentions.

Kinayah 'Lasta Li bi-Imra'ah': Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Denial of Marriage
Ruling on the kinayah 'lasta li bi-imra'ah'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute regarding the phrase 'lasta li bi-imra'ah' as a kinayah for divorce and state the operative ruling according to each imam.

Core Idea
A phrase that can mean either divorce or denial becomes divorce only by intention — according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to the Sahibayn.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains how phrases like 'you are not my wife' can be interpreted as a kinayah for divorce. The Hanafi imams differ on whether such a statement, which is literally a denial, can be effective as a divorce when the husband intends it. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for applying the rules of kinayah in talaq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its legal context
The phrase 'lasta li bi-imra'ah' is a denial of marriage. The husband says it to his wife. Determine whether it can be a valid divorce.
The phrase is a kinayah because it is not explicit for divorce, but can be interpreted as such with intention.
2
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa says divorce occurs if the husband intends divorce; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say no divorce even if he intends it.
The disagreement stems from whether the phrase can be used to initiate divorce or is purely a denial.
3
Analyze the dalil for each position
Abu Hanifa: the phrase is suitable for both initiating divorce and denying marriage, so intention specifies the former. The Sahibayn: the statement is a lie (since the marriage exists), so it cannot be a valid divorce.
Abu Hanifa's reasoning is based on the potential of the words; the Sahibayn focus on factual accuracy.
4
Differentiate phrases that are pure denial vs kinayah
The text lists phrases like 'I did not marry you' or 'there is no marriage between us' which are mere negation and never produce divorce, even with intention. They are not kinayah.
These phrases lack the dual potential required for kinayah.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Given a husband says 'I am not your husband' intending divorce, determine the outcome under each view.
Under Abu Hanifa: divorce valid; under Sahibayn: no divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وتطلق بلست لي بامرأة أو لست لك أن نوى طلاقاً) بزوج يعني وكان النكاح ظاهراً. وهذا عند أبي حنيفة لأنها تصلح لإنشاء الطلاق كما تصلح لإنكاره فيتعين الأول بالنية. وقالا : لا تطلق وإن نوى لكذبه . ودخل في كلامه ما أنت لي بامرأة وما أنا لك ولا نكاح بيني وبينك، وقوله صدقت في جواب قولها لست لي بزوج كما في المحيط . وخرج عنه لم أتزوجك، أو لم يكن بيننا نكاح ووالله ما أنت لي بامرأة، وقوله «لا عند سؤاله بقوله ألك امرأة، وقوله لا حاجة لي فيك كما في البدائع، ففي هذه الألفاظ لا يقع، وإن نوى عند الكل. ولكن في المحيط ذكر في الوقوع قوله ( لا ) عند سؤاله قال: ولو قال لا يصح عندهما مرتين بزوج
His saying: 'And she is divorced by his saying 'you are not my wife' or 'I am not your husband' if he intended divorce' with a husband meaning the marriage was apparent. This is according to Abu Hanifa because it is suitable for initiating divorce just as it is suitable for denying it, so the former is specified by intention. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: she is not divorced even if he intended it because it is a lie. And included in his speech is 'you are not my wife' and 'I am not yours' and 'there is no marriage between me and you', and his saying 'you are truthful' in response to her saying 'you are not my husband' as in al-Muhit. And excluded from it is 'I did not marry you' or 'there was no marriage between us' and 'by Allah you are not my wife', and his saying 'no' when asked 'do you have a wife?' and his saying 'I have no need of you' as in al-Bada'i'. So in these phrases, no divorce occurs even if he intended according to all. But in al-Muhit, it is mentioned regarding occurrence: his saying 'no' when asked, he said: if he said 'no' it would not be valid according to them twice with a husband?
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage deals with phrases that deny the existence of a marriage, such as 'you are not my wife' or 'I am not your husband.' The Hanafi imams differ: Abu Hanifa holds that if the husband intends divorce by such a statement, it is effective as a kinayah, because the words can be used for either initiating divorce or denying marriage. The intention determines which is meant. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, however, hold that no divorce occurs even with intention, because the statement is a lie (since the marriage exists) and cannot be a valid divorce. The text then lists phrases that are pure denials and do not count as kinayah at all, such as 'I did not marry you' or 'there is no marriage between us.' These are mere negation and not suitable for divorce, so no divorce occurs even if intended. The passage ends with a note from al-Muhit about saying 'no' when asked if he has a wife, but the text is unclear.

Key Terms
لست لي بامرأة (lasta li bi-imra'ah): A phrase denying that she is his wife, used as a kinayah for divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a husband says to his wife 'you are not my wife' intending divorce, what is the ruling?
A) Divorce occurs because the phrase can be a kinayah
B) No divorce occurs because it is a lie
C) Divorce occurs only if the wife agrees
D) No divorce occurs under any circumstances

Key Takeaway
For a phrase like 'lasta li bi-imra'ah' to be a divorce under Abu Hanifa, intention is decisive; the Sahibayn reject it outright.

10 min
Explicit Divorce After Ba'in: The Hanafi Position
Ruling with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that an explicit divorce (sarih) can follow a ba'in divorce during the iddah, identify the hadith proof, and explain the qualification regarding suspended divorces.

Core Idea
An explicit divorce follows a ba'in divorce during iddah — the marriage tie is not fully severed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles a nuanced question: can a husband utter an explicit divorce (sarih) after having already issued a ba'in divorce? While a ba'in divorce ends the marriage immediately, the Hanafi school holds that some legal effects persist during the iddah, allowing further explicit divorces to take effect. The passage relies on a hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri and discusses exceptions for suspended divorces.

Guided Path
1
State the main ruling
Identify the general rule that explicit divorce follows both explicit and ba'in divorces, except when the ba'in is suspended.
Focus on the initial statement 'wa al-sarih yulhiq al-sarih wa al-ba'in'.
2
Identify the dalil
Locate the hadith of al-Khudri that serves as the primary proof.
The hadith is: 'al-mukhtali'a yulhaqaha sarih al-talaq ma damat fi al-iddah'.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the hadith proves the ruling: it explicitly states that explicit divorce can follow a khul' divorce (which is ba'in) during iddah.
This indicates that the iddah period preserves some marital effects.
4
Note the exception for suspended divorces
Recognize that if the ba'in divorce was suspended (mu'allaq) on a condition, a subsequent explicit divorce does not take effect.
The text contrasts the immediate case with the suspended case.
5
Consider the ikhtilaf with Abu Yusuf
Be aware that a narration from Abu Yusuf holds that explicit divorce does not follow ba'in because kinayah requires intention, but the majority Hanafi view is otherwise.
The passage sides with the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya).

والصريح يلحق الصريح والبائن يلحق الصريح لا البائن إلا إذا كان معلقاً بأن نكاح بيننا يقع الطلاق والأصل أن نفي النكاح أصلاً لا يكون طلاقاً بل يكون جحوداً، أنه ونفي النكاح في الحال يكون طلاقاً إذا نوى، وما عداه فالصحيح على هذا الخلاف . قيد بالنية لأنه لا يقع بدون النية اتفاقاً لكونه من الكنايات ولا يخفى أن دلالة الحال تقوم مقامها حيث لم يصلح للرد والشتم ويصلح للجواب فقط . وقدمنا أن الصالح للجواب فقط ثلاثة ألفاظ ليس هذا منها فلذا شرط النية للإشارة إلى أن دلالة الحال هنا لا تكفي. وأشار بقوله «تطلق» إلى أن الواقع بهذه الكناية رجعي. وقيدنا بظهور النكاح لأنه لو قال ما أنت لي بزوجة وأنت طالق لا يكون إقراراً بالنكاح لقيام القرينة المتقدمة على أنه ما أراد بالطلاق حقيقته كما في البزازية أول كتاب النكاح فالنفي لا يقع به بالأولى قوله : (والصريح يلحق الصريح والبائن) فلو قال لها أنت طالق ثم قال أنت طالق أو طلقها على مال وقع الثاني، وكذا لو قال لها أنت بائن أو خالعها على مال ثم قال لها أنت طالق أو هذه طالق كما في البزازية : يقع عندنا لحديث الخدري مسنداً المختلعة يلحقها صريح الطلاق ما دامت في العدة، ولما ذكر في الأصول من بحث الخاص. أطلقه فشمل المنجز والمعلق إذا وجد شرطه، فكما يقع في العدة منجزاً يقع إذا وجد شرطه فيها. وأما إذا علقه في العدة فإنه يصح في جميع الصور إلا إذا كان الطلاق بائناً . ، ثم علق البائن في العدة فإنه غير صحيح اعتباراً بتنجيزه كما في البدائع . قيدنا الصريح اللاحق للبائن بكونه خاطبها به، أو أشار إليها للاحتراز عما إذا قال كل امرأة له طالق فإنه لا يقع على المختلعة وكذا إذا قال إن فعلت كذا فامرأته كذا لا يقع على المعتدة من بائن كما في البزازية والمراد على المختلعة وكذا إذا قال إن فعلت كذا فامرأته كذا لا يقع على المعتدة من بائن كما في البزازية والمراد بالصريح هنا ما وقع به الرجعي فتدخل الكنايات الرواجع من اعتدي واستبري رحمك وأنت واحدة وما ألحق بالثلاثة، فلو أبانها أو العدة قال لها في اعتدي ناوياً وقع الثاني في ظاهر الرواية خلافاً لما روي عن أبي يوسف نظراً إلى أنها كناية وجه ظاهر الرواية أن الواقع بها رجعي فكان في معنى الصريح خالعها قوله: (وقيدنا بظهور النكاح اعترضه في النهر بأن قول المصنف وتطلق» مستغن عن التقييد به ما في البزازية لو قالت أنا امرأتك فقال لها أنت طالق كان إقراراً بالنكاح وتطلق لاقتضاء الطلاق النكاح وضعاً قوله : : (فإنه لا يقع على (المختلعة أي إلا أن يعنيها فإن عناها طلقت. كذا في كافي الحاكم الشهيد من باب الخلع ا هـ . والظاهر أن عدم الوقوع لكونها ليست امرأة له من كل وجه بل من بعض الأوجه ولذا يقع عليها بالنية بخلاف ما إذا لم ينو لكونها كالأجنبية ولذا قال في حاوي الزاهدي : قال لامرأته أنت طالق واحدة ثم قال إن كنت امرأة لي فأنت طالق ثلاثاً إن كان الطلاق الأول بائناً لا يقع الثاني، وإن كان رجعياً يقع الثاني قوله : محمول على رواية أبي يوسف) أقول : صرح بذلك في كافي الحاكم بعد ذكره ما هو ظاهر الرواية حيث قال : وكذلك لو قال لها بعد الخلع
And the explicit follows the explicit, and the ba'in follows the explicit, not the ba'in except if it is suspended... and the principle is that negation of marriage absolutely is not divorce but is denial... and negation of marriage at the moment is divorce if intended... and what other than that, the correct is on this difference... restricted by intention because it does not occur without intention by agreement since it is of the kinayat... and it is not hidden that the indication of the situation takes its place where it is not suitable for rejection and insult and is suitable only for answer... and we previously said that the suitable for answer only are three words, this is not among them, so intention is required to indicate that the indication of the situation is not sufficient here... and he indicated by his saying 'she is divorced' that what occurs by this kinayah is raj'i... and we restricted by the appearance of marriage because if he said 'you are not my wife and you are divorced' it is not an acknowledgment of marriage due to the preceding indication that he did not intend the reality of divorce as in al-Bazzaziyya at the beginning of the book of marriage, so the negation does not occur by it, a fortiori. His saying: 'And the explicit follows the explicit and the ba'in' so if he said to her 'you are divorced' then said 'you are divorced' or divorced her for compensation, the second occurs, and likewise if he said to her 'you are ba'in' or divorced her by khul' for compensation then said to her 'you are divorced' or 'this one is divorced' as in al-Bazzaziyya: it occurs according to us due to the hadith of al-Khudri narrated: 'the mukhtali'a is followed by explicit divorce as long as she is in the iddah' and due to what is mentioned in the principles from the discussion of the specific. He made it absolute so it includes the immediate and the suspended if its condition is found, so just as it occurs in the iddah by immediate occurrence, it occurs if its condition is found therein. As for if he suspends it in the iddah, it is valid in all cases except if the divorce is ba'in. Then if he suspends ba'in in the iddah, it is not valid considering its immediate form as in al-Bada'i'. We restricted the explicit that follows the ba'in by his addressing her with it or pointing to her to avoid the case where he says 'every woman he has is divorced' for it does not occur on the mukhtali'a, and likewise if he says 'if you do such and such then his wife is such and such' it does not occur on the one observing iddah from a ba'in divorce as in al-Bazzaziyya, and the intent is the mukhtali'a... And the apparent is that non-occurrence is because she is not his wife in every respect but only in some respects, so it occurs upon her with intention unlike if he did not intend because she is like a foreign woman. Therefore it is said in Hawi al-Zahidi: he said to his wife 'you are divorced once' then said 'if you are my wife then you are divorced three times'; if the first divorce was ba'in, the second does not occur; if it was raj'i, the second occurs. His saying is carried on the narration of Abu Yusuf. I say: it is explicitly stated in Kafi al-Hakim after mentioning what is the apparent narration where he said: and likewise if he said to her after khul'...
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage discusses the rule that an explicit divorce (sarih) can follow and take effect upon a previous divorce, even if the previous was ba'in, as long as the woman is in the iddah. The hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri supports this. However, if the ba'in divorce was suspended (mu'allaq), it cannot be followed by a new explicit divorce during the iddah. The text also distinguishes between raj'i and ba'in divorces: explicit divorce can follow a raj'i divorce always, but following a ba'in divorce is only valid if the explicit is immediate, not suspended. The passage further clarifies that phrases like 'every woman he has is divorced' do not affect the mukhtali'a because she is not fully his wife. Intention matters when using kinayat for divorce after ba'in. The text ends with a discussion of a conditional divorce and references various authoritative works.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): Explicit divorce using clear words like 'talaq'.
بائن (ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately, but the woman observes iddah.
مختلعة (mukhtali'a): A woman who has been divorced by khul' (ransom divorce).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a husband divorces his wife by khul' (ba'in) and then during her iddah says 'you are divorced' explicitly, what happens?
A) The second divorce is valid and reduces the remaining number of divorces
B) The second divorce does not occur because the marriage is already severed
C) The second divorce is valid only if intended
D) The second divorce is considered a revocable divorce

Key Takeaway
Hanafis hold that an explicit divorce can follow a ba'in divorce during iddah, supported by the hadith of al-Khudri and the principle that iddah retains partial marital effects.

Talaq — Divorce

12 min
Explicit Divorce After Bain Divorce: Ruling, Proof, and Dispute
Commentary on the ruling that explicit divorce applies to both revocable and irrevocable divorces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the ruling that explicit divorce words (sarih) take effect after an irrevocable (bain) divorce and EXPLAIN the Hanafi proof from the hadith of al-Khudri, including the intra-madhhab dispute regarding allusions and description.

Core Idea
Explicit divorce is legally effective even after bain, but description can shift the ruling entirely.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq by Ibn Nujaym clarifies a key principle in Hanafi divorce law: after an irrevocable divorce (bain), if the husband pronounces an explicit divorce (sarih) such as 'anti taliq', the second divorce does take effect. The author provides the proof from the hadith of al-Khudri and explores important exceptions, particularly when the pronouncement includes a description like 'bain' that alters the analysis. The discussion also covers internal Hanafi disputes, including Abu Yusuf's differing view on allusions and the effect of intention in ambiguous cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage opens by stating that explicit divorce (sarih) follows both explicit and bain divorces. It then quotes the hadith of al-Khudri as evidence. Your task is to extract the exact phrasing of the ruling from the Arabic text.
The ruling applies during the wife's iddah; after iddah, she is a stranger and no divorce takes effect.
2
Locate and analyse the proof
The proof is the hadith: 'The woman divorced by khul' is followed by explicit divorce as long as she is in the waiting period.' Identify whether this hadith is mutawatir or ahad, and explain how it proves the ruling.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Trace the internal Hanafi dispute
The commentator mentions that according to Abu Yusuf, allusions like 'i'taddi' do not follow bain divorce, while the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) considers them as in the meaning of explicit. Understand the basis of each view.
This dispute affects whether a husband's use of 'i'taddi' after a bain divorce counts as a valid divorce.
4
Examine the effect of description
The text discusses the case where a husband says 'anti taliq ba'in' after a bain divorce intending three. The commentator distinguishes between occurrence by the noun (taliq) and occurrence by the adjective (bain). Learn how this leads to different rulings among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar.
Abu Hanifa rules no divorce, Abu Yusuf says three, Zufar says one.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Identify which position is relied upon for fatwa. The commentator favours Abu Hanifa's view, arguing that the description makes the occurrence by itself, and contextual indication removes it from being truly explicit.
The relied-upon ruling is that 'anti taliq ba'in' with intention of three does not produce a divorce.
